**Hashkafah 101**

   **“Hashkafah”**  
In Tanach, the root *shakaf* (*ש,ק,פ*) means “to look”\[^1] or “to be seen.”\[^2] Recently, people have started using the word “*hashkafah*” to refer to one’s outlook on life.

   As opposed to the term “*machshavah,”* which people often use to refer to philosophical studies (such as metaphysics), *hashkafah* refers to how we understand our world's workings and how we are meant to live within it.

   *Hashkafah* has become an area of significant interest. As opposed to the Rishonim, whose philosophical discussions focused mainly on issues of *machshavah*, recent generations have concentrated more on understanding the nature and goals of our existence.

   **The Central Agreed Tenets**  
Often, people use “*hashkafah*” to refer to what distinguishes *between* different *hashkafos* (Litvish, Chassidish, Modern Orthodox, Religious Zionist, etc.). People are eager to understand and appreciate their unique approach.

   That said, most *hashkafic* issues, including most central ones, are things all Torah Jews agree upon.

   **Hashkafah 101**  
Though how we view our lives is an integral part of our identity, these issues are generally not studied in an organized way. Though they are addressed often and at times of meaningful reflection (*sichos*, *divrei Torah* over Shabbos, *tisches*, etc.), I am not familiar with any comprehensive curriculum or *sefer* that presents the full range of these topics with the relevant *mekoros*.

   This is where the idea for Hashkafah 101 comes from. Hashkafah 101 identifies and addresses Judaism’s hashkafic topics in a comprehensive, systematic, organized, source-based manner.

   **Maseches Avos… and Beyond**  
The pieces in this volume discuss the *hashkafic* topics presented by Maseches Avos. Though Chazal addressed *hashkafic* issues in many places, Maseches Avos (and the related Avos D'Rebbe Nosson) is the central Tannaitic repository of *hashkafic* material.

   The pieces are arranged by topic and theme and flow from one to the next. Though the *sefer* includes a key that lists the pieces in order of the *mishnayos* they address, the *sefer* organizes the chapters in topical order, and I recommend learning them that way.

   I have already begun developing a broader series of pieces that includes topics not addressed by Maseches Avos. May this first offering be received well.

   Reuven Taragin  
Av, 5783  
Yerushalayim Ir HaKodesh

   **Biglal Avot — Parent Principles**

   משֶׁה קִבֵּל תּוֹרָה מִסִּינַי, וּמְסָרָהּ לִיהוֹשֻׁעַ, וִיהוֹשֻׁעַ לִזְקֵנִים, וּזְקֵנִים לִנְבִיאִים, וּנְבִיאִים מְסָרוּהָ לְאַנְשֵׁי כְנֶסֶת הַגְּדוֹלָה (אבות א:א).

   **Avos as Ancestors**  
Maseches Avos’s name seems rooted in the list of the *ba’alei mesorah* — our ancestors (*avos*) who transmitted the Torah Sheba’al Peh from Sinai — with which the *maseches* opens.

   Why does this list appear here — at the end of the *third seder* of the *mishnayos*? Why not at the beginning of *Shas*, as the opening to Maseches Berachos?

   The Meiri\[^3] explains that the list appears in Maseches Avos to emphasize the importance of its topics. Since Avos focuses (mainly) on issues that are neither *halachic* nor derived from *pesukim*, one might view the content as less important. As the *maseches* contains neither *mitzvos* nor *aveiros*, people might not be diligent about observing its directives. To stress the importance of its content, *Maseches Avos* begins by linking itself to (those who transmitted the Torah to us from) Sinai.

   **Avos as Principles**  
After translating *avos* as ancestors, the Meiri presents a second explanation. He equates the term's meaning here to its meaning in the context of *Melachos* (modes of work) *Shabbos* and *Nezikin* (damages). Just as *avos melachah* and *nezikin* are the categories from which other forms of *melachah* and *nezikin* originate (or are encapsulated within), the teachings of Maseches Avos are the root principles for Jewish philosophy, *hashkafah* (outlook), and conduct.\[^4] Most of these ideas are rooted in Tanach’s *mitzvos* and history but not explicitly stated there; Avos fills in the details by formulating the principles.

   Maseches Avos’s teachings facilitate living a proper and meaningful life. The *midos* taught by Maseches Avos are the precondition for Torah itself — “*Derech eretz kadma la’Torah*.”\[^5] Pirkei Avos also addresses matters of faith and *hashkafa*. It teaches us how to view the world and our role within it. Living a proper Jewish life hinges on knowing and internalizing Judaism’s outlook on the world and human life and behaving accordingly.

   These two components — *middos* and *hashkafa* — are inextricably linked. We are meant to express our *hashkafa* in our behavior and root our behavior in our *hashkafa*.\[^6] The *gemara*\[^7] teaches that one becomes pious by studying Maseches Avos. The combination of *hashkafic* ideas and behavioral guidance develops piety.

   **Parent Principles**  
The Meiri presents these two explanations of Avos as a continuation of one another. The two are not in conflict. They complement one another. Our “*avos*” principles come from our “*avos*” ancestors. This is not only because it makes sense to learn from earlier generations but also because Judaism is a religion of continuity.

   Judaism is not just about individuals understanding and worshipping G-d. It is about belonging to a people who descend from and still identify with their *avos* and *imahos*. Though all ancient peoples have living descendants, Jews are the only ones who, on principle, name children after their ancient ancestors.\[^8] This is because we emulate them and their relationship with Hashem.

   \[clt]Judaism … is about belonging to a people who descend from and still identify with their *avos* and *imahos*.

   Despite our spiritual decline in *Mitzrayim*, Hashem redeemed us because we continued using our ancestral names.\[^9] Upon heralding redemption, Hashem identified Himself as the G-d of our ancestors,\[^10] and at the *Yam Suf*, the climax of the redemption, we related to Hashem as *“Elokei avi* (my father’s G-d).*”\[^11]*  Understandably, we begin our *Shemoneh Esrei* by describing Hashem as not only our G-d but also as the G-d of our ancestors. This is because we know that our relationship with Him is a continuation of their’s.

   By leaving the areas of *midos* and *hashkafah* (mostly) unstated clearly by the Torah, Hashem created the need to learn these *avos* of faith from our ancestral *avos*. Like the *halachos* of Torah Sheba’al Peh, we learn our principles of faith, *hashkafah*, and *middos* from our biological parents and spiritual *avos*.

   This book is devoted to studying these principles.

   May our learning to live **by** *avos hashkafos* and **up to** *avos* standards reinforce our place as links in our people’s *avos—banim* *mesorah* chain and make us worthy of His redemption.

   *Biglal Avot, toshi’a banim* *—* because of Avot, children are redeemed.

   **Creation: World and Man**

   **Appreciating Our Lives**

   **Appreciating Hashem's World and Ourselves**

   **Appreciating Our Lives**

   Owing What We Own

   Life On Loan

   Earning Our Lives

   **Owing What We Own**

   רַבִּי אֶלְעָזָר אִישׁ בַּרְתּוּתָא אוֹמֵר, תֶּן לוֹ מִשֶּׁלּוֹ, שֶׁאַתָּה וְשֶׁלָּךְ שֶׁלּוֹ.  
וְכֵן בְּדָוִד הוּא אוֹמֵר (דברי הימים א כט) כִּי מִמְּךָ הַכֹּל וּמִיָּדְךָ נָתַנּוּ לך (אבות ג:ז)

   Rebbe Elazar Ish Bartuta encourages us to “give Him (Hashem) what is His, because you and that which is yours are really His.”\[^12] When we donate money,\[^13] time, or effort to Hashem, we see ourselves as gifting Him something of our own. In truth, we merely give Him what is (already) His.\[^14] This is why Hashem formulates the donations to the *Mishkan* as “*v’yikchu Li terumah.*”\[^15] It is Hashem *taking* what is His, not the Jewish People *giving* a donation.

   **Ownership Through Creation**  
Why do our possessions belong to Hashem? The simplest explanation is that Hashem’s ownership is rooted in His creation of the world. The Torah begins with the story of Creation not just because our world began there historically but also because it starts there philosophically. The creation narrative helps us appreciate where everything came from and (thus) who their true owner is.\[^16]

   This is how Rashi opens his commentary on the Torah. He explains that the Torah begins with the creation narrative, rather than the first *mitzvah*, to teach us where our rights to Eretz Yisrael come from. The response to those who call our settlement of Israel thievery is that because Hashem created and thus owns the world, He has the right to give lands to the nations He chooses.\[^17]

   This idea is a critical part of our *hashkafa*. We express it by reciting *berachos,* (all of) which speak of Hashem’s role as creator, before deriving pleasure from His world. Our recognition of Hashem as creator permits us to partake of and enjoy His creations.\[^18]

   We reaffirm this recognition each Yom Rishon (Sunday) when we commemorate creation by reciting the *pesukim* “*LaHashem ha’aretz u’meloah teivel v’yoshvei vah. Ki Hu al yamim yisadah v’al neharos yichonenehah*” as part of that day’s *shir* *shel* *yom* (daily psalm).\[^19] The *pesukim* assert Hashem’s ownership over two distinct components of the world: the frameworks (*aretz* and *teivel*) and the content (*melo’ah* and *yoshvei vah*).\[^20] During the first three days of creation, Hashem created the frameworks; during the last three, he filled them with content and inhabitants.

   **How We Get What We Have**  
Hashem’s ownership is rooted in a second factor as well *—* His role in gifting us what we own. We mistakenly think that we acquire our possessions “on our own.” In truth, Hashem facilitates our acquisition.

   The *medrash*\[^21] reads the word “*honecha*”\[^22] (simply translated as “your wealth”) as “*chininecha*”\[^23] *—*  the gifts with which Hashem graces us. The full *pasuk* (“*Kabed es Hashem mei’honecha*”) thus encourages us to honor Hashem by donating the wealth He blesses us with to Him.

   Similarly, the medrash explains that Hashem “owes” no one for the *mitzvos* they perform. We are able to post *mezuzos* on our doorposts and separate tithes from our crops *only after* Hashem gifts homes, fields, and crops to us.\[^24]

   **We Too**  
As a prooftext for his teaching, Rebbe Elazar Ish Bartuta quotes the words of Dovid HaMelech: “Because it is all from You, and from your hand we give to You.”\[^25] Dovid  expressed his recognition that donations made for the building of the Beis HaMikdash were merely returning what Hashem owns to Him.

   Having learned this lesson from Dovid HaMelech, what does Rebbe Elazar Ish Bartuta add? The answer lies in his usage of the word *“atah.”* What we “own” is owned by Hashem not only because the objects are His, but also because *we*, the “owners,” are also His creations and, thus, also owned by Him.\[^26]

   Our very lives and bodies are not our own. Both belong to Hashem *—* their creator. This is why we are prohibited from damaging our bodies\[^27] and are responsible for safeguarding them.\[^28] Hashem is our creator and, thus, the owner of our bodies and lives.\[^29]

   \[clt]Our very lives and bodies are not our own. Both belong to Hashem *—* their creator.

   Hashem’s ownership of us is also included in the (aforementioned) *pesukim* we recite on Yom Rishon: “*LaHashem ha’aretz u’meloah tevel **v’yoshvei vah.***” It is not only the land and its contents but also its residents that belong to Hashem.

   Hashem’s far-reaching ownership is what we refer to in *Shemoneh Esrei*‘s first *berachah,* which describes Hashem as *“konei **ha’kol*** — the owner of all.”\[^30] We pray to Hashem, grateful for His having created us and the rest of our world.

   **The Goal of It All**  
The Ramban saw our recognition of this fact as the goal of creation, all of the *mitzvos,* and all of our *tefilos*:  
The purpose of all the *mitzvos* is for us to believe in our G-d and admit that He created us/thank him for creating us.  
This is the goal of creation. We have no other reason for the initial creation because G-d above has no desire for a world below besides man knowing and thanking his G-d for creating him.  
The goal of raising our voices in prayer, the goal of shuls, and the merit of the prayers of the masses is for people to have a place where they can gather and thank Hashem, publicize the fact that G-d created them, and say before Him, ‘We are your creations.’”\[^31]

   **Appreciating Our Lives**  
Sadly, we often take our lives for granted and forget that they are “G-d-given.”\[^32] Iyov is an example of someone who made this mistake. Though he maintained his faith even after losing his children and possessions,\[^33] Iyov faltered when he lost his health.\[^34] He recognized his children and property as G-d-given — but saw his health as his own. When Hashem allowed Satan to take his health away, Iyov felt a line had been crossed.

   Yirmiyahu HaNavi countered Iyov when he asked why “a living person should complain?”\[^35] The medrash\[^36] explains that one who appreciates Hashem’s gift of life does not complain about his circumstances. Hashem’s continued sustenance helps him appreciate that Hashem (who gifts life to him) has his best interests in mind.\[^37]

   We express this appreciation each morning with the *Modeh Ani* prayer. Though not yet able to mention Hashem’s name before washing our hands, we immediately thank Him for reviving us. After washing, we recite *Elokai Neshamah* in which we thank Hashem by name and commit ourselves to continue expressing our appreciation of Hashem as long as our soul remains within us: “*kol zeman she’ha’neshamah b’kirbi…*”

   We determine our perspective on life the moment we awake. Many take this moment for granted. Missing the opportunity to remind themselves of their creator allows them to view their existence as random and meaningless.

   To avoid this, we recite *Modeh Ani* and *Elokai Neshamah* and internalize the fact that Hashem has recreated us. We understand that Hashem created us for particular purposes and missions, and we aim to accomplish them each day of the lives He grants us.

   May Rebbe Elazar Ish Bartuta's words remind us of where both we and what we own come from and help us appreciate our existence and purpose.

   In the next chapter, we will iy”H see how this perspective should impact our appreciation of everything we encounter in G-d’s world.

   **Life On Loan**

   הוּא (רבי עקיבא) הָיָה אוֹמֵר, הַכֹּל נָתוּן בָּעֵרָבוֹן, וּמְצוּדָה פְרוּסָה עַל כָּל הַחַיִּים.  
הַחֲנוּת פְּתוּחָה, וְהַחֶנְוָנִי מַקִּיף, וְהַפִּנְקָס פָּתוּחַ, וְהַיָּד כּוֹתֶבֶת, וְכָל הָרוֹצֶה לִלְווֹת יָבוֹא וְיִלְוֶה,  
וְהַגַּבָּאִים מַחֲזִירִים תָּדִיר בְּכָל יוֹם, וְנִפְרָעִין מִן הָאָדָם מִדַּעְתּוֹ וְשֶׁלֹּא מִדַּעְתּוֹ, וְיֵשׁ לָהֶם עַל מַה שֶּׁיִּסְמוֹכוּ, וְהַדִּין דִּין אֱמֶת, וְהַכֹּל מְתֻקָּן לַסְּעוּדָה (אבות ג:ט"ז).

   **Life on Loan**  
In our last chapter, we studied the teaching of Rebbe Elazar Ish Bartuta, who reminds us that we are created (and owned) by Hashem. Rebbe Akiva builds off this assumption and defines the life Hashem grants us (and the opportunities it includes) as a loan: “*Hakol nasun b’eiravon*.”\[^38]

   Rebbe Akiva continues by comparing the world to a store: “The store is open, the storekeeper gives credit, the book is open, the hand records, and whoever wishes to borrow may come and take a loan.” Hashem generously offers us life and everything in His world free for the taking, but they are all given on loan. Like any other loan, the lender (in this case, Hashem) has the right to reclaim the loan.\[^39]

   Hashem aims to gift it all to us, but on the condition that we use His gifts to help us live meaningful lives. If we do not, Hashem reclaims the gifts from us:\[^40] as the *mishnah* continues, “a trap is spread out over all of the living.” This *mishnah* takes this imagery from Sefer Koheles,\[^41], which compares our lives to those of unassuming fish and birds suddenly swooped up in nets or traps. Life may seem stable and secure; in truth, our lives can end or be upended suddenly. We live life on loan in a world we can be removed from without a moment’s notice.

   \[clt]We live life on loan in a world we can be removed from without a moment’s notice.

   **The Collection**  
The mishnah continues: “The collectors constantly exact payment from man, with or without his cognizance; their actions have what to rely upon, and the judgment is truthful.” Even if our misdeeds do not warrant our complete removal from the world, Hashem’s agents exact the “payments” that our missteps necessitate.

   The Rambam explains that Hashem collects through death or *yisurin*. When people use their lives and the world’s resources improperly, the debt is collected in the form of personal suffering. The “agents” who inflict this pain “collect” on Hashem’s behalf.

   Animals who attack man act at Hashem’s behest.\[^42] Even the minor nuisance of pulling the wrong coin out of a pocket is suffering caused by Hashem.\[^43] While we often dismiss suffering as coincidental and unrelated to our actions, Chazal saw it as Hashem’s way of reclaiming the loan we have misused.

   The collection can be “with or without our cognizance” — with or without our understanding, appreciation, and acceptance of our suffering’s significance. The *gemara*\[^44] explains that when a person suffers, he should attribute the suffering to his misdeeds.\[^45] Ideally, suffering should inspire introspection and prompt us to use our lives and opportunities properly.

   **Preparing For the *Seudah***  
The *mishnah* concludes that “everything is prepared for the ultimate *seudah* (banquet)” — the next world.\[^46] Though this world is the only one we see and, thus, naturally holds our focus, we should realize that *Olam Haba* is the ultimate goal. The loan of life in this world aims to help us earn a seat at the table in the next one.\[^47]

   \[clt]The loan of life in this world aims to help us earn a seat at the table in the next one.

   One might have thought that we would not receive special reward for using our lives properly, as they are merely given to us on loan. The end of *Avos’s* second *perek* teaches us that we actually receive much more: “Your employer is faithful to pay you the reward for your labor... in the coming future.”\[^48] When we use the life Hashem loans us correctly, He rewards us for our efforts — not only by not reclaiming the loan but also by gifting us interest.

   **Faithful Reward**  
Hashem’s just reward is a principle of our faith\[^49], and it should motivate us to live correctly, the way Hashem intends. This is why it is part of the first words we say to Hashem when we awaken — “*rabbah **emuna**secha* (you are very *reliable*\*\*)\*\*.” Hashem’s daily return of our soul to us strengthens our belief in Him as a reliable rewarder.

   We build off this the second time our *tefilah* refers to Hashem as reliable: “*v’**ne’eman** asah l’hachayos meisim* (you are *reliable* to revive the dead).” Hashem’s daily restoration of life inspires reliance upon Him to do so again, even after our lives in this world end.

   Maseches Avos teaches us that life in the World to Come is a reward for taking full advantage of Hashem’s first gift — life in *this* world.

   May rewaking each morning give us the faith to properly use Hashem's daily loan, a renewed lease on life.

   **Earning Our Lives**

   רַבִּי חֲנִינָא בֶן חֲכִינַאי אוֹמֵר, הַנֵּעוֹר בַּלַּיְלָה וְהַמְהַלֵּךְ בַּדֶּרֶךְ יְחִידִי וְ(הַ)מְפַנֶּה לִבּוֹ לְבַטָּלָה,  
הֲרֵי זֶה מִתְחַיֵּב בְּנַפְשׁוֹ: (אבות ג:ד)

   רַבִּי שִׁמְעוֹן אוֹמֵר, הַמְהַלֵּךְ בַדֶּרֶךְ וְשׁוֹנֶה וּמַפְסִיק מִמִּשְׁנָתוֹ וְאוֹמֵר, מַה נָּאֶה אִילָן זֶה וּמַה נָּאֶה נִיר זֶה, מַעֲלֶה עָלָיו הַכָּתוּב כְּאִלּוּ מִתְחַיֵּב בְּנַפְשׁוֹ: (אבות ג:ז)

   רַבִּי דּוֹסְתַּאי בְּרַבִּי יַנַּאי מִשּׁוּם רַבִּי מֵאִיר אוֹמֵר, כָּל הַשּׁוֹכֵחַ דָּבָר אֶחָד מִמִּשְׁנָתוֹ, מַעֲלֶה עָלָיו הַכָּתוּב כְּאִלּוּ מִתְחַיֵּב בְּנַפְשׁוֹ, שֶׁנֶּאֱמַר (דברים ד), רַק הִשָּׁמֶר לְךָ וּשְׁמֹר נַפְשְׁךָ מְאֹד פֶּן תִּשְׁכַּח אֶת הַדְּבָרִים אֲשֶׁר רָאוּ עֵינֶיךָ… (אבות ג:ח)

   Perek Gimmel quotes Rebbe Chanina ben Chachinai, who lists three activities that cause a person to be *“mis’chayev b’nafsho* (mortally guilty\*)”\*: staying awake at night, traveling alone, and directing one’s heart towards meaninglessness.\[^50] Why are these activities (particularly the first two) so problematic, and why is the punishment for engaging in them so severe?

   **Natural Danger**  
Many *meforshim (commentaries)\[^51]* explain that the first two activities (staying up at night and traveling alone) are naturally dangerous. Roads outside cities were less structured and safe, especially in ancient times. Similarly, darkness makes nights a hazardous time to be up and about, and staying late rather than sleeping is an unhealthy practice.

   Though this interpretation explains the *mishnah*’s first two cases, it does not explain the third — *mefaneh libo l’vatalah* (one who directs his heart towards meaninglessness). Why is this dangerous?

   Furthermore, the assumption that the *mishnah* deals with naturally dangerous activities raises two additional questions. Why does the mishnah use the term “*mis’chayev b’nafsho*” (which implies culpability) to refer to one who puts himself in *natural* harm's way? Secondly, why does Avos *—* a *maseches* dedicated to ethics and morals — include a *mishnah* focused upon personal safety?

   **Meaninglessness**  
For these reasons, many other *meforshim* offer an alternative explanation. They explain “*mis’chayev b’nafsho”* as referring not to *naturally* dangerous activities but, instead, to inappropriate behavior that incurs divine *punishment*.

   Let us examine the three activities listed by the *mishnah* from this perspective. The problem with the third activity — *mefaneh libo l’vatalah* — is understandable. We are meant to focus our lives and attention on meaningful things. Though we often become distracted, we should never *direct* our attention to meaninglessness.\[^52]

   The Meiri adds that squandering an opportunity to study Torah reflects a lack of appreciation and love for it.\[^53] This is why Avos’s sixth *perek* teaches that a heavenly voice cries over those who waste the opportunity to study Torah\[^54], and why Hillel taught in Avos’s first *perek*\[^55] that one who does not maximize (by adding to) the time he devotes to Torah study has his life (that he wastes) taken from him.\[^56]

   This part of the *mishnah* is particularly relevant to contemporary society. Though people have always been tempted to involve themselves in meaningless pursuits, easy access to social media and entertainment in the palm of our hands has increased the temptation tenfold. Cell phones offer quick communication, helpful tools, and access to meaningful content, but also distractions and potential time wasters. Rebbe Chanina’s words encourage us to resist the temptation and maintain our focus.

   **Using Our Nights**  
Many *meforshim* explain the problem with the *mishnah*’s first two activities in a similar way.

   Being up at night without learning Torah is a misuse of the night. We work during the day and have free time at night\[^57] We should use this time to sleep or to learn Torah.\[^58] One awake at night but not learning wastes the night, created for these two purposes,\[^59] and is, thus, “*mis’chayev b’nafsho*.” He does not deserve the life he was gifted.

   This message is vital for contemporary life. Electricity has turned night into “day” and extended the time available for work and pursuing areas of interest. To fill this time, society has developed many forms of leisure and entertainment. We must remember that we were given a wonderful gift we are meant to take full advantage of — Torah. We do so by studying it whenever we can — especially at night.\[^60]

   It is also important to use our nights to sleep so we are able to fully maximize the coming day. Electricity and technology often cause us to stay up later than we should. Sadly, this impairs our ability to rise on time and function properly the next day. The Shulchan Aruch opens by emphasizing the importance of having the “strength to wake up like a lion.” In today’s world, we (also) need self-control to go *to* *sleep* on time.

   According to this approach, the real issue is not being up at night but, rather, wasting precious time. In essence, the significance of the *mishnah*’s opening phrase —  *nei’or ba’layla* —  is explained by the *mishnah*’s closing phrase — *mefaneh libo l’vatalah.\[^61]* One is *mis’chayev b’nafsho,* not for being up late but, rather, for focusing on meaninglessness during that time.

   **Travel Opportunities**  
Many *meforshim* similarly explain the problem with the *mishnah*’s second activity. Someone traveling alone has no one to speak with and can, thus, focus on Torah learning.\[^62] Travel, like nights, is a time when we are not working and can focus our minds on Torah. One who chooses meaninglessness instead is “*mischayeiv b’nafsho*” because of this waste of time and opportunity.

   Rebbe Chanina’s message about travel thus connects to Rebbe Shimon’s similar statement and formulation later in the *perek*: “One who is traveling and interrupts his study to observe the beauty of a tree or a plowed field is (similarly) considered to be *mis’chayeiv b’nafsho*.”\[^63] Travel offers an opportunity we are *responsible* to take advantage of. If we divert our attention from our learning, we are not worthy of the life we were given.

   Rebbe Chanina’s and Rebbe Shimon’s common usage of the term “*mis’chayeiv b’nafsho*” links their teachings and connects both of them to Rebbe Meir’s application of the same term (quoted after the teaching of Rebbe Shimon) to one who forgets his Torah learning: “Anyone who forgets (even) one point they learned is considered as if they are ‘*mischayeiv b’nafsho.*’”\[^64]

   The message that emerges from the three Tannaim’s usage of “*mis’chayeiv b’nafsho*” is that we are worthy of our lives only when we appreciate the opportunity to study Torah. One who shows a lack of appreciation by diverting his attention from it while traveling or casually forgetting what he has learned is “*mis’chayeiv b’nafsho*.”

   Rebbe Chanina’s and Rebbe Shimon’s message about travel is also very relevant to the modern world, where many spend hours alone in their cars or traveling with others they do not know. What do we do while we are driving or on the train, bus, or plane? Do we listen to meaningless things on the radio, read the paper, or let our minds wander?

   \[clt]What do we do while we are driving or on the train, bus, or plane?

   Travel time is an excellent opportunity to listen to a shiur or other Torah content. When we do so, we take full advantage of our time and fulfill the words we recite thrice daily in *kriyas shema* — “You should speak about them (words of Torah) when you sit in your home and when you *travel on the road*...”\[^65]

   When we do so, are worthy of the life Hashem gifts to us. One who does not is “*mis’chayev b’nafsho.*”\[^66]

   **Inspired to Learn**  
Traveling alone or being awake at night are not just opportunities to learn Torah; they are also situations that should inspire us to do so. The Rashbatz\[^67] connects the two explanations of the *mishnah* and explains that we should alleviate the potential danger posed by traveling alone or being up at night by learning Torah, which protects those who study it.\[^68] One in danger who intentionally chooses to focus on meaninglessness shows a complete disregard for the protective power of Torah and is, *because of this disregard*, “*mis’chayev b’nafsho.*”

   The Knesses Yisrael adds that travel and nighttime should also inspire us to think about Hashem. After a full day, when we prepare to return our soul to Hashem, we should reflect upon our relationship with Him. Similarly, when in potential danger while traveling alone, we should appreciate Hashem’s protection. One who turns his attention to meaninglessness at these sensitive moments shows deep insensitivity and is “*mis’chayev b’nafsho.*”

   **Owning It — A Positive Spin**  
Rav Nachman Mi’Breslov\[^69] recasts our *mishnah* positively. He explains that *“nei’or b’layla”* can refer to a person who takes advantage of the night (like the day) by learning Torah and being constructive. “*Mehalech b’derech yechidi”* can connote one who lives his life in his own, unique way, not affected by those who mock him. The *mishnah*’s third clause explains that one accomplishes these things by being *“mefaneh libo l’vatalah*,*”* clearing his heart of all meaningless ness and focusing only on what is meaningful. Rav Nachman explains that such a person is *“mis’chayev b’nafsho”* — *deserves* his life instead of receiving it as a gift.

   May the mishnah’s simple explanation caution us against misuse, and may Rav Nachman’s explanation inspire us to take advantage of our time and opportunities and, through this, “earn” our lives!  
**Appreciating Hashem's World and Ourselves**

   A World of Tens  
Creation Appreciation  
The Value of Man  
Dearly Beloved - Our Unique Relationships  
All In - The Segulah of the Jewish People  
If Not I  
If Only I

   **A World of Tens**

   בַּעֲשָׂרָה מַאֲמָרוֹת נִבְרָא הָעוֹלָם. וּמַה תַּלְמוּד לוֹמַר, וַהֲלֹא בְמַאֲמָר אֶחָד יָכוֹל לְהִבָּרְאוֹת?  
אֶלָּא לְהִפָּרַע מִן הָרְשָׁעִים שֶׁמְּאַבְּדִין אֶת הָעוֹלָם שֶׁנִּבְרָא בַּעֲשָׂרָה מַאֲמָרוֹת,  
וְלִתֵּן שָׂכָר טוֹב לַצַּדִּיקִים שֶׁמְּקַיְּמִין אֶת הָעוֹלָם שֶׁנִּבְרָא בַּעֲשָׂרָה מַאֲמָרוֹת: (אבות ה:א)  
*עֲשָׂרָה דוֹרוֹת מֵאָדָם וְעַד נֹחַ, לְהוֹדִיעַ כַּמָּה אֶרֶךְ אַפַּיִם לְפָנָיו, שֶׁכָּל הַדּוֹרוֹת הָיוּ מַכְעִיסִין וּבָאִין עַד שֶׁהֵבִיא עֲלֵיהֶם אֶת מֵי הַמַּבּוּל.*  
*עֲשָׂרָה דוֹרוֹת מִנֹּחַ וְעַד אַבְרָהָם, לְהוֹדִיעַ כַּמָּה אֶרֶךְ אַפַּיִם לְפָנָיו, שֶׁכָּל הַדּוֹרוֹת הָיוּ מַכְעִיסִין וּבָאִין, עַד שֶׁבָּא אַבְרָהָם **וְקִבֵּל שְׂכַר כֻּלָּם:** (ה:ב)*  
עֲשָׂרָה נִסְיוֹנוֹת נִתְנַסָּה אַבְרָהָם אָבִינוּ עָלָיו הַשָּׁלוֹם וְעָמַד בְּכֻלָּם…(ה:ג)  
עֲשָׂרָה נִסִּים נַעֲשׂוּ לַאֲבוֹתֵינוּ בְמִצְרַיִם וַעֲשָׂרָה עַל הַיָּם…(ה:ד)  
עֲשָׂרָה נִסִּים נַעֲשׂוּ לַאֲבוֹתֵינוּ בְּבֵית הַמִּקְדָּשׁ…(ה:ה)

   **Anonymous Numerical Lists**  
Avos’s fifth *perek* diverges from the previous four in two significant ways. Firstly, it includes (predominantly) anonymous statements. In addition, whereas the earlier *perakim* focus mainly on ethics and morals, the fifth presents numerical lists of various historical phenomena, often without delineating any ethical implication.

   The first half of the *perek* presents lists in descending numerical order. The first six *mishnayos* list groups of ten, the following three groups of seven, and the final six *mishnayos* list groups of four.

   **Lists of Ten**  
The lists of ten are uniquely significant because the number ten symbolizes something whole and complete.\[^70]

   The lists of ten appear in historical order. The *perek* begins with the ten *ma’amaros* (utterances) Hashem used to create the world, continues with the ten generations from Adam Harishon to Noach, and then lists the subsequent ten from Noach to Avraham. The third *mishnah* mentions the ten trials Hashem tested Avraham with, the ten miracles Hashem performed on behalf of the Jewish People in *Mitzrayim* and at the Yam Suf, the ten ways the Jews “tested” Hashem in the desert, and, finally, the ten miracles Hashem performed in the *Beis Hamikdash*.

   What do these lists and the juxtaposition between them teach us?

   **Significant Actions Significantly Impact a Significant World**  
A closer look at the lists shows they have a common theme. The theme begins with how the first *mishnah* explains the significance of the first list.

   The *mishnah* explains that Hashem created the world with ten *ma’amaros* in order to increase the reward and punishment *tzadikim* and *resha’im* (respectively) receive for their impact on it. The multiple *ma’amaros* reflect the significance of each aspect of the world we are rewarded for sustaining. *Hashem*’s creation with ten *ma’amaros* is meant to inspire *us* to take *our* role seriously.\[^71]

   Avos D'Rebbe Nosson\[^72] specifies three actions sufficient to maintain the entire world: keeping (even) one Shabbos, saving (even) one life, and observing even one *mitzvah*. Man supports the world by emulating Hashem’s actions, such as His cessation of work on Shabbos and His protection of man.

   We should all see our actions as the ones that sustain (or destroy) the valuable world Hashem invested ten *ma’amaros* in. The *gemara*\[^73] asserts that one should (always) see the world as half meritorious and half liable. *His* actions determine not only his fate but also the *entire* world’s.

   \[clt]Each person is meant to see their actions as those that sustain or destroy a valuable world.

   **The Generations — Noach, Avraham, and the Jewish People**  
The *perek*’s second *mishnah* teaches that Hashem tolerated ten generations of sin twice.\[^74] Rashi\[^75] explains that Hashem did this, hoping that even one person's actions (Noach or Avraham) would justify His tolerance. The ten *ma’amaros* gave the world a chance; the ten generations gave man a chance.

   Noach’s actions saved him and his family but were not enough to save his generation. Avraham’s were. As opposed to Noach, who failed to influence his surroundings, Avraham influenced others and succeeded in steering (at least part of) the world in the right direction.\[^76] Through this, he saved his generation and received the reward intended for the ten generations that preceded him. Avraham was not just a righteous individual; he impacted his surroundings and, thereby, received the reward “of others” (in appreciation of his influence upon them).

   **Test and Reward**  
In addition to justifying the lives of *ten* generations, Avraham passed Hashem’s *ten* tests. This success exemplified the significance of complete commitment to Hashem.

   Rashi links Avraham’s ten tests to the ten *ma’amaros* that created the world. Avraham’s unflinching commitment\[^77] to Hashem’s will justified creation. After two sets of ten generations, the goal of the ten *ma’amaros* was finally realized.

   Avos D'Rebbe Nosson\[^78] connects the next set of tens, the desert miracles, to Avraham’s ten tests. It was Avraham’s commitment and trust that merited the miracles Hashem performed for his descendants. Hashem rewarded Avraham’s supernatural commitment with supernatural intervention on behalf of later generations of Jews.

   May our following Hashem’s directives and passing His tests sustain the world He created and merit His performance of miracles on our behalf and the behalf of many generations of our descendants!

   ## **Creation Appreciation**

## 

   ## הוּא (בן עזאי) הָיָה אוֹמֵר, אַל תְּהִי בָז לְכָל אָדָם, וְאַל תְּהִי מַפְלִיג לְכָל דָּבָר,

   ## שֶׁאֵין לְךָ אָדָם שֶׁאֵין לוֹ שָׁעָה וְאֵין לְךָ דָבָר שֶׁאֵין לוֹ מָקוֹם (אבות ד:ג):

   ## **Every Thing Beautiful In Its Time and Place**

   We have seen how Hashem’s role as creator gives Him ownership rights to the world. His creation should also enhance our appreciation of everything in existence. Our world’s inhabitants and objects are not here coincidently; they were all created intentionally by Hashem and thus have great significance.

   Ben Azay taught, \*“\*Do not be scornful of any person and do not be disdainful of any thing, for each person has his hour and each thing has its place.”\[^79] As the colloquial saying goes, “Every dog has its day.”

   This idea is rooted in Sefer *Koheles*, which explains its assertion that “everything has its time”\[^80] by adding that “Hashem *made each thing* beautiful in its proper time.”\[^81]

   This idea has two implications which are both specified by the *mishnah*: the need to appreciate other people (*kol Adam*) and all the rest of creation (*kol davar*).

   ## **Appreciating People**

   Pirkei Avos emphasizes the importance of appreciating other people properly by strongly discouraging *sinas ha’briyos* (hating creations)\[^82] and highly encouraging *ahavas ha’briyos* (loving creations.\[^83] The Ba’al HaTanya\[^84] explains that Avos specifically uses the term “*briyos”* to describe people in order to teach the need to love even those we see nothing positive in besides the fact that Hashem created them.

   The Maharal\[^85] explains that one who truly loves Hashem loves everything He creates.\[^86] By loving and showing respect to His creations, we, in essence, show respect to Hashem Himself.\[^87]

   Though Chazal use the term *sinas chinam,* they never use the term *ahavas chinam*. This is because the love of Hashem’s creations is never baseless. The fact that Hashem created something is reason enough to love it.

   \[clt]The fact that Hashem created something is sufficient reason to love it.

   Rav Tzvi Yehudah Kook explained that this is why Pirkei Avos\[^88] twice lists the love of “*briyos*” right after the love of Hashem. What does the love of Hashem mean if it does not express itself in the love of his creations? Also, as we do not see Hashem himself, we can only come to love Him by appreciating His word (the Torah) and His world.\[^89]

   This basis for loving others can be inferred from the addition of “I am Hashem” to the *pasuk* that commands us to love others like ourselves — “Love your fellow as yourself; I am Hashem.”\[^90] Hashem explains that we should love others because He is the one who created them.

   This is the lesson Eliyahu HaNavi taught Rebbe Elazar b’Rebbe Shimon. Rebbe Elazar was riding high on his donkey because he had mastered much Torah. Eliyahu appeared as an ugly person and greeted him. Rebbe Elazar did not return the greeting but instead called Eliyahu an empty and ugly person.\[^91] Eliyahu responded that Rebbe Elazar should tell this to “the One who created him” *—* Hashem. Immediately realizing his mistake, Rebbe Elazar begged for forgiveness.\[^92]

   No matter how learned or accomplished one is, it is critical to continue respecting all people. They are all Hashem’s creations and are thus valuable and worthy of love and respect.\[^93]

   Rav (Avraham Yitzchak) Kook summarized this first implication by teaching that “the love that naturally resides in the souls of *tzaddikim* includes all creations. It excludes no object or nation — not even Amalek!”\[^94]

## 

   ## **Beyond People**

   The *mishnah* also mentions a second implication. We should appreciate not only people but *all* of Hashem’s creations. The *medrash*\[^95] famously tells us that Hashem created each thing for a purpose. He uses even the smallest animals (like frogs) as His emissaries. In fact, because Dovid HaMelech doubted the value of spiders and their webs, Hashem arranged for him to need them when hiding him from Shaul.\[^96]

   Rebbe Shalom Dov Ber of Lubavitch applied this to every leaf of every tree.  He was once on a leisurely walk through the forest with his young son Yosef Yitzchok (the Freirdiker Rebbe), who absentmindedly plucked a leaf off a tree. Surprised, the father turned to his son and admonished him for his seemingly harmless action:  
The leaf you tore from its branch was created by the Ribbono Shel Olam for a specific purpose! It’s alive, its physical structure is akin to a body, it’s imbued with a Divine life-force, it’s guided by *hashgacha pratis* (Divine Providence). Every blade of grass, every leaf on every tree is invested with G-d’s own vitality, created intentionally, each with a Divine spark, part of a “soul” that has descended to earth to find its correction and fulfillment. How can you be so callous towards a creation of G-d?\[^97]

   The Rishonim extended this idea to include even natural phenomena. The Ramban,\[^98], for example, proves the holiness of the physical relationship between man and woman from the fact that Hashem created it.

   Rav Kook formulated this broader idea beautifully: “Anyone who thinks in a pure, godly way cannot hate or denigrate any creation or potential found in our world.”\[^99] He explains further that hatred and denigration are rooted in our choosing to focus on what is lacking instead of identifying and appreciating the positive potential Hashem imbues His creations with.

   In a second piece,\[^100] Rav Kook applies the same concept to every human thought. He explains that  
“All thoughts are logical and worthwhile. Even those that seem illogical are rooted in principles of logic and value.  
All people are “treasure-houses” of higher meaning whose thoughts need to be “filtered” to expose their true deeper meaning.  
The greater we are as people, the more thoughts we succeed at identifying as valuable.  
When we do so, we elevate these thoughts and become elevated through them.

   **Appreciating It All**  
The upshot of these *mishnayos* is that everything Hashem creates has value and holiness and needs to be appreciated. We often take our world for granted and see its contents as mundane and insignificant. Avos reminds us that everything is created by Hashem and should be appreciated as such. By doing so, we deepen our understanding and appreciation of the world, ourselves, and Hashem Himself.

   Though all creations are significant, human beings — particularly their thoughts and mission — are of even greater value and importance. In the next chapter, we will see how Maseches Avos relates to and deepens our appreciation of this point.  
**The Value of Man**

   הוּא (רבי עקיבא) הָיָה אוֹמֵר, חָבִיב אָדָם שֶׁנִּבְרָא בְצֶלֶם.  
**חִבָּה** **יְתֵרָה נוֹדַעַת לוֹ** שֶׁנִּבְרָא בְצֶלֶם,  
שֶׁנֶּאֱמַר (בראשית ט), כִּי בְּצֶלֶם אֱלֹקים עָשָׂה אֶת הָאָדָם. (אבות ג:יד)

   Even though all of Hashem’s creations are important, Rebbe Akiva explains in Avos’s third *perek* that Hashem created human beings in His own image, and they are, therefore, uniquely precious.\[^101]

   Dovid HaMelech also expressed this idea in Sefer Tehillim. The climax of his praise for all aspects of creation is: “What is man that You have been mindful of him, that You have taken note of him, that You have made him just a bit less than the Divine and adorned him with glory and honor?”\[^102]

   Chazal saw this incredible notion — that man is “just a bit less than the Divine” — as something that should impact how we treat both ourselves and others.

   **Body and Soul**  
Hillel\[^103] defined bathing as a *mitzvah* because washing ourselves shows respect to G-d, whose “image” we bear. He compared cleansing our bodies to the washing of the statues of leaders. Because Hashem imbued our bodies with godlike creative powers, even our physical bodies (and not just our souls) are representative (on some level) of Hashem.

   Parshas Ki Teitzei takes this message further by commanding us to respect the human body *even after death*. The corpse of someone killed by *beis din* (which, in some instances, needs to be hung on a tree to deter others from committing similar sins) must be taken down before nightfall “for the denigration of G-d hangs.”\[^104]

   Rebbe Meir\[^105] explains the association of the hanging body with G-d through a *mashal*. There were twin brothers who pursued divergent paths — one became king, the other a criminal. When the criminal was caught and hung for his crimes, observers mistakenly thought that the king himself was hanging.

   Though surprising, the message is clear. When a human body hangs, it seems as if G-d Himself hangs!

   **Greater Than *Ka’mocha***  
Our appreciation for having been created in G-d’s image should also impact how we relate to other human beings.

   After presenting Rebbe Akiva, who saw the *pasuk* of “*v’ahavta l’rei’acha ka’mocha*” as the Torah’s greatest principle, the Sifra\[^106] quotes Ben Azay, who chose the Torah’s description of man created in G-d’s image as an even greater principle. The *medrash*\[^107] explains that Ben Azay chose this *pasuk* because it mandates respecting others *beyond* the level we (choose to) respect ourselves.

   We should show all humans the respect they deserve for being created in G-d’s image. When we respect others, we respect Hashem. This is why the *mishnah* in Avos’s fourth *perek*\[^108] compares respecting other people to respecting Hashem Himself.\[^109]

   \[clt]When we respect others, we respect Hashem.

   **Knowing and Appreciating**  
Returning to the *mishnah* in Avos, we note that it contains an additional line that emphasizes that Hashem not only created man in His image but also *let him know about it*. In addition to creating us in His image, Hashem told us about it so we could know what to live up to.

   We can appreciate the importance of this knowledge by studying the fate of those who lived between man’s creation in Hashem’s image and Hashem telling man about it. Though the Torah described Hashem creating man in His image in its first *perek*,\[^110] Hashem only told man about it in the ninth *perek*, following the flood.\[^111]

   This explains the failure of the generations who lived in between — those who died in the flood. Though Adam Harishon and his descendants were created in Hashem’s image, they did not live up to their potential (and, in many ways, acted like animals partly) because they were unaware of it. Hashem restarted civilization after the flood by *telling* Noach and his children that they were created in His image,[^112](Ibid.) hoping that this knowledge would inspire them to emulate Him and His ways.

   Humans are very similar to animals. We are programmed to ensure our survival by pursuing food and procreation. We differ from animals in that we are created in Hashem’s image. May our awareness and internalization of this preciousness inspire us to live meaningful lives and realize our godly potential!

   **Dearly Beloved — Our Unique Relationships**

   הוּא (רַבִּי עֲקִיבָא) הָיָה אוֹמֵר… חֲבִיבִין יִשְׂרָאֵל שֶׁנִּקְרְאוּ בָנִים לַמָּקוֹם.  
חִבָּה יְתֵרָה נוֹדַעַת לָהֶם שֶׁנִּקְרְאוּ בָנִים לַמָּקוֹם, שֶׁנֶּאֱמַר (דברים יד) בָּנִים אַתֶּם לַה' אֱלֹקיכֶם (אבות ג:יד)

   **Hashem’s Children**  
After mentioning that all humans are created in Hashem’s image, Rebbe Akiva adds that the Jewish people are (even more) precious in that they are Hashem’s children. Though all humans were created in Hashem’s image, we have a unique personal relationship with Him.\[^113]

   Hashem first mentioned this idea in His message to Paroh: “Yisrael is my first-born son... If you refuse to release them, I will kill your firstborn.”\[^114] Hashem presented His relationship with us as the philosophical basis for *Makas Bechoros*.

   The *mishnah* adds that Hashem also let the Jewish people know about this special relationship and of our need to conduct ourselves accordingly.\[^115] The Torah introduces the prohibition of “*Lo sisgodidu”* (which, when translated  literally, prohibits gashing oneself) with the words: “You are children of Hashem, your G-d.” As Hashem’s children, we need to maintain a comely physical appearance.\[^116]

   This idea has implications on boththe  *bein Adam laMakom* (between man and G-d) and *bein Adam l’chaveiro* (interpersonal) levels\*.\*

   ***Bein Adam LaMakom:*** **Through Thick and Thin**  
Like any familial (blood) relationship, our relationship with Hashem is permanent and immutable. Rebbe Meir quotes many *pesukim* to prove that “either this way (when we act properly) or that way (when we do not), we are still called (Hashem’s) children.”\[^117]

   This idea is also expressed at the beginning of Sefer Hoshei’a,\[^118] where Hashem commands Hoshei’a to marry and have children with a harlot. The *gemara*\[^119] explains the backdrop to this perplexing command. After Hoshei’a responded to Hashem’s complaint about the sinful Jews by suggesting that He exchange them for another people, Hashem sought to help Hoshei’a appreciate His relationship with the Jewish people by challenging him to separate himself from his own disloyal wife and her children.

   Hashem’s relationship with us, like the relationship of any parent with his children, is natural and (thus) eternal. No sin — no matter how severe — can sever it.\[^120]

   The *gemara*\[^121] describes a similar conversation between Hashem and Yitzchak Avinu. At the End of Days, Hashem notifies each of the Avos that “their children” have sinned. After Avraham and Yaakov accept the Jews’ deserved punishment, Yitzchak responds by “reminding” Hashem that the Jews are (still) His children. At that point, the Jews show their appreciation to Yitzchak by identifying him as their *Av*. Yitzchak directs their attention to Hashem, our eternal Father.

   Rebbe Akiva, the author of our *mishnah*, emphasizes the importance of our father-son relationship with Hashem in additional contexts as well. He instituted the *taanis tefilah* of “*Avinu Malkeinu,*”\[^122], and encouraged rejoicing on Yom Kippur because “our Father in Heaven” purifies us.\[^123] Rebbe Akiva taught us that, no matter how desperate our situation, we can always ask Hashem, our Father, for forgiveness and assistance.\[^124]



   ***Bein Adam L’chaveiro:*** **Brotherly Love**  
Our father-son relationship with Hashem should impact our relationship with other Jews as well. The Navi Malachi\[^125] uses it as a basis for *achdus*: As we are all Hashem’s children, we should see all Jews as our brothers.

   Similarly, the Ritva\[^126] uses this idea to explain the *gemara*’s[^127](Ibid.) understanding that “*Lo sisgodidu”* prohibits not only gashing skin but also dividing ourselves into distinct subgroups. It is understandable for a *pasuk* that begins by describing us as Hashem’s children to then prohibit our disunity. We should be unified because we are all brothers with a common father.

   \[clt]As we are all Hashem’s children, we should see all Jews as our brothers.

   The Rambam\[^128] also formulated the *bein Adam l’chaveiro* implications of our relationship with Hashem. He used it to explain the responsibility to support and assist other Jews. If brothers do not care for one another, who else will? We are not just relatives. We are all brothers.

   Rav Kook\[^129] used this idea to explain his objection to the secession of Orthodox Jews from the official non-Orthodox-dominated European Jewish communities. As we are all brothers, children of Hashem, he saw separating from one another as an affront to Hashem’s oneness.\[^130]

   May we appreciate our special relationships with Hashem and, through Him, with one another!  
**All In — The Segulah of the Jewish People**

   כָּל יִשְׂרָאֵל יֵשׁ לָהֶם חֵלֶק לָעוֹלָם הַבָּא, שֶׁנֶּאֱמַר וְעַמֵּךְ כֻּלָּם צַדִּיקִים… (אבות, הקדמה; סנהדרין י:א)

   The last chapter focused on our special Father-son relationship with Hashem. Our unique status is also evident in the *mishnah* we recite before learning Pirkei Avos, which confirms that *all* Jews have a place in *Olam Haba*.\[^131]

   **We All Have Hope**  
The Machzor Vitri explains that we recite this *mishnah* before studying Pirkei Avos because it inspires all Jews — even the uneducated and the sinners — to learn Torah and seek growth. Since we all have a portion in the next world, we should seek to further cultivate our portion by improving ourselves.\[^132]

   The Sefas Emes\[^133] identifies the same approach in Rava’s insistence on informing the uneducated about their ability to fulfill the mitzvah of *talmud Torah*. The great *Tanna* Rashbi (Rebbe Shimon Bar Yochai) held that one fulfills the *mitzvah* through even a bit of Torah learning each day and each night. Out of fear that people would settle for this minimal amount, he insisted that this *halachah* be withheld from the masses.

   *Amoraim* generally avoid disagreeing with *Tannaim*, but the *Amora* Rava does so here. He takes the *opposite* position — that it is a *mitzvah* to inform the masses of this *halachah*.\[^134] The Sefas Emes explains that Rava disagreed with Rashbi because he wanted those with limited time to know that they, too, could fulfill the *mitzvah* of *talmud Torah*.\[^135]  No one should exclude themselves from Torah study because they regard the *mitzvah* as impossible to fulfill.

   **Eventual Arrival**  
Tosafos\[^136] also emphasized the idea of Jewish inclusiveness in *Olam Haba*. He explains that even *reshaim,* who the *gemara* describes as “written and sealed for Gehinom,”\[^137] eventually gain entry to *Olam Haba*.

   The gemara in Berachos\[^138] teaches a similar idea. It explains that even the “emptiest” Jews are as full of *mitzvos* as a pomegranate is full of seeds.\[^139] All Jews — even those who seem empty — have a deep connection to *mitzvos\[^140]* and, therefore, have a place in *Olam Haba*.

   **Segulah and Jewish Identity**  
Our guaranteed place in *Olam Haba* may be connected to something more intrinsic. In the leadup to *Matan Torah*, Hashem told the Jewish people that he was establishing them as His *am segulah*,\[^141] His beloved treasure. This seems to be intrinsic to every Jew, irrespective of his life choices. We are born precious, and we die and enter *Olam Haba* the same way.\[^142]

   Rav Kook\[^143] used this concept to explain why he supported the Zionist settlers. People were surprised at Rav Kook and critical of this support. Though the settlers were committed to the Land of Israel and the Jewish people, many were non-religious or even *anti*-religious. Rav Kook saw their connection to the *segulah* aspect of Judaism as the source of their focus on the nationalistic elements of Jewish peoplehood and the land of Israel. Though they were gravely mistaken in disregarding *mitzvah* observance, their identification as Jews and their actions on behalf of our people and land accentuated a critical facet of Jewish identity.\[^144]

   **Tzaddikim Together**  
In another piece, Rav Kook\[^145] related to the *pasuk* that our *mishnah* quotes as a proof text: “*V’ameich kulam tzaddikim.*” The simple translation of the pasuk is, “And your people are all righteous.” Rav Kook, though, had a powerful, broader interpretation. He explained the *pasuk* as defining *tzaddikim* as connected to the rest of the Jewish people — even the *reshaim*.\[^146] *Tzaddikim* are those connected to *all* of the Jewish people.

   \[clt] People are considered *tzaddikim* only when they attach themselves to the entire Jewish people — even the *reshaim*.

   Rav Kook explained that no matter how wicked *reshaim* may be, as long as they identify with the Jewish people, they remain part of “*V’ameich kulam tzaddikim.*” He added that even sinners are part of the “aging wine” of the Jewish nation (even if only the “sediments”).

   May the knowledge that mere identification with the Jewish people ensures our place among them in this world and the next inspire us to take full advantage of both!  
**If Not I**

   ֿֿהוּא (הִלֵּל) הָיָה אוֹמֵר, אִם אֵין אֲנִי לִי, מִי לִי (אבות א:יד)

   **It’s On Me**  
Though many help us throughout our lives, in the end, we are responsible for ourselves. Others cannot be, will not be, and are not meant to be responsible in our place.

   \[clt]Others cannot be, will not be, and are not intended to be responsible in our place.

   It is convenient for us to believe that our success hinges on forces and conditions beyond our control. One might get such an impression from the *gemara,* which asserts that “everything is in the hands of Heaven, except for fear of Heaven.”\[^147] The Rambam\[^148] dispels this mistaken impression by explaining that our personal growth is part of *yiras Shamayim*, which is in *our* hands. Hashem sets the circumstances in the world around us; how we respond and develop ourselves is up to us.\[^149]

   Other people can help us, but our growth does not depend upon them. The Seforno sees this as the message of the *pasuk*\[^150] which describes *teshuvah* as “not above you or beyond your reach.” We do not need *nevi’im* (above us) or wise men in faraway lands (beyond us) in order to grow. Even though both offer important guidance, we have all that we really need within ourselves; growth is in our hands, and we need not and cannot offload it to anyone else.

   The support we receive from others is also limited to our time with them. Rabbeinu Yonah\[^151] reminds us that making good decisions and consistent, continuous growth (even when we are alone) hinges on what we internalize, not (just) on who our friends and neighbors are. We must take full responsibility for ourselves; no one else will or can do so in our place.

   We learn this from Elazar Ben Durdaya.\[^152] After deciding to do *teshuvah* for his initial sinful lifestyle, he asked the mountains and hills, the sky and earth, and the sun, moon and stars to help him. After each responded that they needed to focus on themselves, he realized that “*ein ha’davar talui ella bi* — the thing depends only on me.”

   After crying himself to death while curled up in the fetal position, a *bas kol* announced that *Rebbe* Elazar ben Durdaya merited entry into *Olam Haba*. Rebbe Yehudah Hanasi responded by reflecting upon how, as opposed to the many who spend their entire lives earning entry into the next world, some do so in *one* moment — the moment they take full responsibility for themselves and their growth. His momentous realization earned Rebbe Elazar not only a portion in the World to Come but also the title “Rebbe.” He taught us all the great significance of accepting responsibility for ourselves.

   **Our Unique Importance**  
The Sefas Emes\[^153] understands our *mishnah* as containing an additional message. We must take responsibility for ourselves because each person is independently significant. Hashem created each person differently because He intends a unique mission for each of us.\[^154] If we rely only upon others to inspire our growth, inevitably, our identity will reflect theirs (rather than our true selves), and we will miss the unique role we are meant to play.\[^155]

   \[clt]If we rely only upon others to inspire our growth, inevitably, our identity will come to reflect theirs, and we will miss the unique role we are meant to play.

   The Kotzker Rebbe taught: “If I am I because you are you, and you are you because I am I  — then I am not I, and you are not you. But if I am I because I am I, and you are you because you are you — then I am I, and you are you.” Each of us is meant to be a unique person playing a particular role. Though we ought to learn from and be inspired by others, we need to take responsibility for forging our singular identity and (through this) realize our unique potential.

   May this *mishnah* inspire us to take full responsibility for ourselves and, through this, fulfill the mission Hashem created us to accomplish!  
**If Only I**

   הוּא (הִלֵּל) הָיָה אוֹמֵר, אִם אֵין אֲנִי לִי, מִי לִי. וּכְשֶׁאֲנִי לְעַצְמִי, מָה אֲנִי. (אבות א:יד)

   After emphasizing the importance of personal responsibility by stating, “If I am not for myself, who is for me?” Hillel immediately presents the flip side by adding, “When I am (only) for myself, what am I?”\[^156]

   The *meforshim* understand Hillel’s teaching on multiple levels which include the importance of gaining from and contributing to others, identifying with others, and seeing our existence as inclusive of others.

   **Our Interaction With Others**  
Rav Chaim Volozhin\[^157] explains Hillel’s statement as reminding us of our dependence upon Heavenly assistance. Though responsible for putting in our own effort, we need Hashem’s support to help us realize our potential.

   The Midrash Shmuel sees Hillel as encouraging us to think beyond ourselves. Accepting responsibility for ourselves should not lead us to care *only* about ourselves. If we focus *solely* on ourselves, then “what are we?” A person whose life begins and ends with himself is of little significance.

   Judaism emphasizes our responsibility for one another and the importance of viewing ourselves as part of the larger Jewish community. The Torah teaches the obligation of *tochachah,*\[^158] the requirement to admonish sinners. We should care about the mistakes others make and commit ourselves to helping them improve.

   The principle of “*Kol Yisrael areivin zeh ba’zeh*”\[^159] takes this a step further by defining each Jew as *responsible and culpable* for the actions of others.\[^160]

   It is also critical that one identify as part of the Jewish community. Many\[^161] explain that Hashem told Moshe to teach how to attain holiness in the presence of the entire Jewish people in order to emphasize that we achieve holiness through our relationship with the whole Jewish people, not through a life of seclusion.

   According to the Rambam,\[^162] this is not just an ideal. It is a *condition* for receiving a portion in the next world. The only way to gain entry into the next world is by connecting to, caring for, and acting together with the Jewish people in this one.

   **Our Existence**  
Hillel’s language (“What am I?”) hints at a deeper, existential component. Naturally, people view their existence as limited to themselves. Rav Shimon Shkop, the great Pre World War Two Rosh Yeshiva, labeled this as crude. He explained that more refined people see their existence as inclusive of other Jews and, ideally, all of Hashem’s creations.\[^163]

   People often see care for others as competing with care for themselves. Rav Shimon explains that since our existence should include all others, caring for others actually *complements* caring for ourselves.

   Rav Kook\[^164] encouraged using this broad, existential perspective to inspire us to live our lives properly. Our existential connection to the rest of creation means our actions impact much more than just ourselves. Hashem revealed (the kabbalistic details of) how our actions affect the rest of the world in order to help us appreciate ourselves and their significance. Our job is to internalize this reality and view our lives and actions accordingly.

   \[clt]Our existential connection to the rest of creation means our actions impact much more than just ourselves.

   **Soul Perspective**  
The Ba’al Hatanya\[^165] uses a similar idea to explain Hillel’s response to the convert who asked to learn the entire Torah while standing on one foot: “Don’t do to others what you don’t want done to yourself — this is the whole Torah.”\[^166] As important as this idea is, how could Hillel present it as the *entirety* of the Torah?

   The Ba’al Hatanya explains that the ability to care for others on the same level we care for ourselves hinges on focusing on our spiritual (as opposed to our physical) existence. On the physical level, human beings are separate, independent entities. On the spiritual soul level, we are all part of a larger whole. Because the Torah ultimately aims to focus us on the spiritual, caring about others as we do ourselves reflects a value that is the core of the entire Torah.\[^167]

   **Balance**  
The Sefas Emes brings the two sides of our *mishnah* together and summarizes that, on the one hand, we must appreciate that we are each unique, essential, and responsible for our own growth and success. On the other hand, we need to see ourselves as part of the broader picture of Klal Yisrael and Hashem’s whole creation. May we always be mindful of both perspectives!  
**Goal, Purpose, and Perspective**

   &#x20;   Purpose- The World's and Our Own


   Our Perspective on Our Lives

   The World’s Three Pillars

   Building The World: Home and Society  
**Purpose- The World's and Our Own**

   For Heaven’s Sake: The World

   For Heaven’s Sake: The Jewish People

   Why We Do

   What We Want  
**For Heaven’s Sake: The World**

   כָּל מַה שֶּׁבָּרָא הַקָּדוֹשׁ בָּרוּךְ הוּא בְּעוֹלָמוֹ, לֹא בְרָאוֹ אֶלָּא לִכְבוֹדוֹ,  
שֶׁנֶּאֱמַר (ישעיה מג), כֹּל הַנִּקְרָא בִשְׁמִי וְלִכְבוֹדִי בְּרָאתִיו יְצַרְתִּיו אַף עֲשִׂיתִיו,  
וְאוֹמֵר (שמות טו), ה' יִמְלֹךְ לְעֹלָם וָעֶד (אבות ו:יא).

   **For Him**  
Pirkei Avos ends with a powerful and critical message. Building off Sefer Yeshaya, the *mishnah* writes, “Everything that Hashem created in His world, He created only for His honor.” Though we may not be able to fully understand Hashem’s intentions,\[^168] we know that, at least on some level, all of creation exists to honor Him.

   The Ba’al HaTanya\[^169] links our *mishnah* to the *medrash*’s description of Hashem wanting His Presence to reside in a world in which His Presence is unclear.\[^170] The creation of our world allows Him to illuminate (through man) an otherwise dark reality.\[^171]

   Based on this mishnah, the Ramban saw man’s recognition of G-d as the world’s purpose. He writes: “The goal of all the *mitzvos* is for us to believe in our G-d and recognize that He created us. This, and only this, is the creation’s intention. G-d above seeks from this world only that man should know Him and admit that He created him.”\[^172]

   **For Us?**  
Interestingly, another *medrash*\[^173] uses the *same* formulation (“*kol mah she’barah*”) in a (seemingly) very different way. It explains that when Hashem showed the world to Adam Harishon, He told him that He created it all *for him*. This *medrash* implies that everything Hashem created was for *man*’s use, not Hashem’s honor.

   Rav Yosef Karo\[^174] and the Ramchal\[^175] take this idea further by explaining that Hashem created the world to allow people to develop their souls through personal choice and struggle. Instead of being naturally close to G-d in a world devoid of alternatives, Hashem allows us to choose closeness to Him in a world where His presence is hidden.\[^176] The Ramchal adds that our mission is to realize that real, meaningful pleasure is closeness to Hashem in the next world and (thus) a relationship with Him in this one.\[^177]

   \[clt]Hashem allows us to choose closeness to Him in a world where His presence is hidden.

   These sources seem to imply that the world was created for us and our own personal development, not for Hashem’s honor.\[^178] How can we reconcile these contradictory understandings of why Hashem created the world?

   **The Goal Versus the Means**  
Rav Chaim Freidlander\[^179] explains the relationship between these two ideas by distinguishing between the goal and the means. Obviously, Hashem does not need this world nor the honor He “receives” from it. The world exists (as depicted by the *medrash*, Rav Yosef Karo, and the Ramchal) to allow us to develop *ourselves*.\[^180]

   The *mishnah* in Avos, on the other hand, is explaining the *means* — *how we achieve this growth*. The *mishnah* teaches that personal growth hinges on our appreciation that we and the world exist only to recognize and glorify G-d. Though the ultimate goal is personal growth, this growth occurs through committing ourselves to glorifying G-d.

   The idea that our recognition and service of Hashem help us grow is emphasized by Moshe’s description of *yiras Shamayim* (fear of heaven). On the one hand, he presents *yiras Shamayim* (and *mitzvah* observance) as Hashem’s requests from us. On the other hand, he emphasizes that this “request” is made for *our own* good.\[^181] Hashem does not need and gains nothing from our world.\[^182] *Yiras Shamayim* and *mitzvah* observance are for *our* benefit.\[^183]

   **The Universe and Man**  
The centrality of *kevod Hashem* as the world’s goal helps us understand why Hashem created such a vast and intricate universe. Dovid HaMelech exclaimed, “The heavens declare the glory of G-d; the sky proclaims His handiwork.”\[^184] *Pesukei D'zimra* reinforces this idea with the *pasuk*, “Our Lord is great and full of power. His wisdom is beyond reckoning.”\[^185] The universe reflects the G-d who created it. Only Hashem could have created the boundless, sophisticated world we can only scratch the surface of understanding.

   Man, created in the image of G-d, is able to appreciate and express this recognition on a higher level than the rest of creation. That is why we exclaim in our *tefilah*: “Blessed is He, our G-d, Who created us for His honor.”\[^186] Man is created for the glory of G-d, to recognize and appreciate Him.

   That said, sometimes our unique, G-d-given abilities go to our heads, and we see our lives as about ourselves. This “forces” G-d to remind us of our rightful place in His world.

   **Whose Name?**  
The snake claimed that Hashem forbade eating from the Eitz Hada’as because He did not want man to be like Him.\[^187] The snake was correct: Hashem does not want us to be like Him. We need to know that we exist to serve and glorify His name, not to focus on ourselves.

   This problem continued in the generation after the flood with their tower building. The stated goal of the builders was to “make a name” for themselves.\[^188] To keep them from focusing on themselves and their own name, Hashem dispersed them by introducing multiple languages.\[^189] When man recognizes G-d and works on His behalf, he can enjoy the world and work with others on this sacred mission. When he focuses on his own name, Hashem foils his plans and breaks up the misguided unity. This is the backdrop to Hillel’s words at the end of Avos’s first *perek*: “One who advances his (own) name, destroys his name.”\[^190]

   The contrast to the tower builders was Shem ben Noach and his descendants. Shem facilitated the first *kiddush Hashem* (sanctification of Hashem’s name) when his noble actions caused Noach to exclaim, “Blessed is Hashem, the G-d of Shem.”\[^191]

   His descendant, Avraham Avinu, was the first to disseminate Hashem’s name among the masses.\[^192] Avraham’s mission was bringing people close to Hashem. Understandably, Hashem promised to make Avraham’s name great.\[^193] When we realize that we are here to serve and glorify Hashem’s name, the greatness of our name contributes to this higher goal.

   The *medrash*\[^194] attributes the contrast between focusing on our name versus Hashem’s name to Avraham himself. Avraham responded to those who asked for his help building the tower, “You've abandoned G-d's name, and you want me to help you make a name for yourselves?!”\[^195]

   Hashem chose Avraham’s descendants — the Jewish People — to be a nation focused on glorifying His name. Instead of being focused on a tower that celebrates our name and achievements, we build a Beis Hamikdash that marks Hashem’s name and centrality. The next chapter will elaborate further on our people’s unique role.  
**For Heaven’s Sake: The Jewish People**

   כָּל מַה שֶּׁבָּרָא הַקָּדוֹשׁ בָּרוּךְ הוּא בְּעוֹלָמוֹ, לֹא בְרָאוֹ אֶלָּא לִכְבוֹדוֹ,  
שֶׁנֶּאֱמַר (ישעיה מג), כֹּל הַנִּקְרָא בִשְׁמִי וְלִכְבוֹדִי בְּרָאתִיו יְצַרְתִּיו אַף עֲשִׂיתִיו,  
וְאוֹמֵר (שמות טו), ה׳ יִמְלֹךְ לְעֹלָם וָעֶד (אבות ו:יא).

   As we saw in the last chapter, Pirkei Avos explains that the world was created for the glory of G-d. Though the *whole* universe proclaims this notion, man is uniquely able to appreciate and express it.

   After the failures and challenges of humanity’s first generations, Avraham Avinu emerged as the one who recognized this calling. This is the backdrop to the purpose and unique mission of his descendants — the Jewish people.

   **Our Purpose and Importance**  
The *medrash*\[^196] learns from a *pasuk in* Sefer Yeshayahu\[^197] (that appears in the *same perek* as the *pasuk* quoted by the *mishnah*) that the Jewish people were created in order to sing Hashem's praises.\[^198] The *medrash* takes this further by asserting that the Jewish people *themselves* embody G-d’s glory and that one of the purposes of our *galus* (exile) is to spread G-d’s name worldwide.

   This explains why the Jewish people are central to the world’s creation and continued existence. Rashi\[^199] (quoting the *medrash*) derived this idea from the Torah’s *very* *first* word — *Bereishis*.\[^200] Rashi explained that the word implies that Hashem created the world for “things called *reishis”* — the Torah and the Jewish people. Since the world’s *raison d’etre* is to honor Hashem, it focuses on Am Yisrael, who learn Torah and affirm Hashem’s honor by living Torah-inspired lives.

   The Netziv\[^201] used creation’s focus on the Jews’ Torah learning to explain the Beha”g’s characterization of Sefer Shemos as “*Sefer Ha’sheini*.” Why would Shemos be described as a mere continuation of Sefer Bereishis and not receive its own independent name and identity? The Netziv explains that the name reflects the fact that creation, and thereby Sefer Bereishis, were not completed until the Jewish people accepted the Torah in Sefer Shemos.\[^202] Sefer Shemos is, therefore, the Sefer Haseini — the continuation and completion of Sefer Bereishis.

   **Our Role: Kiddush Hashem**  
This helps explain the importance of *kiddush Hashem* (sanctification of G-d’s name) and the severity of *chilul Hashem* (desecration of His name).

   Like the prohibition against idolatry, *chilul Hashem* is one of the few *aveiros* we are commanded to sacrifice our lives to avoid.\[^203] The Sefer HaChinuch\[^204] explains that “the root of this commandment is well-known: man was created only to serve his Creator.” We should show that we are entirely dedicated to and prepared to forfeit our lives (when called) for *Avodas Hashem*.

   This beautifully explains why, in the “*Al Hanisim*” prayer recited on Chanukah, we mention: “And You made Yourself a great and sanctified name in Your world” *before* “and for Your people, Yisrael, You performed a great deliverance and redemption unto this very day.”\[^205] One would have expected the order of these phrases to be reversed — *first* mention the salvation of Klal Yisrael and *then* the *kiddush Hashem.* The actual order reflects the fact that *kiddush Hashem* is more significant than our salvation. This is because it is the mission we exist to accomplish. It is the reason for and purpose of our survival.\[^206]

   **Our Eternality**  
This is also how the *medrash* explains why the Jewish people are eternal. Based on *another pasuk* in Sefer Yeshayahu,\[^207] the *medrash* teaches that just as Hashem is eternal, so are the Jewish people, who proclaim His glory and sing His praises.\[^208]

   Yechezkel HaNavi builds on this idea regarding the Jewish people’s past and future. In *Perek* 20, he describes how, even though the Jews were not worthy of redemption, Hashem took them out of Mitzrayim anyway in order to avoid a *chilul Hashem.*\[^209] Similarly, even when the Jews continued rebelling against Him in the desert, Hashem maintained a relationship with them — again, to avoid a *chilul Hashem*.\[^210]

   In *perek* 36, Yechezkel Hanavi speaks about the great *chilul Hashem* generated by the Jewish people’s presence in *galus.* The central goal of the eventual redemption is to repair this *chilul Hashem* with a bigger *kiddush Hashem*.\[^211] This is why Shemoneh Esrei’s first *berachah* describes Hashem’s deliverance of the Jewish people as “for the sake of his name.”

   **Our Unity**  
Our definition and mission as the people created to sing Hashem’s praises is meant to unify\[^212] all Jews.\[^213] Amos described the Jewish people as “Hashem’s group (that he founded) on Earth.”\[^214] We should use our common mission to foster unity by emphasizing our joint role as members of Hashem’s “team.”

   \[clt]We should use our common mission to foster unity by emphasizing our joint role as members of Hashem’s “team.”

   This *achdus* is not just an ideal; it is a mitzvah. Chazal\[^215] employ Amos’s words as the philosophical (and textual) basis of the prohibition against our dividing into separate groups. Our view of ourselves as Hashem’s people should keep us from subdividing.

   This is the logic behind the famous comparison of a divided Jewish people to an individual drilling a hole under *his* seat on a boat he shares with others.\[^216] We, the Jewish people, are “all in the same boat” because we have a shared mission of highlighting Hashem’s glory in our world. We are meant to work on this mission together, unified as one.

   Ideally, this mission should unify not only Jews but also *humanity*. Based on our *mishnah*, the Ramchal posits that when each part of G-d’s world properly fulfills its purpose and mission, it connects with the rest of the universe as one entity unified upon focusing on, appreciating, and celebrating G-d’s presence in our world.\[^217]

   The Jewish people are meant to inspire all of humanity to recognize this common goal. Tzefanyah\[^218] speaks about how Hashem will eventually bring the whole world to “clearly invoke Hashem’s name and serve Him together.” Tzefanyah’s language calls to mind the Torah’s description of the builders of the tower of Bavel. They, too, were unified, but their goal was glorifying their *own* name. Eventually, the world will unify around celebrating *Hashem’s* name.

   This is the vision we describe in the Rosh Hashanah and Yom Kippur *tefilos*. We express our yearning for the day when all of humanity “will form a single band to do Your (Hashem’s) Will with a perfect heart.”

   May we, the Jewish people, use our shared purpose to unify ourselves, and subsequently all humanity, around the mission of proclaiming G-d’s glory in the world — together!  
**Why We Do**

   אַנְטִיגְנוֹס אִישׁ סוֹכוֹ קִבֵּל מִשִּׁמְעוֹן הַצַּדִּיק.  
הוּא הָיָה אוֹמֵר, אַל תִּהְיוּ כַעֲבָדִים הַמְשַׁמְּשִׁין אֶת הָרַב עַל מְנָת לְקַבֵּל פְּרָס,  
אֶלָּא הֱווּ כַעֲבָדִים הַמְשַׁמְּשִׁין אֶת הָרַב שֶׁלֹּא עַל מְנָת לְקַבֵּל פְּרָס, וִיהִי מוֹרָא שָׁמַיִם עֲלֵיכֶם (אבות א:ג)

   רַבִּי יוֹסֵי אוֹמֵר… כָל מַעֲשֶׂיךָ יִהְיוּ לְשֵׁם שָׁמָיִם (אבות ב:יב)

   **Important Intent**  
The past two chapters presented Avos’s definition of the world’s goal as glorifying Hashem. This goal should impact not only *what* we do but also *why* we do it.

   Chazal emphasized the importance of *kavanah* (intention) in many places. According to the Ramban,\[^219] this is the message of the command (found in *k’rias Shema)* to “serve Hashem with all of our heart.”\[^220]

   **While Fulfilling Mitzvos**  
The Rambam\[^221] similarly asserts that *mitzvos* we do with proper intentions are what gain us entry into the next world. It is not only about *what* we do but also about *why* we do it.\[^222]

   Rav Kook\[^223] used the importance of *kavanah* to explain the *gemara*’s\[^224] justification for why Rav Hamnuna devoted significant time to *tefilla* despite it being less important than Torah learning (“*chayei olam*” vs. “*chayei sha’ah*”). Since everything we do with the correct *kavanah* contributes to *kevod* *shamayim* (the glory of G-d), we should appreciate and fully focus upon the *mitzvah* we are performing without considering other (even more rewarding) opportunities.

   The centrality of intention makes it the great equalizer. Though we differ in what we can invest and accomplish, we are all equally able to have the right *kavanah*. The rabbis of Yavneh used this to encourage respect for the simple farmers who spent their days in the fields instead of the Beis Midrash. Though they learned less Torah, the farmers deserved respect as their actions were of equally pure intent.\[^225]  
The *Amora* Rava made many statements about the importance of *kavanah*.
