He taught that “it would be preferable for one who fulfills (*mitzvos*) *shelo lishma* (without proper intention) not to have been created!”\[^226] People who serve Hashem with ulterior motives take what is meant to focus on Hashem and make it about themselves. As this diametrically opposes the goal of creation, it would be better for such a person not to exist.

   Rava also saw our intentions as determining whether Torah learning heals or (even) harms. He asserted that “anyone who engages in Torah *lishmah* (for its sake), his Torah study will be an elixir of life for him…And anyone who engages in Torah *shelo lishmah*, his Torah will be an elixir of death for him.”\[^227] Our mere intention can be the difference between life and death.

   **While Committing Aveiros**  
Rava took this idea further by seeing proper positive *kavanah* as significant even when accompanying an *aveirah*. He explains that the *pasuk* “Know him in all your ways, and He will direct your paths”\[^228] refers even to sins!\[^229] Even when a person is involved in something inappropriate, his noble intentions keep him connected (at least on some level) to Hashem.\[^230] Obviously, we should always strive to do the right thing, but even when we fail, our connection with Hashem continues as long as we stay focused on Him.

   The Rambam\[^231] expresses a similar idea when explaining the *mishnah’s* interpretation of the words *b’chol levavcha* as commanding us to serve Hashem with *both* our *yeitzarim* (the *yetzer tov* and *yetzer hara)*.\[^232] How are we meant to serve Hashem with our *yetzer hara*? The Rambam explains that even when sinning, even when following his *yetzer hara*, he should remember his relationship with Hashem. We should maintain our connection with Hashem at all times; even when our actions do not meet His expectations, our relationship can be sustained through proper intention and focus.

   Rav Nachman bar Yitzchak took the significance of our *kavanah* even further when he said that “an *aveirah* committed *lishmah* is greater than a mitzvah performed *shelo lishmah*.”\[^233] Not only are positive intentions (even when accompanying sin) valuable, they can even be more valuable than the rote performance of a *mitzvah*!

   Sinful intentions are also significant. The *gemara*\[^234] tells how Rav Chiya bar Ashi did lifelong *teshuvah* for a sin he had *intended* to commit, even though he did not actually commit it. Though he did not actually sin, he felt that his (mere) inappropriate *kavanah* required *teshuvah*.

   **Right Intent**  
*Kavanah’s* significance makes identifying its correct form of the utmost importance. What motive should we ideally have when performing *mitzvos*?

   The *mishnah* at the beginning of Avos’s first *perek* warns us of the type of intention to *avoid*. Antignus Ish Socho taught: “Do not be like *avadim* (servants) who serve the master on the condition that they receive a reward. Rather, be like *avadim* who serve the master without the condition of receiving a reward, and let the fear of *Shamayim* be upon you.”\[^235] Fear of heaven should dispel selfish ulterior motives.

   In addition to deterring ulterior motives (the *sur mei’ra* perspective), the second *perek* of Avos teaches us the right *kavanah*: “V’chol *ma’asecha y’heyu l’shem shamayim* (Let all your actions be for Heaven’s sake).\[^236] The world was created for the glory of G-d; similar intention should drive our actions.

   Obviously, acting for Heaven’s sake precludes aiming for personal reward, but what exactly does it require?

   On the most basic level, “*l’shem Shamayim*” means seeking to fulfill Hashem’s Will, doing *mitzvos* because He commanded us to.\[^237] Such performance is the most basic connotation of *Avodas Hashem*. We serve the G-d who redeemed us from slavery in Mitzrayim the same way a slave does his master.\[^238] We reinforce this intention by reciting the *berachah* of “*asher kidshanu b’mitzvosav v’tzivanu*” before performing (most) *mitzvos*. We perform *mitzvos* in order to fulfill Hashem’s command.

   On a deeper level, *l’shem Shamayim* means identifying with not just our religious obligations but also *Hashem’s interests* in this world. The Mesilas Yesharim\[^239] definition of the intention we should have while *davening* reflects this deeper level. He explains that we should pray for redemption not in order to end our painful exile but, rather, to increase Hashem’s glory in our world.

   Ideally, *mitzvah* observance should have a similar goal. We should follow Hashem’s Will not just to fulfill our obligations but because we care about what Hashem wants.\[^240] In the words of Rav Yochanan,\[^241] we are fortunate growing in Torah because we know that “it gives *nachas ruach* to Hashem.” We should learn Torah, *daven*, and fulfill *mitzvos* because they are what please Hashem.

   Contemporary society emphasizes personal fulfillment. This has also impacted religious perspectives and caused many to see religion as a means of personal fulfillment. We should strive to focus our religious observance on serving Hashem, not realizing our self-interests.\[^242] In the words of the Kotzker Rebbe, “We need to ensure that what we perform “*l’shem Shamayim”* is *actually l’shem Shamayim*.”

   ***Kol*** **Ma’aseinu**  
The *mishnah* could have conveyed its message by saying, “*Ma’asecha y’heyu l’shem shamayim.*” What does the word “*kol*” add?

   Rabbeinu Yonah explains that the term “*kol”* extends the *mishnah*’s message beyond *mitzvos* to include even *devarim shel reshus* (voluntary deeds). Even things (we generally view as) beyond the purview of *Avodas Hashem* should be done *l’shem shamayim*.

   Indeed, Pirkei Avos emphasizes the importance of action for the sake of heaven in many additional contexts. Our gatherings,\[^243] community work,\[^244] and even arguments\[^245] should all ideally be for Heaven's sake.

   \[clt]Our meetings, community work, and even arguments should all ideally be for Heaven's sake.

   The Rambam\[^246] develops this idea and connects it to the word “*da’eihu* (know Him)” used by the aforementioned *pasuk* in Mishlei. *Everything* should be done to increase our knowledge of Hashem and grow closer to Him.  We should connect our work, eating and drinking, marital relations, and other mundane actions to the higher purposes of knowing G-d and glorifying His Name.

   **Trickling Down**  
It is crucial to ensure that our understanding of the world’s purpose and our existence trickles down to our daily lives, to the intention that accompanies the minutiae of our lives. Every aspect of our lives, not just *mitzvah* performance, should be directed *l’shem Shamayim*, motivated by the goal of enhancing *kevod Shamayim*.  
**What We Want**

   הוּא הָיָה אוֹמֵר, עֲשֵׂה רְצוֹנוֹ כִּרְצוֹנֶךָ, כְּדֵי שֶׁיַּעֲשֶׂה רְצוֹנְךָ כִּרְצוֹנוֹ. בַּטֵּל רְצוֹנְךָ מִפְּנֵי רְצוֹנוֹ, כְּדֵי שֶׁיְּבַטֵּל רְצוֹן אֲחֵרִים מִפְּנֵי רְצוֹנֶךָ. (אבות ב:ד)

   The last chapter addressed the importance of our intentions. The *mishnah* at the beginning of Avos’s second *perek* relates to another aspect of our psyche — our will.

   Rabban Gamliel, the son of Rebbe Yehuda Hanasi, encourages us to “make His will like your own so that He will make yours like His” and to “nullify your will before His so that He will nullify the will of others before yours” (Avos 2:4).

   **Identification**  
We should not only nullify our will before Hashem’s but also *transform* it to match His. Instead of (just) submitting to Hashem’s will, we should *identify* with it. In addition to fulfilling Hashem’s will, we should want what He “wants.” We should aim to value what He “values” and feel what He “feels.”\[^247]

   We learn this idea from Pinchas, whom Hashem described as “*kano et kinati.*”\[^248] Pinchas did more than just take action in response to sin. His reaction was more profound — it was visceral. He empathized with Hashem’s “pain” and “jealousy.”

   Rav Yechezkel Weinfeld *Shlit”a* uses this idea to explain Rav Shimon Bar Yochai’s surprising interpretation of the *pasuk* (in the second *parshah* of *k’rias Shema*) that mentions working the fields.\[^249] Rav Shimon Bar Yochai encouraged devoting all day to Torah learning and relying on Hashem to provide for us. He explained the *pasuk’s* mention of working the fields as referring (only) to a time when people are not “*oskim b’retzono shel Makom* (involved in the will of G-d).”

   Tosafos\[^250] points out that the *perek* within which the *pasuk* appears begins by describing people fulfilling *mitzvos* and continues with Hashem’s reward for this fulfillment. How could this *parshah* be understood as referring to people *not oskim b’retzono shel Makom?*

   Tosafos explains that although the *parshah* relates to people who fulfill Hashem’s Will, they are not entirely “*osin retzono”*. What does this mean? What are they lacking?

   Rav Weinfeld explains that the *parshah* refers to people who fulfill the commandments and *act* in line with Hashem’s Will but have not yet synched their will with Hashem’s. They are not yet “*osin retzono shel Makom*.” Their incomplete reward mirrors their incomplete *avodah*. Hashem rewards their *mitzvah* fulfillment by supporting their work in the field, but they still need to work.

   **The Form — Passionate Heart and Soul**  
Identification with Hashem’s Will affects both the form and scope of our *Avodas Hashem*.

   It impacts the form by enabling us to serve Hashem with a full heart. The Ramban\[^251] saw this level of *avodah* as the intention of the command to serve Hashem “with *all* your heart.”\[^252] When fulfilling *mitzvos*, we should focus fully on *Avodas Hashem* and act wholeheartedly.

   This wholeheartedness should express itself as passion. Ramban explains that this is why the Torah emphasizes how quickly Avraham served his guests. The Torah wants us to appreciate Avraham’s passion for *chesed*.\[^253] He did not just *do* it; he *ran* to do it.

   We see the relationship between “running” and “will” through the similarity between the Hebrew word for will — *ratzon,* and the abbreviated version we use to describe running — *ratz.* We run to what we are passionate about. Rav Yehuda Ben Teima links the two in his encouraging “running (*ratz*) like a gazelle to fulfill the Will (*ratzon*) of (y)our Father in Heaven.”\[^254]

   \[clt]When fulfilling *mitzvos*, we should focus fully on our *Avodas Hashem* and act wholeheartedly.

   **Scope — What Hashem Wants**  
Aligning our will with Hashem’s means that we seek to fulfill not just commandments but *everything* Hashem “wants.” We should commit ourselves to what He stated explicitly as well as what we infer “between the lines.”\[^255]

   The Mesillas Yesharim\[^256] explains that we should relate to Hashem the way we relate to those we love and care deeply for. When we care about someone, we care about not only their formal requests but whatever will make them happy.

   Avraham’s actions at the *Akeidah* demonstrated this attitude. After Hashem told him to spare Yitzchak’s life, he searched for something else to sacrifice.\[^257] Avraham could have headed home satisfied with Hashem’s praise for his willingness to sacrifice Yitzchak. But he thought Hashem wanted him to go further and sacrifice something. Even though he was *not commanded* to sacrifice, Avraham *sought* the opportunity.\[^258] Interestingly, Hashem blessed Avraham *only after* he offered this voluntary sacrifice.\[^259] The *berachos* were given to Avraham because he was not just a loyal servant but also one who loved Hashem.\[^260]

   Mordechai’s words to Esther in response to her hesitance to approach Achashverosh on behalf of the Jewish people are another example of pursuing Hashem’s unspoken Will. Mordechai told Esther that interceding with Achashveirosh may be “the moment for which you became queen.”\[^261] Neither Esther nor Mordechai were commanded by Hashem to take action. Like Avraham, Mordechai reflected on the circumstances and estimated what Hashem wanted. This estimation was something he felt Esther was responsible to risk her life for.

   The Chasam Sofer\[^262] used this idea to explain the surprising statement of the *gemara*\[^263] that “one who says that they have only Torah, lacks *even Torah*.” What besides Torah does having Torah depend upon?

   The Chasam Sofer explains that Torah, as eternal and universal, can mandate only the generic responsibilities all Jews have at all times. Each of us has additional responsibilities we are meant to determine from the unique abilities Hashem creates us with and the circumstances He puts us in. Those committed to Hashem’s Will seek to determine and commit themselves to these additional aspects of what Hashem wants from us. Only those who do this *truly* have Torah.

   **May It Be Our Will**  
May we successfully sync our will with Hashem’s in a way that brings us to identify with His goals and inspires us to serve him fully and comprehensively!  
\*\*Our Perspective on Our Lives  \*\*

   Life In the Corridor

   Food For Thought

   Aware of Awareness

   Our Life in Perspective

   The Danger of Overconfidence

   In Our Hands

   Taking Full Advantage  
**Life in the Corridor**

   רַבִּי יַעֲקֹב אוֹמֵר, הָעוֹלָם הַזֶּה דּוֹמֶה לִפְרוֹזְדוֹר בִּפְנֵי הָעוֹלָם הַבָּא. הַתְקֵן עַצְמְךָ בַפְּרוֹזְדוֹר, כְּדֵי שֶׁתִּכָּנֵס לַטְּרַקְלִין:  
הוּא הָיָה אוֹמֵר, יָפָה שָׁעָה אַחַת בִּתְשׁוּבָה וּמַעֲשִׂים טוֹבִים בָּעוֹלָם הַזֶּה, מִכָּל חַיֵּי הַעוֹלָם הַבָּא.  
וְיָפָה שָׁעָה אַחַת שֶׁל קוֹרַת רוּחַ בָּעוֹלָם הַבָּא, מִכָּל חַיֵּי הָעוֹלָם הַזֶּה (אבות ד:טז-יז)

   At the end of Avos’s fourth perek, Rebbe Yaakov makes two statements about the relationship between this world and the next. The two statements are connected and together define the focus and goal of life in this world.

   **A Mere Corridor**  
Rebbe Yaakov begins by comparing this world to a corridor that leads to a palace —  *Olam Haba* (the next world).\[^264] *Olam Hazeh* (this world) is not the end goal. There is a bigger, better, nicer, and more meaningful place — the “palace” — we reach after passing through this world, which is actually a mere corridor. We are, of course, familiar with this world, drawn to its pleasures, and focused on its reality and challenges. Rebbe Yaakov teaches that this world is essentially just the “promo,” just the hallway.

   Mesilas Yesharim begins by explaining (based on our *mishnah*) that we need to realize that we were not created (mainly) for this world.\[^265] Our souls ultimately seek things more meaningful than what this world has to offer; we should ensure not to get lured astray by the non-spiritual aspects of our world.

   \[clt]Our souls ultimately seek things more meaningful than what this world has to offer.

   **Ultimate Reward**  
This concept connects to two other statements of Rebbe Yaakov. The first is his assertion that Hashem rewards *mitzvah* observance only in the next world, not in this one.\[^266] Understandably, the reward is given in the palace, not the corridor.

   Rebbe Yaakov also expresses this idea in the second *mishnah* (above), where he teaches that “one hour of tranquility in the World to Come is more precious than all of life in this world.”\[^267] Unlike in this world, where one can never be completely happy, the next world offers us true peace, satisfaction, and tranquility. Reward is given only in the next world because one moment of peace and calm there is more significant than a whole life of this world’s pleasures. Knowing and appreciating this should help us avoid pursuing the pleasures of this world. Doing so would be selling ourselves terribly short.\[^268]

   **The Way In**  
All this does not mean there is no importance to this world, to the hallway. Though not the ultimate end, life in the hallway is significant, and our time here needs to be maximized. We see this from the first part of the (second) *mishnah*, where Rebbe Yaakov asserts that “one hour of repentance and good deeds in this world are more precious than all of the World to Come.” Though the *next* world is the world of reward and satisfaction, we earn this reward and satisfaction through our actions and personal growth in *this* world.\[^269] Though only a corridor, it is where we prepare to enter and earn our place in the palace.\[^270]

   Koheles Rabbah adds that “the next world is earned *only* by what we do in this world.”\[^271] Like passing through a corridor that is the *sole* entry to the palace, preparation in this world is the *only* way to gain access to the next one. Our efforts in this world are not just *one* way forward; they are the *only* way to earn and enhance our portion in the next world.\[^272]

   Rebbe Yaakov emphasizes the need to take advantage of life in this world in the first *mishnah* as well when he urges, “Prepare yourself in the corridor, so that you may enter the palace.”\[^273] The right to enter the palace of *Olam Haba* is earned through our efforts in *Olam Hazeh*.

   The GRA expressed this idea with his last words while crying on his deathbed.\[^274] His talmidim asked him why he was crying. Was he afraid of facing his Maker? What did he think he would be punished for? The GRA responded that he was crying because he would soon be unable to perform *mitzvos* and earn reward. The GRA appreciated the unique opportunity life in this world offers. Only here can we perform *mitzvos* and enhance our eternal life in the palace of the world to come.\[^275]

   \[clt]Only here can we perform *mitzvos* and improve our eternal life in the palace of the world to come.

   Rebbe Yaakov’s teachings aim to help us develop the proper perspective on our world. We should realize that it and its pleasure are not the ultimate goal. That said, this world is important as the venue through which we earn our place in the next world. In the words of Rav Sa’adyah Gaon, “The *tzaddik* loves this world, not for itself, but only because of the level he reaches through it that he can use to rise from it to *Olam Haba*.”\[^276]

   ***Sha’ah Achas***  
Rebbe Yaakov’s words teach two additional important lessons. We learn the first lesson from his usage of the term “*sha’ah achas* (one moment)*.*” *Each and every moment* has significance and thus needs to be taken advantage of. Rebbe Yehuda Hanasi famously quipped, “Some acquire their share in the next world through one moment (as opposed to others who need many years).”\[^277] Each moment can and should be of great significance and impact.\[^278]

   **Action and Reflection**  
A second lesson is about the primary importance of deed and self-reflection. Instead of Torah and *chochmah*, Rebbe Yaakov focuses on *teshuvah* (repentance) and *ma’asim tovim* (good deeds) as our world’s meaningful content.

   Though Judaism attributes great value to Torah learning and accumulating wisdom (*Talmud Torah k’neged kulam*), actions are more important. As Rebbe Shimon the son of Rabban Gamliel taught in the first perek of Avos: “*Lo hamidrash hu ha’ikar, ela hama’aseh.”* (Avos 1:17)

   In fact, *ma’asim* are learning’s ultimate aim. Later in Avos,\[^279] Rabbi Yishmael ben Berokah defines ideal learning as that done in order to facilitate action. Ultimately, it is the *ma’asim* our learning inspires that make our learning of supreme importance. In the *gemara*’s words, “Learning is great(est) because it facilitates *ma’aseh*.”\[^280]

   Rava emphasized the importance of both *teshuvah* and *ma’asim* in asserting that “*teshuvah* and *ma’asim tovim* are the *tachlis* (goal) of *chochmah*.”\[^281] Torah study and the pursuit of *chochmah* are a central focus of our time and energy because they help us build our identity and live our lives properly.\[^282]

   **Today and Tomorrow**  
We can summarize Rebbe Yaakov’s teachings using the *gemara* which explains the Torah’s\[^283] emphasis on the need to do *mitzvos today* with five words: “*Hayom la’asosam; machar l’kabel secharam*.”\[^284] Today (this world) is when we work. Tomorrow (the next world) is when we receive reward.

   May we maximize every day of our lives in this world by realizing that we are here to prepare for the next world through reflection and growth!  
**Food For Thought**

   רַבִּי חֲנִינָא בֶן תְּרַדְיוֹן אוֹמֵר, שְׁנַיִם שֶׁיּוֹשְׁבִין וְאֵין בֵּינֵיהֶן דִּבְרֵי תוֹרָה, הֲרֵי זֶה מוֹשַׁב לֵצִים,  
שֶׁנֶּאֱמַר (תהלים א) וּבְמוֹשַׁב לֵצִים לֹא יָשָׁב.  
אֲבָל שְׁנַיִם שֶׁיּוֹשְׁבִין וְיֵשׁ בֵּינֵיהֶם דִּבְרֵי תוֹרָה, שְׁכִינָה שְׁרוּיָה בֵינֵיהֶם, שֶׁנֶּאֱמַר (מלאכי ג) אָז נִדְבְּרוּ יִרְאֵי ה' אִישׁ אֶל רֵעֵהוּ וַיַּקְשֵׁב ה' וַיִּשְׁמָע וַיִּכָּתֵב סֵפֶר זִכָּרוֹן לְפָנָיו לְיִרְאֵי ה' וּלְחֹשְׁבֵי שְׁמוֹ.  
אֵין לִי אֶלָּא שְׁנַיִם, מִנַּיִן שֶׁאֲפִלּוּ אֶחָד שֶׁיּוֹשֵׁב וְעוֹסֵק בַּתּוֹרָה, שֶׁהַקָּדוֹשׁ בָּרוּךְ הוּא קוֹבֵעַ לוֹ שָׂכָר,  
שֶׁנֶּאֱמַר (איכה ג) יֵשֵׁב בָּדָד וְיִדֹּם כִּי נָטַל עָלָיו: (אבות ג:ב)

   רַבִּי שִׁמְעוֹן אוֹמֵר, שְׁלשָׁה שֶׁאָכְלוּ עַל שֻׁלְחָן אֶחָד וְלֹא אָמְרוּ עָלָיו דִּבְרֵי תוֹרָה, כְּאִלּוּ אָכְלוּ מִזִּבְחֵי מֵתִים,  
שֶׁנֶּאֱמַר (ישעיה כח) כִּי כָּל שֻׁלְחָנוֹת מָלְאוּ קִיא צֹאָה בְּלִי מָקוֹם.  
אֲבָל שְׁלשָׁה שֶׁאָכְלוּ עַל שֻׁלְחָן אֶחָד וְאָמְרוּ עָלָיו דִּבְרֵי תוֹרָה, כְּאִלּוּ אָכְלוּ מִשֻּׁלְחָנוֹ שֶׁל מָקוֹם בָּרוּךְ הוּא,  
שֶׁנֶּאֱמַר (יחזקאל מא) וַיְדַבֵּר אֵלַי זֶה הַשֻּׁלְחָן אֲשֶׁר לִפְנֵי ה': (אבות ג:ג)

   **Sitting and Eating**  
The second *mishnah* of Avos’s third *perek* quotes Rebbe Chanina ben Teradyon’s statement about the importance of sharing Torah with those we sit with. The very next *mishnah* quotes Rebbe Shimon who emphasizes the need to share Torah with those we eat with. What is the relationship between these two statements? Does sharing Torah at a meal have additional significance?\[^285]

   **What We Are Sustaining**  
The Chasid Yavetz explains that sharing Torah at meals is uniquely important because it defines and expresses how we view our lives. Man, like animal, needs to eat to sustain his life. That having been said, our existence means more than that of animals. We need to express this distinction when we eat, like animals, to sustain our lives; we do so by sharing Torah with those we eat with.

   The *Netziv* sees the source of the difference between the lives of man and animal in how Bereishis describes each brought to life. As opposed to animals, which Bereishis defines as “alive” as soon as they were created, Adam HaRishon is depicted as “alive” only after Hashem added his soul.\[^286]   Because man’s life is meant to be more spiritual than an animal’s, Bereishis describes him as alive only once Hashem added his soul.

   In his commentary to Sefer Devarim,\[^287] the *Netziv* uses this idea to explain why the Torah links our lives to *mitzvah* observance. A person is only described as "living" when he realizes his ultimate purpose. The more a person fulfills *mitzvos*, the more alive he is.

   *Onkelos* makes this point in a subtle but powerful way. The Torah writes that man lives not off bread, but, rather, through G-d’s word.\[^288] Whereas the Torah uses the same word (“*yichyeh*”) to describe man’s survival off both food and the word of G-d, Onkelos uses a different word to translate the latter — “*chayei*” as opposed to “*miskayem*.”\[^289] Though we can *survive* off bread, we only truly *live* when we involve G-d in our lives.

   This also explains Rebbe Akiva’s famous comparison of a Jew’s need for Torah to a fish’s dependency upon water.\[^290] A person can survive physically without Torah, but his true life is lost. This is why *resha’im*, even when alive, are considered dead: their lives mean nothing more than their physical existence.\[^291]

   **Food For Thought**  
Lacking this perspective can allow the pursuit of food and survival to cloud our judgment. When tired and hungry, we are anxious to revive and sustain ourselves with food. This anxiety can cause us to lose sight of our life’s bigger picture and purpose and eat like animals rather than humans.

   Eisav is an excellent example of this phenomenon. The Torah tells us that Eisav denigrated the *bechorah* (firstborn rights) to which he was naturally entitled, trading them for a bowl of lentils. Why did he make the trade? The Torah tells us that he returned home tired and (it seems) hungry. In that state, he “reasoned” that, as he would die in any case, he had no need for the *bechorah.*\[^292] His hunger caused Eisav to ignore the value of anything more than sustaining his physical life.

   Our lives matter because we use them to pursue valuable goals. We emulate our ancestor, Yaakov, who worked to acquire *bechor* rights for himself and his descendants. The Kuzari uses this idea to explain the purpose of the *berachos* we recite before and after eating.\[^293] The *berachos* express our appreciation of the special significance of sustaining our lives, as opposed to those of animals. Both man and animal eat to maintain our lives, but our lives mean more. We express and reinforce this distinction by thanking Hashem for our food.

   \[clt]Both man and animal eat to maintain our lives, but our lives mean more. We express and reinforce this distinction by thanking Hashem for our food.

   In our *mishnah*, Rebbe Shimon teaches us that we should express this appreciation not only by thanking Hashem for our food but also by putting the most meaningful content of our lives — Torah learning — on the table. By sharing Torah at meals, we express our belief that the lives we sustain matter because of the Torah we learn and the *mitzvos* we fulfill.

   **We Are *Why* We Eat**  
Rebbe Shimon adds that those who do not share Torah at meals are considered to be eating from “*zivchei meisim.*”\[^294] The Chasid Yavetz explains this term as referring not to the state of the food (which is, of course, dead) but to the status of the consumer. Because one who does not include Torah as part of his meal is not alive, he is considered to be eating a meal that belongs to the dead. Though the food he eats sustains his physical life, his lack of emphasis on his life’s meaning causes us to regard him as dead.

   **Elevating Our Meals**  
Rebbe Shimon adds that, in contrast, one who does share Torah at a meal is considered to be eating from G-d’s table. A Torah context elevates a meal to the point where we are considered to be eating from (no less) than Hashem’s table.

   Rav Yosef Dov Soloveitchik expressed a similar idea:\[^295] “Transforming an animal need into an act of worship is a uniquely Jewish idea. The table is referred to in our tradition as an altar, and meals which are associated with religious observances are called *se’udos mitzvah*.”

   Rav Soloveichik spoke of a *seudas mitzvah* — a meal associated with *mitzvah*. Rebbe Shimon teaches us that *even regular* meals eaten as part of our daily lives can be elevated — by sharing Torah at them.

   We live in a world that sees food, along with sexuality, as ends unto themselves instead of as mere means of survival. This expresses and reinforces a lack of appreciation of life’s more profound meaning and purpose.

   Let’s make sure to share Torah over meals in order to define our lives properly. May doing so give us access to Hashem’s table!  
**Aware of Awareness**

   רַבִּי אוֹמֵר… וְהִסְתַּכֵּל בִּשְׁלשָׁה דְבָרִים וְאִי אַתָּה בָא לִידֵי עֲבֵרָה  
דַּע מַה לְּמַעְלָה מִמְּךָ, עַיִן רוֹאָה וְאֹזֶן שׁוֹמַעַת, וְכָל מַעֲשֶׂיךָ בַסֵּפֶר נִכְתָּבִין: (אבות ב:א)

   עֲקַבְיָא בֶן מַהֲלַלְאֵל אוֹמֵר, הִסְתַּכֵּל בִּשְׁלשָׁה דְבָרִים וְאֵין אַתָּה בָא לִידֵי עֲבֵרָה.  
דַּע, מֵאַיִן בָּאתָ, וּלְאָן אַתָּה הוֹלֵךְ, וְלִפְנֵי מִי אַתָּה עָתִיד לִתֵּן דִּין וְחֶשְׁבּוֹן.  
מֵאַיִן בָּאתָ, מִטִּפָּה סְרוּחָה, וּלְאָן אַתָּה הוֹלֵךְ, לִמְקוֹם עָפָר רִמָּה וְתוֹלֵעָה. וְלִפְנֵי מִי אַתָּה עָתִיד לִתֵּן דִּין וְחֶשְׁבּוֹן, לִפְנֵי מֶלֶךְ מַלְכֵי הַמְּלָכִים הַקָּדוֹשׁ בָּרוּךְ הוּא (אבות ג:א).

   **Consciousness**  
In the previous section, we studied Pirkei Avos’s delineation of the proper view of life. Though we know how we are meant to view life, maintaining this view in a way that consistently guides our lives is challenging.

   Rav Ovadia MiBartenura points out that most sins do not emanate from pure desire. They are rooted in our loss of perspective.\[^296] Having correct values and goals is not enough; we need to be conscious of and driven by them.\[^297]

   \[clt]Most sins do not emanate from pure desire. They are rooted in our loss of perspective.

   Pirkei Avos speaks about how to accomplish this as well. Two of Avos’s *perakim* begin with advice on how to generate the consciousness that helps avoid sin. The third *perek* opens by quoting Akavya ben Mahalalel’s (a *Tanna* who lived in the first *tannaitic* generation) guidance. The second *perek* begins with advice formulated similarly by Rebbe (Rebbe Yehudah HaNasi  — a *Tanna* who lived at the end of the *tannaitic* period).\[^298]

   Both *Tannaim* advise us to focus on three things,\[^299] but differ on the type of three they recommend. This chapter focuses on Rebbe’s three.

   **What’s Above**  
Rebbe encourages reflection upon Hashem’s presence and omniscience: “Look at three things, and you will not come to sin. Know what is above you: a seeing eye, a listening ear, and a recording of all your actions.”\[^300]

   Earlier sources mention this type of reflection, as well. The Torah\[^301] explains that *tzitzis* help us avoid sin by reminding us of Hashem and His *mitzvos*.\[^302] Dovid Hamelech went beyond and constantly imagined himself in Hashem’s presence.\[^303]

   Rebbe, however, takes the idea further by encouraging us to focus on Hashem’s *Omniscience*.\[^304] Rebbe explains that reflection upon Hashem’s awareness of our actions keeps us “far away” from sin.\[^305]

   **The Seeing Eye**  
Rebbe specifies three aspects of Hashem’s omniscience.

   He begins with “the seeing eye” — that Hashem sees everything in our world.\[^306] Many studies indicate that people are more hesitant to do the wrong thing when they know that others are watching — or even if they see a picture of an eye before them.

   Students were asked to participate in the “Dictator Game,” in which people are given money and the opportunity to share any or none of it with an anonymous stranger. Beforehand, and without realizing that it was part of the experiment, some students were briefly shown a pair of eyes as a computer screen saver (while others saw a different image). Those exposed to the eyes gave 55 percent more to the stranger than the others.

   In another study, researchers placed a coffee maker in a university hallway. Passersby could take coffee and leave money in the box. On some weeks, a poster with watchful eyes was hanging on the wall nearby; on others, a picture of flowers. On the weeks where the eyes were displayed, people left on average 2.76 times as much money as at other times!\[^307] Ara Norenzayan, author of the book *Big Gods*, which quotes these studies, concludes that “watched people are nice people.”\[^308]

   Obviously, our awareness of the constant presence of *Hashem*’s “eyes” should have an even *stronger* impact. This makes religion a force for honest and altruistic behavior and *mitzvah* observance; we believe that Hashem sees what we do.

   Rav Uri Zohar, a famous Israeli *ba’al teshuvah* who inspired many others to follow his path, appreciated this idea. A car once pulled up next to his car at a traffic light. The driver rolled down the window and asked Rav Zohar to convince him to do *teshuvah* before the light turned from red to green.

   Rav Zohar replied: “Why do you stop at these lights? Why don’t you just go through the red light? There is nobody here!” The man retorted: “I’m afraid that maybe a policeman will suddenly emerge and perhaps my license will be revoked, or a camera will see me and report my actions, send a letter to my house, and fine me!”

   Rav Zohar replied: “You should know there is a camera focused upon you at all times. You think the world is empty, but there is an eye above that always watches!” The driver was inspired to do *teshuvah*.

   It is no coincidence that, as belief in a personal G-d has waned in the West, surveillance by cameras,  CCTV, and other means has had to be increased.

   **The Hearing Ear**  
Rebbe’s next image — the “hearing ear” — expands Hashem’s omniscience. In addition to seeing our actions, He also hears our words.

   Many *meforshim* interpret Hashem’s “eye” and “ear” as His ability to also know our thoughts. As Hashem told Shmuel Hanavi, “Man sees only (up to) the eyes, while Hashem sees to the heart.”\[^309] The fact that Hashem also knows our thoughts should serve as a significant deterrent.

   **Running Record**  
Rebbe adds a third component: “Our actions are recorded for posterity.” Our actions are remembered and have long-term significance.

   We are not always careful about our actions because we see them as lacking importance or consequence. The *medrash*\[^310] tells us that had Reuven known that his saving Yosef would be recorded in the Torah, he would have put him on his shoulders and taken him back to Yaakov. Similarly, if Boaz had known that Nach would record how he cared for Rut, he would have offered her a four-course meal.

   We should recognize that all of our actions, even those that seem insignificant to us, are recorded and of great significance.\[^311]

   **Rebbe’s Mishnah In the Age of Technology**  
The Chofetz Chaim used this *mishnah* to explain the technological developments of the early 20th century. He commented on the invention of the phonograph: “Earlier generations more readily believed that G-d sees, hears, and records our actions. Unfortunately, in our generation, people have less faith. Therefore, Hashem facilitated the creation of the phonograph so people could believe that He records our actions and voices.”

   One wonders how the Chofetz Chaim would view the explosion of recording technology in the hundred years since then. Maybe we need to know that each and every move we make is observed, heard, and recorded by cameras and satellites in order to help us believe that Hashem is doing the same thing.

   May our recognition of Hashem’s all-encompassing awareness (which shares similarities with the FBI’s but far surpasses it!) keep us far from sin and inspire us to live our lives properly!  
**Our Life in Perspective**

   עֲקַבְיָא בֶן מַהֲלַלְאֵל אוֹמֵר, הִסְתַּכֵּל בִּשְׁלשָׁה דְבָרִים וְאֵין אַתָּה בָא לִידֵי עֲבֵרָה.  
דַּע, מֵאַיִן בָּאתָ, וּלְאָן אַתָּה הוֹלֵךְ, וְלִפְנֵי מִי אַתָּה עָתִיד לִתֵּן דִּין וְחֶשְׁבּוֹן.  
מֵאַיִן בָּאתָ, מִטִּפָּה סְרוּחָה, וּלְאָן אַתָּה הוֹלֵךְ, לִמְקוֹם עָפָר רִמָּה וְתוֹלֵעָה. וְלִפְנֵי מִי אַתָּה עָתִיד לִתֵּן דִּין וְחֶשְׁבּוֹן, לִפְנֵי מֶלֶךְ מַלְכֵי הַמְּלָכִים הַקָּדוֹשׁ בָּרוּךְ הוּא (אבות ג:א).

   **An Existential Three**  
As we saw in the last chapter, Avos’s second *perek* begins by quoting Rebbe (Rebbe Yehudah HaNasi), who encourages us to use our cognizance of three aspects of Hashem’s omniscience to keep us far from sin.

   Avos’s *third perek* begins by quoting Akavya ben Mahalel, who directs us to focus on three aspects of another matter— the nature of our existence.**\[^312]**

   “Know from where you come, where you are going, and before whom you will need to give an account and reckoning.”\[^313] Sin can emanate from either high or low self-esteem. Akavya’s teaching helps us avoid both dangers.

   **Our Fleeting Existence**  
People often see themselves as worth more than they actually are. Remembering our lowly origins and eventual demise humbles us.\[^314] Though we know that we emanate from “a putrid drop,” we can become intoxicated with how much we have grown since then. Akavya teaches us that reflection upon our eventual destination —  a place of dust, worms, and maggots — reminds us that our existence and glory are temporary.

   Parshas Bereishis\[^315] teaches us that Hashem made man mortal in order to humble him. Before man ate from the Etz Hada’as, when he recognized his dependency upon Hashem for direction, he was allowed access to the Etz Hachayim (which sustained eternal life). Once he ate from the Etz Hada’as intending to make decisions for himself, Hashem made man mortal by exiling him from Gan Eden (so he could no longer eat from the Etz Hachayim). Mortality was needed to sharpen the gap between man and G-d.

   Once decreed by Hashem, our reflection upon our mortality helps us live properly. Sefer Koheles,\[^316] a book that focuses on the meaning of life in light of the reality of death, identifies cognizance of death as the one thing we know for sure and the *only* advantage the living have over the dead. Only this knowledge makes the living dog better than the dead lion.

   This explains why the *medrash*\[^317] understands the words “*tov me’od* (very good)”\[^318] as referring to death. Death is “very good” because reflection upon it gives us perspective on our lives.\[^319]

   The realization that our life eventually ends should inspire us to think beyond the present and be cautious about how we live. This cognizance is the best deterrent from sin and why Chazal\[^320] encourage reflecting upon it as a failsafe when all other attempts to deter ourselves from sin fail. Reminding ourselves of our eventual demise inspires us to live life properly.\[^321]

   **What We Amount To**  
Lest this reflection cause us to think that our lives do not matter (which can itself lead to sin), Akavya also reminds us of the ultimate reckoning we eventually need to give for our lives.\[^322] Though our existence emanates from and ultimately ends in nothingness, we are expected to maximize this existence as long as possible.\[^323] The final reckoning shows how significant our life is meant to be, as Tehillim teaches: “What is man that You have been mindful of him, mortal man that You have taken note of him?”\[^324] What is man that G-d should take so seriously? Yet, He does.

   Koheles ended his sefer by linking our need to fear Hashem and observe His *mitzvos* to the fact that Hashem judges all His creations.\[^325] The gift of life comes with the expectation that we use it properly. Our knowledge of our inevitable responsibility to account for our lives should inspire us to maximize it fully.

   \[clt]Our knowledge of our inevitable responsibility to account for our lives should inspire us to maximize it fully.

   **3x3**  
The statements of Akavya and Rebbe complement one another. Reflecting on *G-d’s presence* and the meaning of *our existence* both inspire proper perspective. We need awareness of G-d’s Presence and omniscience to ensure *yiras Shamayim* and appreciation of our existence to maximize it.

   May our sustaining consciousness of G-d’s presence and the nature of our existence protect us from sin and inspire us to live our lives properly!

   **The Danger of Overconfidence**

   הִלֵּל אוֹמֵר, אַל תִּפְרֹשׁ מִן הַצִּבּוּר, וְאַל תַּאֲמִין בְּעַצְמְךָ עַד יוֹם מוֹתְךָ… (אבות ב:ה)

   In the second *perek’s* fifth mishnah, Hillel lists five things to avoid. The second one is: “Do not believe in yourself until the day that you die.”\[^326] The Rambam explains that even if a person has successfully strengthened himself spiritually, he should realize that he can still fall.\[^327]

   According to Rabbeinu Yonah,\[^328] Hillel refers to both our ability to avoid sin and to our broader faith and religious identity.

   **Avoiding Sin**  
Rabbeinu Yonah adds that the *yetzer hara* (evil inclination) constantly seeks ways to trip us up, utilizing new strategies at each stage of our lives. This is why Rav Chiyya bar Ashi used to *daven* (even in his old age), “*Harachaman Hu yatzileinu mi’yetzer hara* — May the All-Merciful save us from the evil inclination.” Not understanding why he was still worried at such an old age, his wife decided to test him by propositioning him while disguised as another woman — and Rav Chiya succumbed to his *yetzer hara*.\[^329] She learned how true Hillel’s words are.

   The Meiri\[^330] connects this idea to the mistake made by Shlomo Hamelech, whose overconfidence caused him to ignore the Torah’s prohibition against kings marrying too many women for fear that they might cause him to sin.\[^331] Sadly, Shlomo’s overconfidence was misplaced. His wives swayed him towards idolatry.\[^332]

   Chazal tell us that Shlomo’s father, Dovid, also fell victim to overconfidence. His sin with Batsheva occurred because he challenged G-d to test him.\[^333] Chazal brand a person who chooses an unnecessary *nisayon* (challenging situation) as a *rasha*.\[^334] One who fears sin avoids situations that can lead to it.

   We should also carefully observe Rabbinic safeguards. The great Rebbe Yishmael ben Elisha learned this the hard way. Though the rabbis prohibited reading from an oil lamp on Shabbos, fearing that one might come to absentmindedly tilt the lamp to generate more light, Rav Yishmael was confident that he could read without doing so. He read from an oil lamp and absentmindedly tilted it, causing him to remark, “*Kama gedolim divrei chachamim* — How great are the words of the wise.”\[^335] If that happened to the great Rebbe Yishmael, we obviously need to be even more careful.

   **Faith and Religious Identity**  
Rabbeinu Yonah adds that Hillel’s idea also applies to one’s faith and broader religious identity.

   Even a person who has maintained strong beliefs their entire life should avoid exposure to heresy.\[^336] Rashi\[^337] references the sad case of Yochanan Kohen Gadol, who became a heretic at the end of his life despite having served as Kohen Gadol for eighty (!) years.\[^338] Even a lifetime of success does not make one immune to sin.\[^339]

   **Maximizing Potential**  
It is noteworthy that Hillel mentions neither sin nor faith by name. This opens the door to the broader interpretation offered by Rav Yisrael of Vishnitz,\[^340], who explains the mishnah as referring to *positive* spiritual growth. One should never be complacent and feel that he has achieved *enough*.\[^341]

   Every moment of our lives can and should be used for continued growth. This is why the Torah commands us to respond to the finding of a corpse (whose murderer is unknown) by killing a calf that has never worked in a ravine that was never plowed.\[^342] The lost potential of the calf and ravine should help us appreciate the significance of the potential growth lost by the premature loss of life.\[^343]

   \[clt]Every moment of our lives can and should be used for continued growth.

   Though we should be proud of how we resist temptation and the positive things we accomplish,\[^344] we should not allow overconfidence to cause us to let down our guard or complacency to justify settling for less than what we have the potential to achieve.  
**In Our Hands**

   הַכֹּל צָפוּי, וְהָרְשׁוּת נְתוּנָה נָה, ובְטוֹב הָעוֹלָם נִדּוֹן... (אבות ג:טו)

   Rebbe Akiva believed in man. More correctly, he taught that *G-d* believes in man. This is why He created man in His image and gave him control over himself and the world around him. Rebbe Akiva presents these two points together towards the end of Avos’s third *perek*. In *mishnah* fourteen, Rebbe Akiva spoke about G-d’s creation of man in His image. In *mishnah* fifteen, Rebbe Akiva uses two words — “*hareshus nesunah*” —  to emphasize the choice and control G-d gives man.

   *Mishnah* fifteen begins with two other words that describe Hashem's oversight — *“Ha’kol tzafui* — Everything is seen (by Hashem).” Man controls, and G-d oversees. Though consisting of only four words, this *mishnah* conveys two important *hashkafic* ideas.\[^345] This chapter addresses both of them.

   **Part 1 — Hashem’s Vision**  
The first two words of the mishnah, *“Ha’kol tzafui,*” can be interpreted in two ways — as referring to the present or to the future.

   **The Present**  
Rashi\[^346] understands the phrase as referring to the present. This is how *Sefer Mishlei* uses the term when it portrays Hashem’s “eyes” as “observing (*tzofos*) the bad and the good everywhere.”\[^347]

   The Rambam\[^348] lists this idea as one of our principles of faith. Hashem’s awareness of our deeds is critical for *sechar* (reward) and *onesh* (punishment). Without awareness of our actions, Hashem could not reward or punish us for them.

   According to Rashi’s understanding, this statement of our *mishnah* is similar to that of Rebbe Yehuda Hanasi: “Know what there is above you: an eye that sees, an ear that hears...”\[^349]

   **The Future**  
This redundancy may be (part of) why the Rambam\[^350] understood our *mishnah*’s statement as referring to Hashem’s knowledge of the future as well.

   Yeshayahu\[^351] describes Hashem as “*maggid mei’reishis acharis* — speaking (to prophets) about the end from the beginning.”\[^352] The *medrash* tells us that Hashem foresaw the future actions of both *tzaddikim* and *resha’im* from the beginning of time\*.\*\[^353] Later, He showed Moshe Rabbeinu the lives of future leaders throughout the generations.\[^354]



   **Part 2 — Our Control**

   **Choice**  
This heavenly omniscience makes the *mishnah’s* following two words — “*V*’*ha’reshus nesunah* — surprising. The Rambam[^355](Ibid.) explains that *despite* Hashem seeing the future, He allows us to decide for ourselves. He may know what we will ultimately choose, but the choice is fully our own.\[^356]

   The earliest source of man’s free choice is Hashem’s words to Kayin in Sefer Bereishis, “If you will be good, you will be uplifted; if not, sin crouches at the door.”\[^357] In other words, it’s in your hands; it’s your choice.

   Hashem gave the Jewish people an additional level of control in His words to them in Sefer Shemos. The first *mitzvah* Hashem gave to the Jewish people begins: “*Ha’chodesh ha’zeh lachem rosh chodoshim, rishon hu lachem l’chodshei ha’shanah* — This month is the first of *your* months; it is *for you* the first of the months of the year.” The repetition of the word “*lachem*” highlights the fact that even setting the actual calendar is in our hands.

   In *Sefer Devarim*, Hashem and Moshe extend our choice to all *mitzvos*. In many different *pesukim*\[^358] they offer the choice between observance and non-observance (of all *mitzvos*) to the Jewish people. We have the right and the responsibility to choose.

   \[clt]We have the right and the responsibility to choose.

   The Seforno and Meshech Chochmah\[^359] see free choice as fundamental to our creation in Hashem’s image. Just as Hashem makes His own unhindered choices, so too, we humans live as godly creatures with the same ability.\[^360]

   The Rambam\[^361] elaborates on the importance of the belief in free choice and describes it as an “*ikkar gadol”* and “*amud haTorah v’ha’mitzvah*.” Without free choice, Hashem would not need to encourage us to make the right choices, and our “decisions” would be meaningless and unworthy of *sechar* or *onesh*.

   Hashem’s awareness of our actions, described by the first part of the *mishnah*, is not enough basis for reward and punishment. For us to deserve *sechar* or *onesh*, our deeds must result from decisions fully in our hands.\[^362] The *mishnah*’s two phrases have full significance only when taken *together*. Only Hashem’s awareness (*ha’kol tzafui*) *coupled* with our free choice (*ha’reshus nesuna*) make reward and punishment (mentioned next by the *mishnah*) possible.

   **Total Control**  
The word the *mishnah* uses to describe this choice — *reshus* — means “domain” or “control.” The implication is that we are given not only free choice to decide but also control over the outcome. The Rambam begins his discussion about free choice by emphasizing this point, asserting that each person has the ability to become a *tzaddik* like Moshe Rabbeinu, a *rasha* like Yeravam, a wise man or a fool, merciful or cruel.\[^363] Though we are created with fixed genetic natures and nurtured by our cultural milieu,\[^364] good choices and hard work can (with heavenly assistance) help us become whatever we aim for.\[^365] Even one who starts learning at age forty can become Rebbe Akiva.

   This belief makes our decisions even more significant. Our choices are not only ours to make; they also determine who we become. They are not only something Hashem holds us responsible for; they are also responsible for what we amount to.

   **Four Important Words**  
In just two phrases consisting of a mere four words, Rebbe Akiva teaches us two principles of faith that, when read together and fully appreciated, should inspire us to take ourselves and our choices seriously. Knowing that (on the one hand) Hashem is fully aware of our actions and that (on the other hand) our decisions are fully ours to make and ultimately determine who we become should motivate us to make our choices carefully and live our lives maturely and responsibly.  
\*\*Taking Full Advantage \*\*

   רַבִּי טַרְפוֹן אוֹמֵר, הַיּוֹם קָצָר וְהַמְּלָאכָה מְרֻבָּה, וְהַפּוֹעֲלִים עֲצֵלִים, וְהַשָּׂכָר הַרְבֵּה, וּבַעַל הַבַּיִת דּוֹחֵק: (אבות ב:טו)  
הוּא הָיָה אוֹמֵר, לֹא עָלֶיךָ הַמְּלָאכָה לִגְמֹר, וְלֹא אַתָּה בֶן חוֹרִין לִבָּטֵל מִמֶּנָּה.  
אִם לָמַדְתָּ תוֹרָה הַרְבֵּה, נוֹתְנִים לְךָ שָׂכָר הַרְבֵּה.  
וְנֶאֱמָן הוּא בַעַל מְלַאכְתְּךָ שֶׁיְּשַׁלֵּם לְךָ שְׂכַר פְּעֻלָּתֶךָ. וְדַע מַתַּן שְׂכָרָן שֶׁל צַדִּיקִים לֶעָתִיד לָבֹא: (אבות ב:טז)

   Towards the end of Maseches Avos’s second *perek*, Rebbe Tarfon makes two statements about the effort expected from us in this world.

   **A Challenging Mission**  
His first statement delineates the context of our lives. The Chassid Yavetz gives the background to this statement by identifying three reasons people fail to maximize their potential fully: they overestimate the amount of time they have, misestimate the amount they need to accomplish, or fail to appreciate the importance of the task.\[^366]

   Rebbe Tarfon responds to all three reasons by emphasizing that “the ‘day’ (a metaphor for life) is short, there is much work to do, the workers are lazy, the reward is great, and the master (Hashem) has high expectations.”\[^367]

   Each point is independently significant.

   **Short on Time**  
Rebbe Tarfon begins with the length of the “day.” Earlier in the second *perek,* Rebbe Eliezer taught the importance of taking advantage of every day of life when he urged doing *teshuvah* “the day *before* we die.”\[^368] As this can occur any day, Rebbe Eliezer advises doing *teshuva* daily.\[^369]

   If the “day” were long, we would have lots of time to accomplish our objectives. In truth, however, our lives fly by. Tehillim compares man’s life to a shadow: man, like a shadow, disappears quickly.\[^370]

   The Chofetz Chaim compared how we experience life to people’s postcard inscription. The postcard initially has lots of blank space, so people write with regular-sized letters. As they get to the end of the card and little room remains, people often feel they have much more to add and need smaller letters to fit it all in. Sadly, many people live life the same way. While young, with their whole lives ahead of them, they often lack the urgency to maximize their time. Only once the end is near, people scramble to “fit it all in.”

   We should appreciate the time we have at an earlier stage. The Chiddushei HaRim explains the custom of giving a *chasan* a gold watch this way. The watch should remind him to appreciate the great value of time, life’s most precious commodity.\[^371]

   **The Significant Task**  
The shortness of life is compounded by the enormity of the task. This is Rebbe Tarfon’s next point: “There is much work to do.” The *meforshim* explain that this pertains mainly to Torah learning, which is “longer than (all) land and wider than the seas.”\[^372]

   What makes matters worse is that we do not appreciate the importance of the task. The *medrash* tells us that Moshe Rabbeinu fasted and did not sleep for the entirety of the 120-day period he was receiving the Torah. His mindset was like that of a minister permitted by the king to keep all the gold coins he could count within a limited period.\[^373] Moshe realized his golden opportunity and did not want to squander even a minute.

   Rebbe Tarfon taught that for us, like for Moshe, “the reward is great.” Every moment of our lives is an opportunity to perform meaningful actions or study Torah, described in Sefer Mishlei as more rewarding than money or any other commodity.\[^374] These pursuits give our lives meaning and are the only things we take from this world.\[^375]

   \[clt]Every moment of our lives is an opportunity to perform meaningful actions or study Torah.

   **Doing Our Part**  
Rebbe Tarfon’s emphasis on the enormity of the task can lead one to a dangerous mistaken conclusion. If the amount to accomplish so greatly exceeds the time available, maybe we should not even begin the task. Rebbe Tarfon responds to this in his second *mishnah,* explaining that we are not expected to “finish the work.”\[^376] The amount that needs to be *completed* should never deter us from *doing what we can*.

   This is how Rav Meir Shapiro understood the significance of Hashem asking Avraham to count the stars (as representative of his descendants).\[^377] Though no human can calculate all of the stars, Hashem taught Avraham the importance of doing what he could, of beginning the task nonetheless. Avraham's doing so served as a model for his descendants, who should never hesitate to embark on missions that seem beyond their reach.

   Rav Meir Shapiro exemplified this idea by initiating the *Daf Yomi* learning program. When he started the program, did he believe that he would have the ability to convince *hundreds of thousands* across the world to join? Despite his limited reach, Rav Meir did his part; the rest is history.\[^378]

   Rebbe Tarfon’s use of the uncommon word “*ligmor* (to finish)” may hint to the *pasuk* “*l’keil gomeir alai,*”\[^379] which (if this explanation is correct) he understood as referring to Hashem finishing the work we begin. We must take the first steps, believing that Hashem will fill in the parts we cannot complete. When we act *with such faith*, Hashem finishes our work for us.\[^380]

   Though we are not expected to finish, Rebbe Tarfon teaches that we “are not free to decide to desist from it.”\[^381] Life is not just an opportunity; it comes with the expectation that we maximize it. As Rabban Yochanan ben Zakkai (Rebbe Tarfon’s rebbe) taught just a few *mishnayos* earlier: *“Im lamadsa Torah harbei, al tachazik tovah latzmecha, ki l’kach notzarta.”*\[^382] To translate as paraphrase, “If you learn much Torah, don’t ‘pat yourself on the back,’ as you are merely fulfilling the mission you were created to accomplish.” Many people see Torah learning and maximizing their time as a personal choice. In truth, we are all *required* to do both.

   While we cannot finish the whole job, we are responsible for doing our part. Often, our inability to ensure the completion of a task keeps us from taking action. Our faith in Hashem and understanding of our responsibility to do our part should inspire us to take action. In doing so, we emulate

* Bas Paroh, who reached out for Moshe’s basket though it was beyond her reach,\[^383]
* Amram, who remarried Yocheved without knowing if their children would survive,\[^384] and
* Esther, who approached Achashveirosh unannounced without knowing if he would agree to receive her.\[^385]

  Great people accomplish great things when they fulfill their (relatively) small role with the faith that Hashem will complete the process.

  \[clt]Great people accomplish great things when they fulfill their (relatively) small role with the faith that Hashem will will complete the process.

  May Rebbe Tarfon’s teachings help us appreciate how short life is, how much we need to accomplish, and what is truly valuable. Though one lifetime is not enough to complete the task, may we embrace our mission and commit ourselves to accomplish the most we can!



  **The World's Three Pillars**

  The Significance of Three

  The Foundation(s) of Our World

  How We Rise Up

  Freedom To Focus

  Ultimate Freedom

  Why We Should Love Work

  The Jewish Home  
**The Significance of Three**

  הוּא (שִׁמְעוֹן הַצַּדִּיק) הָיָה אוֹמֵר, עַל שְׁלשָׁה דְבָרִים הָעוֹלָם עוֹמֵד, עַל הַתּוֹרָה וְעַל הָעֲבוֹדָה וְעַל גְּמִילוּת חֲסָדִים. (א:ב)

  **An Existential Opening**  
After Maseches Avos’s first *mishnah* concludes (its description of those who relayed the Torah from generation to generation) by mentioning the *Anshei Knesset Hagedolah* (Men of the Great Assembly), the second mishnah presents a foundational statement of Shimon HaTzaddik, who was “*mi’sheyarei Knesset Hagedolah* (of the last members of the Great Assembly).”

  Shimon HaTzaddik’s statement is critical for our general worldview. Unlike most of Maseches Avos, which consists of directives for *best living* *our lives*, Shimon HaTzadik relates to the more fundamental question of *why* the *world* exists. He asserts that the world stands on three pillars: it exists to facilitate *Torah* (learning),\[^386] *avodah* (service of Hashem), and *gemilus chasadim* (acts of kindness).

  Shimon HaTzadik teaches us that the world’s sustenance hinges upon human action and behavior. Because Hashem created the world to offer humanity the opportunity to live meaningful lives, He conditioned its continued existence on us doing so.

  The *meforshim* associate the pillars with different aspects of personal development. The Tiferes Yisrael, for example, sees the three as representative of our need to develop ourselves as well as our relationships with others. Torah learning is how we enrich *ourselves*, *avodah* is how we relate to *Hashem*,\[^387], and *gemilas chasadim* reflect healthy relationships with *other people*.

  \[clt]Torah learning is how we enrich *ourselves*, *avodah* is how we relate to *Hashem*, and *gemilas chasadim* reflect healthy relationships with *other people*.

  The Rambam saw all three pillars as modes of our internal growth. Torah develops our intellectual ability, *avodah* our observance, and *gemilus chassadim* our character.\[^388]

  Both interpretations agree that the pillars are essential not just for the world but also for ourselves. They teach us not just what the world needs to exist but also what *our* existence is meant to be about. The world cannot learn, *daven*, or do acts of *chesed* on its own. Its purpose is realized through *our* actions.

  **Pillars of Diversity**  
The existence of *multiple* pillars helps us understand human diversity and the diverse aspects of each person’s unique identity and mission. The Alshich\[^389] uses our *mishnah* to explain why some people are drawn to Torah, others feel an affinity for *tzedakah,* while others focus on *gemilus chasadim*. People are created with these different affinities because the world needs all these missions.

  Rebbe Shimon Bar Yochai and the Chassidim Harishonim were excellent examples of this phenomenon. The *gemara\[^390]* tells us that Rashbi and his colleagues focused almost exclusively on Torah learning. They were excused from the responsibility to pray (the central form of *avodah* in their and our time). The Chassidim Harishonim were a stark contrast. They would spend *nine* hours, the majority of each day, praying.\[^391] Rebbe Yochanan went even further and exclaimed that, ideally, people should *daven* all day!

  Which approach is correct — Rashbi’s, Rav Yochanan’s, or something in between? How could there be such a stark contrast?

  Shimon HaTzaddik helps us answer this question. The world’s three pillars symbolize its multiple purposes. To fulfill these different goals, Hashem creates people interested in each of them. Some are drawn to Torah, others to *avodah*, and a third group to *gemilus chasadim.*

  **Harmonic Integration**  
The Akeidat Yitzchak\[^392] and Rav Kook use this idea to explain the deeper meaning of *shalom*. Avos’s first *perek* concludes with a statement of Rabban Shimon Ben Gamliel that he patterned after the earlier one of Shimon HaTzaddik.

  Like Shimon HaTzaddik, Rabban Shimon lists three factors that the world’s existence depends upon. Unlike Shimon HaTzaddik, who focused on the world’s *purpose*, Rabban Shimon identifies the three elements that *sustain* the world by maintaining healthy societal interaction — *din* (justice), *emes* (truth), and *shalom*.\[^393]

  What does Rabban Shimon mean by “*shalom”*? The Akeidas Yitzchak explains that *shalom* is more than just a lack of hostility. It refers to people able to work together to realize shared goals.

  Rav Kook\[^394] takes this idea further and defined *shalom* as people appreciating and finding an appropriate role for each other’s strengths. The example he gives sharpens the connection between the parallel statements of Rabban Shimon and Shimon HaTzaddik. *Shalom,* explains Rav Kook, is the ability of those involved in *chesed* to appreciate and find the appropriate place for those passionate about *avodah* and for the two to integrate themselves with those focused upon Torah learning. The existence of people focused upon different pillars necessitates *shalom* — our successful integration of and with one another.

  Recognizing the world’s multiple pillars should help us appreciate the existential importance of the efforts of those different from ourselves and inspire us to seek to integrate our efforts with theirs.

  **Internal Integration**  
Rav Kook\[^395] applies this integrative perspective to our internal mindset as well. People should appreciate the importance of their involvement (at least on some level) in *all* aspects of the world’s purpose.

  Rav Kook sees Rav Hamnuna as exemplifying this idea. The *gemara*\[^396] tells us that Rava criticized Rav Hamnuna for devoting excessive time to prayer. Rava felt that Rav Hamnuna should have focused on Torah learning, which earns one a place in the next world, instead of praying for his needs in this (fleeting) one.

  The gemara explains that Rav Hamnuna’s opinion was that *“zman tefilah lachud u’zman torah lachud* (both *tefilah* and Torah have their appropriate time).”\[^397] Rav Kook understands Rav Hamnuna’s response as reflective of a broader principle. He explains that the pasuk “*B’chol derachecha da’eihu* (know Hashem in all your pursuits)” teaches us to appreciate and focus upon whichever avenue of *Avodas Hashem* we are presently involved in without concern for other forms of worship.\[^398]

  Rav Kook uses Shimon HaTzaddik’s pillars to present this idea. Though Torah learning may be the most important activity, when one is involved in *tefilah*, he should not think about the need to learn Torah (or perform *gemilus chassadim*). Similarly, when engaged in Torah learning, one should not be distracted by the need to *daven* or do *chesed*.\[^399] Z’*man tefilah lachud u’zman torah lachud*.

  May Shimon HaTzaddik’s teaching focus us on accomplishing what the world and we were created for. May the multiple pillars help us appreciate the importance of those whose focus differs from our own as well as develop ourselves properly!  
**The Foundation(s) of Our World**

  הוּא (שִׁמְעוֹן הַצַּדִּיק) הָיָה אוֹמֵר, עַל שְׁלשָׁה דְבָרִים הָעוֹלָם עוֹמֵד, עַל הַתּוֹרָה וְעַל הָעֲבוֹדָה וְעַל גְּמִילוּת חֲסָדִים. (א:ב)

  **Torah**  
Avos’s second *mishnah* begins its list of the world’s three pillars with Torah. Though all three pillars are important, Torah learning reigns supreme. Chazal\[^400] see this idea in the Torah’s very first word — “*bereishis*.” Torah learning is the *reishis* the world was created to facilitate.

  We remind ourselves of this fact each morning when we conclude our recital of the *berachos* related to Torah learning with the *mishnah* in Pei’ah, which teaches that “*Talmud Torah k'neged kulam* (the significance of Torah learning is equal to that of all other *mitzvos*).”

  Understandably, the *gemara*\[^401] asserts that Torah learning is of greater value than building the Beis Hamikdash, honoring one’s parents, and even saving a life.\[^402] In fact, Chazal explain that the world's very existence hinges upon Torah learning. The *gemara*\[^403] understands the Torah’s formulation of the sixth day of creation as *“yom hashishi”* (as opposed to “*shishi*” like the description of previous days — “*yom echad,” “yom sheni,” “yom shlishi,”* etc.) as teaching that Hashem conditioned creation on a future special sixth day — the sixth day of Sivan when the Torah was given. Had we not committed ourselves to the Torah, Hashem would have returned to nothingness.

  Rav Chaim Volozhin\[^404] adds that the world’s existence not only hinged (in the past) on the original *Kabbalas HaTorah* but also *continues* to depend upon *constant* Torah learning. If there would be even one moment completely bereft of Torah learning, the world would cease to exist.\[^405]

  *Talmud Torah* is not just the world’s purpose; it is also the unique purpose and mission of the Jewish people. As the *mishnah* tells us in Avos’s second *perek*, “If you have learned much Torah, do not take special credit; it is (simply) why you were created.”\[^406] Hashem created the world to be a context for Torah learning; He created the Jewish people as the vehicle. This explains why Hillel taught that one who does not study Torah deserves to die.\[^407] Torah study is a central reason for our existence. If we do not commit ourselves to it, we do not deserve to exist.

  Why is *Talmud Torah* so important? Firstly, it is the one pursuit we can (and should) devote our free time to. While we perform *chesed* in response to another’s situational need and *daven* three times a day, we can learn Torah at any and every free moment. It is the constant that should fill our lives with meaning.

  Additionally, through Torah learning, we transcend our world and meet Hashem through His wisdom. Though we connect to Hashem through the performance of all *mitzvos* and forms of *Avodas Hashem*, when we study Torah, we achieve a higher connection because we *immerse ourselves in His thought*. We understand and connect to Hashem by appreciating the way He “thinks” and what He values.

  \[clt]When we study Torah… we understand and connect to Hashem by appreciating the way He “thinks” and what He values.

  ***Avodah***  
But Torah is not the only pillar. *Avodah* is also important. Study alone is not enough for the world and our existence. This is why man’s presence in Gan Eden (which was self-sustainable but still) included his responsibility to work.\[^408]

  *“Avodah”* should focus on *Avodas Hashem*, broadly defined.\[^409] This is why the *meforshim* explain the mishnah’s mention of *avodah* as referring to *korbanos*.\[^410] *Korbanos* express our appreciation of Hashem’s role in the world (and our lives) and our interest in giving to and sacrificing for Hashem. As with all relationships,\[^411] we reinforce our commitment and closeness to Hashem through gift and sacrifice.

  Kayin and Hevel were the first people the Torah records as having brought *korbanos*.\[^412] When Noach (later) offered a *korban* upon exiting the ark, Hashem responded with His promise to continuously sustain the world.\[^413] When we show our appreciation of and commitment to Hashem, He commits Himself to us and our world.

  Of course, today, we are unable to offer *korbanos*. We offer our *tefilos* instead.\[^414] The *gemara*\[^415] teaches that the offering of the *korban tamid* used to sustain the world; today, our *tefilos* play this role.\[^416] In certain ways, *tefilos* are even more significant than *korbanos*.\[^417] Petitioning Hashem for our needs expresses recognition of our dependence upon Him and our interest in a relationship with Him.\[^418]

  The Rambam\[^419] extends the pillar of *avodah* beyond *korbanos* and *tefilah* by explaining that the mishnah uses *korbanos* as a paradigm for *mitzvos* in general. *Korbanos* are significant because they are how we fulfill Hashem’s commandment (to sacrifice them). We serve Hashem in a similar way by observing *any and all* of His *mitzvos*.\[^420]

  ***Gemillut Chassadim***  
The third pillar is *chesed*. One might have thought personal development and commitment to Hashem would be enough to sustain the world. Our *mishnah* teaches us that this is not the case.\[^421] In fact, the Nevi’im Micha\[^422] and Yeshaya\[^423] present care for others (in contrast to *korbanos*) as central to what Hashem wants from us. *Korbanos* and *Avodas Hashem* are important, but Hashem wants us to serve Him by (also) caring about and for His creations — particularly those He created in His image.\[^424]

  By doing so, we emulate Hashem who created the world as an act of *chesed*.\[^425] He, of course, does not need the world; He created it for us.

  Our acts of *chesed* are also our way of giving back to Hashem. Rav Avraham Chaim Feuer explains that this is why the mishnah uses the term ***gemilus*** <i>chasadim</i>: our *chesed* is a way of “paying Hashem back” for His. By assisting others created in His image, we show our appreciation that we, too, were created this way.

  \[clt]By assisting others created in His image, we show our appreciation that we, too, were created this way.

  **Torah and *Ma’asim Tovim***  
*Chesed* is an essential complement to *Talmud Torah*. The gemara\[^426] compares one involved in only Torah learning but not *chesed* to one who has no G-d. One focused only on himself lacks a meaningful relationship with Hashem. As we saw, Torah learning can be “*gadlus”* — greater than other *mitzvos*\[^427], but if taken to a self-centered extreme, it can also be godless. Torah learning is only *gadlus* when it inspires us to care for Hashem’s other creations.

  \[clt]Torah learning can be *gadlus,* but if taken to a self-centered extreme, it can also be godless.

  This is why we celebrate Torah and *ma’asim tovim* (good deeds) as the goals and epitome of life. The *mishnah* in Avos’s sixth *perek* tells how Rebbe Yossi Ben Kisma rejected a substantial monetary offer to move to a city lacking a strong Torah presence. Rebbe Yossi explained his refusal by the fact that we take only Torah learning and good deeds with us to the next world. This is why Torah and *ma’asim tovim* are the life goals the community wishes newborn babies and mothers *daven* for each week when they light candles. The two *together* are how we serve Hashem in the fullest sense of the word.



  **The Three-Legged Stool**  
Put together, these three foci — Torah, *avodah*, and *gemilus chasadim* — are what the world exists for and what we, therefore, should be focused upon. May appreciating this help us maximize our lives and our contribution to sustaining the world!  
**How We Rise Up — The Greatness of Torah Learning**

  רַבִּי מֵאִיר אוֹמֵר כָּל הָעוֹסֵק בַּתּוֹרָה לִשְׁמָהּ, זוֹכֶה לִדְבָרִים הַרְבֵּה.  
וְלֹא עוֹד אֶלָּא שֶׁכָּל הָעוֹלָם כֻּלּוֹ כְדַי הוּא לוֹ…וּמְגַדַּלְתּוֹ וּמְרוֹמַמְתּוֹ עַל כָּל הַמַּעֲשִׂים (ו:א)

  כָל מִי שֶׁעוֹסֵק בְּתַלְמוּד תּוֹרָה הֲרֵי זֶה מִתְעַלֶּה (ו:ב)

  גְּדוֹלָה תוֹרָה שֶׁהִיא נוֹתֶנֶת חַיִּים לְעֹשֶׂיהָ בָּעוֹלָם הַזֶּה וּבָעוֹלָם הַבָּא… (ו:ז)

  **Perek Kinyan Torah**  
*Maseches Avos* originally consisted of five *perakim*. In order to continue the study of *Pirkei Avos* on the sixth Shabbos between Pesach and Shavuot, a sixth *perek* was added to the *maseches*. As that Shabbos generally falls out right before Shavuot, the sixth *perek* helps us prepare for the *chag* by focusing on Torah learning. This *perek* is known as *“Kinyan Torah”* because it refers to two aspects of Torah acquisition: how we acquire Torah and how we acquire other important values along with it.\[^428]

  **Grants Life**  
Torah learning benefits the student in both this world and beyond. *Mishnah Zayin* formulates the point this way: “Torah is great, for it grants life in this world and the next.”

  **The Next World**  
Considering Torah learning’s status as a central *mitzvah*, we easily understand how it earns one life in the next world. *Mishnah Yud* tells of Rebbe Yossi Ben Kisma’s rejection of a substantial monetary offer to move to a city lacking a strong Torah presence. Rebbe Yossi explained his refusal by saying that we take only Torah learning and good deeds (not gold and silver) to the next world. Many things seem valuable in this world. When choosing how to live our lives, we should focus on what has eternal value.

  ***This*** **World**  
The *mishnah*’s assertion that Torah learning also grants life in *this* world is a greater *chiddush*. Rebbe Akiva reinforced this point in his response to those who questioned his teaching Torah despite the Roman prohibition against doing so. Rebbe Akiva compared a Jew’s need for Torah learning to a fish’s dependency on water.\[^429] Torah does not just enhance life; it is a necessary condition for it. Though we can physically live and breathe without Torah, our spiritual life hinges on its nourishment.\[^430]

  **Making The Whole World Worthwhile**  
In addition to a meaningful life, *Mishnah Aleph* quotes Rebbe Meir who lists many additional benefits Torah learning confers.

  Before listing these benefits, Rebbe Meir emphasizes that a person who learns Torah also makes the existence of the entire world worthwhile. As we have seen, Maseches Avos begins with Shimon Hatzaddik’s assertion that the world exists for Torah learning (as well as *avodah* and *gemilus chasadim*). Rebbe Meir takes this notion a significant step further by portraying even a *single* person’s Torah learning as making the *whole* world worthwhile!

  **The Greatest and Highest Life**  
*Mishnah Aleph* concludes its list of benefits by declaring that Torah learning makes one “greater” and “higher”\[^431] than all creations. Torah learning is “great” not just because it grants life (*Mishnah Zayin*) but also because it helps us grow.\[^432]

  The *gemara\[^433]* further develops the “greater” aspect of Torah learning by asserting that it is “greater” than honoring parents, building the *Beis Hamikdash*, and even saving a life. Saving a life takes *priority* over Torah learning, but learning is of *greater value* because it deepens our appreciation of Hashem. Though we connect to Hashem by fulfilling any and all *mitzvos*, Torah learning immerses us in Hashem’s wisdom and facilitates familiarity with Him.

  As we saw, *Mishnah Aleph* describes Torah learning as raising the student above other creations. *Mishnah Beis* adds a second aspect by explaining that Torah learning elevates people (not just relative to other creatures, but also) to a higher (in fact, the highest) version of themselves.

  This second dimension supports how Rav Yosef reflected on his Torah learning. The *gemara*\[^434] tells us that, when asked about his custom to celebrate the giving of the Torah on Shavuos by eating a special meat sandwich, Rav Yosef explained that without Torah learning, he would have amounted to no more than “the average Joe (Yosef).”

  Rashi’s formulation of Rav Yosef’s words (“if not for the days I learned Torah and *elevated* myself…”) links them to our *mishnah*’s focus on Torah‘s elevative capacity. Though the *mitzvos* and good deeds we perform earn us reward, only Torah learning develops us in a way that inherently elevates and distinguishes us.

  \[clt]Though the *mitzvos* and good deeds we perform earn us reward, only Torah learning develops us in a way that inherently elevates and distinguishes us.

  To summarize, Avos’s sixth *perek* emphasizes the great significance of Torah learning: it grants us life in both this world and the next and also helps us achieve greatness and the highest level of our potential growth. May these *mishnayos* inspire us to appreciate and fully maximize Torah-learning opportunities!  
**Freedom To Focus**

  אָמַר רַבִּי יְהוֹשֻׁעַ בֶּן לֵוִי… וְהַלֻּחֹת מַעֲשֵׂה אֱלֹקים הֵמָּה וְהַמִּכְתָּב מִכְתַּב אֱלֹקים הוּא חָרוּת עַל הַלֻּחֹת, אַל תִּקְרָא חָרוּת אֶלָּא חֵרוּת, *שֶׁאֵין לְךָ בֶן חוֹרִין אֶלָּא מִי שֶׁעוֹסֵק בְּתַלְמוּד תּוֹרָה.*  
וְכָל מִי שֶׁעוֹסֵק בְּתַלְמוּד תּוֹרָה הֲרֵי זֶה מִתְעַלֶּה, שֶׁנֶּאֱמַר (במדבר כא) וּמִמַּתָּנָה נַחֲלִיאֵל וּמִנַּחֲלִיאֵל בָּמוֹת: (אבות ו:ב)

  רַבִּי נְחוּנְיָא בֶּן הַקָּנָה אוֹמֵר, כָּל הַמְקַבֵּל עָלָיו עֹל תּוֹרָה, *מַעֲבִירִין מִמֶּנּוּ עֹל מַלְכוּת וְעֹל דֶּרֶךְ אֶרֶץ.*  
וְכָל הַפּוֹרֵק מִמֶּנּו עֹל תּוֹרָה, נוֹתְנִין עָלָיו עֹל מַלְכוּת וְעֹל דֶּרֶךְ אֶרֶץ: (אבות ג:ה)

  **The Linkage of Freedom to Torah**  
Rebbe Yehoshua ben Levi taught\[^435]: “*Ein lecha ben chorin ela mi she’osek b’Talmud Torah* — Only those involved in Torah learning are (truly) free.” He derived this idea from the Torah’s description of the writing on the *luchot* as *“charus*,*”* a word spelled the same way as “*cheirus*, free.” What did Rebbe Yehoshua mean by this teaching? In what way do those not involved in Torah learning lack freedom?

  Many *meforshim* connect Rebbe Yehoshua’s statement to an (earlier) one of Rebbe Nechunya ben Hakanah: “The *ol* (yoke) of *malchus* and of *derech eretz* are removed from one who accepts the *ol* of Torah.”\[^436] Man is put in this world to work\[^437] and chooses what kind of work to focus on. He can opt for backbreaking work in the fields or “work” with his mouth by learning Torah instead.\[^438] If he chooses the latter, he is “freed” from the former.\[^439]

  How does that happen? How does a person who accepts the yoke of Torah have other responsibilities removed from him?

  Rashi\[^440] explains that other people do his work for him. Rebbe Shimon bar Yochai\[^441] famously applied this idea to *k’rias Shema*. He explained that the second *parshah*’s reference to our working in the field\[^442] must refer to a time when the Jewish people are not fully committed to Hashem’s Will because, at a time when we are, our work is done for us by others.

  How does this happen? Who does the work for those learning Torah, and why?

  **A Divine Directive**  
Rabbeinu Yonah explains that our *mishnah mandates* exempting those fully immersed in Torah learning (*toraso umanuso*) from contributing to community tax collection and government-required work.\[^443] Other people are required to cover the *talmid chacham*’s share.

  Rebbe Yochanan\[^444] understood this principle in an even broader way. He taught that, in addition to covering the \*talmid chacham’\*s share of communal responsibilities, people are also responsible to do the *talmid chacham*’s *personal* work for him. All of us should express our appreciation of the importance of Torah learning by enabling those devoted to its study to focus on their important mission.

  **A Divine Promise**  
The Rambam\[^445] himself explains the *mishnah* differently. Instead of a Divine *directive*, the Rambam understood the *mishnah* as a Divine *promise*. Rebbe Nechunya (and Rebbe Yehoshua by association) teaches us that *Hashem* spares those committed to Torah learning from the challenges and burdens most people endure.\[^446] The person devoted to Torah learning is freed from the yokes of *malchus* and *derech eretz* by Hashem, not (just) other people.

  \[clt]One dedicated to Torah learning is released from the yokes of *malchus* and *derech eretz* by Hashem, not (just) other people.
