  The *medrash*\[^447] quotes this idea about *ol malchus* in Hashem’s name. Moshe asked Hashem how He expected people suffering persecution in exile to devote time to Torah learning. Hashem replied, *“Kol ha’osek baTorah, nitzol mi’shibud galuyot* — Anyone involved in Torah will be saved from the yoke of exile.”\[^448]

  The *gemara*\[^449] summarizes this idea beautifully by identifying the oil Chizkiyahu HaMelech used to light up the *Batei Medrash* and *Batei Knesset* as what merited the removal of Sancheirev’s *ol* from the Jewish People.\[^450]

  The *Machzor Vitri* and *Bartenura* apply this idea to the yoke of *derech eretz* as well. Hashem ensures that those committed to Torah learning are unhindered by the need to (work too hard to\[^451]) earn a living. Rebbe Shimon ben Elazar points out that Hashem takes care of animals without their having to work for a living.\[^452] Hashem would also take care of our needs if we lived correctly.

  Before he ate from the *Eitz Hada’as*, Hashem provided for man without his having to work hard (“by the sweat of his brow”).\[^453] Hashem did this for the entire Jewish people when he fed us *mon* daily in the desert. Rebbe Shimon and Rebbe Nechunya teach us that if we focus our lives on Torah learning, we are still (even after the original sin) able to tap into this prelapsarian ideal of survival without difficult work.

  The Chassidim Harishonim were an excellent historical example of this principle. The *Chassidim Harishonim* spent nine hours a day davening. The *gemara*\[^454] wonders how they got their work done and answers that Hashem ensured their work was completed quickly and easily. How long our tasks take and how hard they are to complete hinges on whether we devote ourselves to what Hashem wants to help us find the time and headspace for.

  **The Chicken and The Egg**  
People often feel like they lack the time to commit to Torah learning because they are saddled with work and other burdens. The *mishnah* teaches us that, in truth, the linkage goes the other way. We are saddled with responsibilities *because* we do not commit ourselves to Torah learning.\[^455]

  May we devote ourselves properly to what matters most so we do not have to spend time on what matters less!

  We have seen how many associate the freedom Rebbe Yehoshua ben Levi mentioned in the sixth *perek* with the removal of the yokes mentioned in the third *perek*. In our next chapter, we will see a second (more intrinsic) understanding of the freedom Rebbe Yehoshua describes.  
**Ultimate Freedom**

  אָמַר רַבִּי יְהוֹשֻׁעַ בֶּן לֵוִי… וְהַלֻּחֹת מַעֲשֵׂה אֱלֹקים הֵמָּה וְהַמִּכְתָּב מִכְתַּב אֱלֹקים הוּא חָרוּת עַל הַלֻּחֹת (שמות לב), אַל תִּקְרָא חָרוּת אֶלָּא חֵרוּת, שֶׁאֵין לְךָ בֶן חוֹרִין אֶלָּא מִי שֶׁעוֹסֵק בְּתַלְמוּד תּוֹרָה…  
(אבות ו:ב)

  In the previous chapter, we began studying Rebbe Yehoshua ben Levi’s declaration that only those involved in Torah study are truly free. Rebbe Yehoshua derived this idea from the Torah’s description of the writing on the *luchos* as *“charus”* (a word spelled the same way as “*cheirus*, free.”)\[^456] What is the significance of this specific source? Why does the Torah choose specifically this method of teaching us freedom’s dependency on Torah learning?

  **Freedom From Others**  
We saw many *meforshim* who link Rebbe Yehoshua’s statement to Rebbe Nechunya’s earlier assertion that Torah scholars are exempted from taxes and other communal responsibilities.\[^457]

  These obligations are not the sole way people are subjugated to others. Many end up under other people’s dominion by falling under their sway. When we model ourselves off other people’s values and expectations, we sacrifice the unique personal identity we were meant to develop.

  The worst form of slavery is that of the spirit. True freedom is not achieved through political emancipation. We find it by pursuing our intended life path; we are only free when we realize our true (and natural) selves.\[^458]

  Rav Kook\[^459] measured slavery and freedom by spiritual independence, not social standing. One can live free of restrictions and obligations (and even own slaves) but actually be a slave because he feels beholden to society’s expectations. Conversely, many are physically enslaved but truly free because they maintain their spiritual independence.

  The Jews of the Warsaw Ghetto were an excellent example of this phenomenon. Even before their uprising against the Nazis, Rav Oshri\[^460] — Rav of the Warsaw Ghetto — directed them to continue reciting the *berachah* of *shelo asani aved* (that He did not make me a slave) because they were free in spirit and fully able to choose to commit themselves to *Avodas Hashem*.\[^461] The Nazis may have controlled them physically, but they remained spiritually free. (From this perspective, the eventual Warsaw Ghetto uprising was merely an attempt to translate their spiritual freedom into a physical reality.)

  Torah helps us achieve this spiritual independence by teaching us eternal heavenly truth, wisdom, and direction. Torah study gives us the ability to free ourselves from fleeting contemporary perspectives. Our connection to Hashem’s eternal values has enabled us to transcend the values and milieus of hundreds of countries and cultures we have interfaced with.

  The reality of modern media has submerged us even more deeply and intensely within secular society, threatening our unique, godly values. Now more than ever, it is critical that we use Torah learning to help us sustain our spiritual autonomy.

  \[clt]Now more than ever, it is critical that we use Torah learning to help us sustain our spiritual autonomy.

  **Freedom From (a False Version of) Our Selves**  
Torah learning also frees us from additional *internal*, more subtle forms of slavery: the subjugation to our own physical drives and desires and the pursuit of meaningless activities.\[^462] Focusing on important things helps us avoid focusing on what is not.\[^463]

  Contemporary society sees freedom as (merely) the lack of external control — freedom from control, commitment, and responsibility. For example, in his famous 1941 State of the Union Address (later known as The Four Freedoms Address), Franklin D. Roosevelt spoke about the freedoms of speech and worship and freedom from fear and want. Facing the evil Nazi regime, which limited personal freedoms, he identified the values that the United States and its allies went to war to defend.

  In the broader historical sense, Franklin Roosevelt’s address responded not only to the Nazis of his generation but also to the abuse of millennia of monarchies and dictatorships that institutionalized slavery and serfdom and curtailed fundamental freedoms. Modern democracies aim to solve these issues by establishing human rights as unalienable.

  Unfortunately, their (over)emphasis on freedom has fostered a lack of responsibility and meaning. Released from governmental mandates and religious and societal norms, many have become addicted to pleasure and focused on meaningless pursuits.

  Our *mishnah* teaches that the *only* way to avoid these pitfalls is to engage in *Talmud Torah*. Focusing on Torah learning also gives us a meaningful mission to be passionate about. The enthusiasm we develop for it inspires us to minimize engagement with meaningless things and gives us something more meaningful to pursue than physical pleasure.\[^464]

  The Maharal\[^465] explains how Rebbe Yehoshua derived this idea from the Torah’s description of the letters of the *luchos*. The letters were engraved from the front to the back of the rock in a way that left the internal parts of the letters *Samech* and *Mem Sofi* disconnected from (and thus unsupported by) their physical surroundings. Despite this lack of material support, the innards of these letters (miraculously) remained suspended in mid-air. So, too, explains the Maharal, a person’s focus on the spiritual helps him avoid dependency upon the physical.

  **Freedom To Be Our *True* Selves**  
There is a fourth way that Torah study and *Avodas Hashem* facilitate freedom. They not only free us *from* commitment (*sur mei’ra*) but also help us realize our *true* potential. *Avodas Hashem* and Torah learning also make a positive contribution (*asei tov*).

  In the Kuzari, the *chaver* used this idea to answer the Kuzar king’s question about his intended *aliyah*. The king asked why he sought to incur the additional *mitzvos* and responsibilities *aliyah* would generate. The *chaver* responded, "Commitment to Hashem is the ultimate freedom, and subjugation to Him is the ultimate honor.”\[^466]

  Though we understand the Kuzari’s association of honor with *Avodas Hashem*, it is harder to explain why he saw *Avodas Hashem* as freedom. We know that *mitzvos* are important responsibilities, but how does fulfilling them make us *free*?

  Rav Kook\[^467] explained that (only) *Avodas Hashem* helps us realize our unique potential and the ability to forge our true identity. Hashem creates each person with a creative, independent soul that yearns to fulfill its unique potential. *Avodas Hashem* is the framework that enables each person to make their unique contribution to the world.

  Food and sleep sustain our physical existence but do not help us realize our potential. We accomplish that through *Avodas Hashem*.

  ***Cheirus***  
This is why we use specifically the phrase *ben chorin* and the word *cheirus*, as opposed to *chofesh*, to describe our freedom. *Chofesh* means the lack of responsibility to another.\[^468] *Cheirus* connotes true freedom.

  We characterize the *Yom Tov* of Pesach as “*z’man cheiruseinu”* because Yetzias Mitzrayim’s goal was more than just liberation from servitude to Mitzrayim. More importantly, it facilitated *Avodas Hashem*.

  The story we tell on the Seder night is not (just) about our *physical* emancipation from Mitzrayim. The Haggadah summarizes the process we commemorate and celebrate on the as our transformation from *ovdei avodah zarah* to *ovdei Hashem*: “*Mi’techilah ovdei avodah zarah hayu avoseinu, v’achshav kervanu haMakom **la’avodaso*** (Our ancestors initially served idols. Now Hashem has drawn us close to His service).”

  Though we appreciate Hashem freeing us from physical slavery, we focus on the transition to “*avodas haMakom*” because that gives our life and physical freedom meaning and purpose. It is what makes us “*b’nei chorin*” — genuinely free people.

  May our appreciation of true freedom and Torah study’s ability to help us achieve it inspire us to maximize our opportunities to involve ourselves in it!  
**Why We Should Love Work**

  שְׁמַעְיָה אוֹמֵר, *אֱהֹב אֶת הַמְּלָאכָה*, וּשְׂנָא אֶת הָרַבָּנוּת, וְאַל תִּתְוַדַּע לָרָשׁוּת (אבות א:י).

  In past chapters, we studied Shimon Hatzaddik’s identification of the world’s three pillars: Torah, *avodah*, and *gemilus chassadim*.\[^469]

  Though the *meforshim* understand his mention of *avodah* as referring to *korbanos* and *tefillah*, *melachah* (physical labor) is also important. Shemaya\[^470]  emphasized this importance later in the first *perek* when he encouraged us to “love work.”\[^471]

  ***Torah She’bichsav***  
We should love work because it is an integral part of our lives.

  From the very start, the Torah emphasizes the importance of work. The world’s vegetation was ready to sprout but did not because “there was no man to work the ground.”\[^472] Afterwards, when finally created, man was placed in *Gan Eden* “*l’ovdah u’l’shomrah* — to work and protect it.”\[^473] Though Hashem created *Gan Eden* with its own “watering system,” Hashem made Adam responsible for it to teach him the importance of his work.

  This importance is why the *mitzvah* to observe Shabbos includes not only the prohibition against work on the seventh day but also the command to work six days.\[^474] “*Sheshes yamim ta’aseh melachah* (for six days you shall work).”\[^475] Work is also part of our covenant with Hashem.\[^476]

  Rebbe Yishmael\[^477] understood this to be the point made by the *pasuk* (that appears in the second *parshah* of *k’rias Shema*) of “*v’asafta d’ganecha* (and you shall gather your grain).” Rebbe Yishmael explained the *pasuk* as proscriptive: “*hanheg bahem minhag derech eretz*.” In addition to learning Torah, we are meant to spend time following “the ways of the world.” Working the field is so important that we spend time we would have devoted to Torah  working the fields instead.

  Rabban Gamliel (the son of Rebbe) took this idea even further by teaching that Torah learning not complemented by work will “end up failing and leading to sin.”\[^478] Work is not just independently valuable. It ensures that our Torah learning leads to a healthy, sustainable, and spiritual life.

  **Chazal**

  **Importance**  
Chazal emphasized the importance of work in many additional places. The *medrash*\[^479] attributes this value to the very first Jew. After Hashem commanded Avraham to “*lech lecha*” (without specifying which land he should set out for), Avraham visited various countries. When he encountered lands whose people spent their days partying, he said, “I hope that I am not meant to live here.” When he arrived in Eretz Yisrael and saw people working the land, he said, “I hope this is where Hashem intends me to be.” Hashem responded and said, “Indeed, this is the place.” Avraham recognized the importance and positive nature of productive physical labor.

  Chazal also taught that planting takes precedence even over welcoming Mashiach. A person planting a sapling who hears that Mashiach has arrived should finish planting and *only then* go out to greet Mashiach.\[^480] Greeting Mashiach is not our priority; planting is.

  Chazal saw work as more than just a value. They saw it as a condition for a serious, mature life. The *mishnah,*\[^481] therefore, disqualifies a person who earns his livelihood through gambling from testifying in court. One who does not take life seriously cannot be trusted.

  The *gemara* explains that we disqualify the testimony of gamblers because they are not “*oseik b’yishuvo shel olam* (involved in developing the world).” Hashem put the world in our hands and expects us to develop it. The Rambam\[^482] sharpens this idea by describing the gambler as violating the *prohibition* (!) against being involved in meaningless pursuits. He explains that Hashem put us in this world to focus on two things: developing ourselves and developing the world.

  Rebbe Akiva learned from the *mitzvah* of *bris milah* that we are supposed to work on finishing what Hashem created.\[^483] He saw the fact that humans are born with an umbilical cord we need to cut as a model for this broader idea.\[^484]

  **Emulation**  
Work is not just how we continue Hashem’s creation; it is also a way we emulate Him. The *medrash*\[^485] encourages us to follow Hashem’s ways by emulating His first actions when He created the world: planting and developing.

  This is why Rav Kook trembled when he planted a tree. When the settlement of Magdiel celebrated its establishment, Rav Kook participated in the festivities. The ceremony included planting trees, and R’ Kook was given the honor of placing the first sapling in the ground. The organizers handed R’ Kook a hoe to dig the hole, but he threw it aside and began digging with his bare hands instead.

  Suddenly, Rav Kook’s entire body seemed to quiver and shake, and his face looked like a burning flame as he placed the sapling in the ground with awe and trepidation. “What is all this excitement about?” he was asked. “Thank G-d, people plant hundreds of trees every day in Eretz Yisrael!”

  Rav Kook replied, ‘When I held that tender sapling in my hand, I remembered the imperative to emulate G-d and walk in His ways. When I was about to put the sapling in the ground,” Rav Kook explained, “I remembered these words and felt as if I was clinging to the Shechinah. Thus, I was overcome by emotion, fear, and trembling.”\[^486]

  ***Loving*** **Work**  
In addition to being a way to emulate Hashem, work is also part of how we connect to our true selves. We see this from Shemaya’s emphasis on *loving* work. We should not only do work but also love the work we do.\[^487]

  \[clt]We should not only do work but also love the work we do.

  The *gemara*\[^488] tells us that Hashem instills within each of us a love for the type of work we are each meant to be involved in.\[^489] Because work is part of the mission Hashem intends for us in this world, He instills a love of work within us to guide us toward the work He wants us to do and to inspire us to commit ourselves to it. When we work, we realize part of Hashem's destiny for us.\[^490]

  Understandably, Rebbe Tarfon taught that people only die once they find themselves without something constructive to do.\[^491] Sefer Iyov summarized this idea in three words: “*Adam l’amal yulad* — Man is created for the work that he does.”\[^492] When we work, we realize our mission in life. When we are idle, we do not.

  We all need to work hard to support ourselves. It is important to internalize Shemaya’s message that work is not a “necessary evil” but, rather, one of Hashem’s basic expectations. Realizing this can help us approach our work in a way that allows us to enjoy and even love it.

  May internalizing Shemaya’s words help us develop ourselves as complete *ovdei Hashem* who serve Him in all of our endeavors!  
**The Jewish Home**

  יוֹסֵי בֶן יוֹעֶזֶר אִישׁ צְרֵדָה וְיוֹסֵי בֶן יוֹחָנָן אִישׁ יְרוּשָׁלַיִם קִבְּלוּ מֵהֶם.

  יוֹסֵי בֶן יוֹעֶזֶר אִישׁ צְרֵדָה אוֹמֵר, יְהִי בֵיתְךָ בֵית וַעַד לַחֲכָמִים, וֶהֱוֵי מִתְאַבֵּק בַּעֲפַר רַגְלֵיהֶם, וֶהֱוֵי שׁוֹתֶה בְצָמָא אֶת דִּבְרֵיהֶם: (אבות א:ד)

  יוֹסֵי בֶן יוֹחָנָן אִישׁ יְרוּשָׁלַיִם אוֹמֵר, יְהִי בֵיתְךָ פָתוּחַ לִרְוָחָה, וְיִהְיוּ עֲנִיִּים בְּנֵי בֵיתֶךָ, וְאַל תַּרְבֶּה שִׂיחָה עִם הָאִשָּׁה...  (אבות א:ה)

  **The *Zugot***  
After Avos’s first three *mishnayos* list the individuals (Moshe and Yehoshua) and groups (*Zekeinim*, *Nevi’im*, and *Anshei Knesset HaGedolah*) who transmitted the *mesorah*, the *maseches* introduces us to the *zugos —* the pairs who continued this chain. As opposed to earlier and later periods, during the *zugos* period, two people passed on the *mesorah* together.\[^493]

  Maseches Chagigah\[^494] tells us that the first of each pair was the Nasi, while the second was the Av Beis Din. The two led the community by both relaying past *mesorah* and deciding on contemporary matters.

  Rashi\[^495] points out that this period marks the beginning of *machlokes* (disagreement) among the Jewish people. Divergent perspectives, and thus different opinions, developed.

  Despite the differences between them, though, each *zug* shared a certain commonality. We see this in the similar names of the first *zug*: “*Yossi ben* Yoezer *Ish* Tz’redah” and “*Yossi ben* Yochanan *Ish* Yerushalayim\*.\*”

  **The Jewish Home**  
Many of the *zugos* also worked together on a shared topic, which they fleshed out in different ways. The first *zug* teaches us how to build a Jewish home — “*Yehi beis’cha…*”

  We know about the significance of the Jewish home from Bilam’s *berachah*, which we use to open our morning *tefilos*: “*Mah tovu ohalecha Ya’akov*.”\[^496] What makes a home a *good* Jewish home? Which values should it reflect?

  The first *zug* answered these questions by applying two of Shimon Hatzaddik’s seminal values (Torah and *gemilus chasadim*)\[^497] to the context of the home.\[^498] Yossi ben Yoezer speaks about the home as a place of Torah, while Yossi ben Yochanan presents it as a place of *chesed*.

  **A Torah Home (Yossi ben Yoezer)**  
Yossi ben Yoezer’s\[^499] encourages us to be *“mis’avek”* in the dust at the feet of Torah scholars. We should sit at their feet,\[^500] learn their Torah,\[^501] and walk behind them to learn from their ways.\[^502]

  He also encourages us to “drink their words with thirst.” We should utilize Torah teachings to quench the thirst we ought to have for meaning and direction.\[^503]

  As opposed to these two general statements about the value of Torah learning, Yossi ben Yoezer’s opening statement, “May your home be the meeting place for scholars,” connects Torah learning to the home.

  Why is this important?

  Many *meforshim* explain that hosting *shiurim* offers family members the opportunity to learn from the wisdom[^504](Ibid.) and behavior\[^505] of Torah teachers.

  Additionally, we can suggest that Torah events in the home define its mission and purpose — they turn the home into a *makom Torah* (place of Torah). Many types of gatherings and activities take place day and night in homes throughout our communities; we should ensure that our homes host *Torah* gatherings.

  \[clt]Many types of gatherings and activities take place… in homes throughout our communities; we should ensure that our homes host *Torah* gatherings.

  **An Open and Inclusive Home (Yossi ben Yochanan)**  
Yossi ben Yochanan speaks about a different aspect of the home — it serving as a place of *chesed*. He presents this from two perspectives.

  His first statement, “Your home should be wide open,” encourages us to host all those in need. Rabbeinu Yonah\[^506] points to Avraham as a model. The Torah\*\[^507]\* describes how Avraham sat outside his tent looking for guests, ran to invite guests in, and rushed to serve them.\[^508] To identify and encourage the maximum number of visitors, his tent had entrances facing every direction.\[^509] We should learn from Avraham to actively seek, be passionate about, and maximize opportunities to host those in need.

  Yossi ben Yochanan’s second teaching, “The poor should be members of your household,” furthers this idea. One’s home should not just be open to servicing the passerby or those in occasional need; it should also include the poor *as household members.*\[^510] This is important for two reasons. Firstly, some need more than occasional *chesed*; they need consistent support. Secondly, we should include the poor as family members because doing so defines our household as inclusive of those in need.\[^511]

  \[clt]We should include the poor as family members because doing so defines our household as inclusive of those in need.

  **Personal Growth and Care For Others**  
What emerges from the collective teachings of this first *zug* is that Torah and *chesed* should be more than just personal pursuits; they should *define our homes*. Hosting Torah gatherings emphasizes the importance of our family’s Torah identity and personal growth. Actively pursuing guests and including the poor as household members expresses our household’s commitment to the broader community — we value our growth and caring for those who need extra care.

  Let’s make sure to define our homes as places of Torah and *gemilus chasadim*!



  **Building the World: Home and Society**

  What We Need to Survive

  Sustaining Peace

  Stepping Up

  Being on the Team

  Our Social Circles

  The Space Between Us  
**What We Need to Survive**

  רַבָּן שִׁמְעוֹן בֶּן גַּמְלִיאֵל אוֹמֵר, עַל שְׁלשָׁה דְבָרִים הָעוֹלָם עוֹמֵד, עַל הַדִּין וְעַל הָאֱמֶת וְעַל הַשָּׁלוֹם, שֶׁנֶּאֱמַר (זכריה ח) אֱמֶת וּמִשְׁפַּט שָׁלוֹם שִׁפְטוּ בְּשַׁעֲרֵיכֶם: (אבות א:יח)

  **A Second Three**  
Rashbag (Rabban Shimon Ben Gamliel)\[^512] completes Maseches Avos’s opening *perek* by teaching that the world “stands” on three pillars: *din,* *emes,* and *shalom*.\[^513]

  Rashbag’s three pillars remind us of the three pillars of Torah, *avodah*, and *gemilus chasadim,* described earlier in the *perek* by Shimon Hatzaddik.\[^514]

  What is the relationship between the teachings of the two (Rav) Shimons? Clearly, Rashbag, the latter of the two, built upon the formulation of Shimon Hatzaddik. The question is whether he disagrees with the earlier pillars or further develops them.

  The Meiri’s\[^515] version of Rashbag’s statement, “*ha’olam mekayeim* (the world is *sustained* by),*”* highlights a significant difference between Rashbag’s teaching and that of Shimon Hatzaddik, who listed three things upon which the world *stands (omeid)*. Shimon Hatzadik identified the *goals* of the world’s existence; Rashbag explained what the world needs *in order to exist* — what sustains and maintains a functional and healthy society.

  Let us examine Rashag’s three pillars from this perspective.

  ***Emes***  
*Emes* is a critical *midah*. It is one of Hashem’s thirteen attributes\[^516] and what He uses as His seal.\[^517]

  Emes is essential for us as well. Rabbeinu Yonah\[^518] explains that valuing *emes* should inspire us towards constant *teshuvah* and reflection. We know that Hashem stands for truth, and His Torah exemplifies it. We should use His Torah to help and inspire us to serve Hashem with similar honesty.\[^519] We connect between these three (Hashem, the Torah, and our service) truths in the central *berachah* of the Rosh Hashanah davening: “*V’taher libeinu l’avdecha be’emes, ki atah Elokim emes u’devarcha emes v’kayam la’ad.*”\[^520]

  Rabbeinu Yonah adds that *emes* is critical for interpersonal relationships as well. The Torah prohibits lying to others,\[^521] and David HaMelech includes “truth of the heart” as one of his eleven basic (behavioral) principles.\[^522] If we cannot rely on each other, personal relationships and social structures (that depend on these relationships) unravel.



  ***Din***  
Understandably, judges who are responsible for maintaining a healthy, fair, and functional society need to be “men of truth.”\[^523] This attribute gives them the best chance of achieving Rashbag’s first goal — *din* (law/justice). Though they cannot always determine truth and often recommend compromise (instead of truth) to maintain *shalom* (the *mishnah*’s third value), judges should never allow these factors to blur their vision of truth. A society lacking truth as its moral compass inevitably becomes rife with injustice and cynicism.

  Commitment to justice is the basis of Sefer Bereishis’s contrast between Avraham and Sedom. The people of Sedom acted cruelly and unfairly towards others, generating *ze’akah* (cries) and *tze’aka* (shouts).\[^524] Hashem told Avraham about the destruction of Sedom because Avraham intended to teach his family the inverse — “the way of Hashem, (which is) *tzedakah* *u’mishpat* (fairness and justice).”\[^525]

  Hashem chose Avraham because he appreciated what the world needed for its survival. Hashem created our world and entrusted it to us, expecting that we, like Avraham, maintain it properly. Understandably, the *gemara*\[^526] teaches that one who judges fairly is considered Hashem’s partner in creation.

  \[clt]Hashem created our world and entrusted it to us, expecting that we, like Avraham, maintain it properly.

  This partnership is the basis of our eternal closeness to Hashem. Hoshei’a quotes Hashem as declaring that He weds us to Him eternally through the medium of (our pursuit of) justice — *“V'erastich Li l’olam, v’eirastich Li b’tzedek u’v’mishpat.*”\[^527]

  Like Sefer Bereishis, the rest of the Torah also emphasizes the importance of justice. Sefer Shemos tells us that, leading up to *Matan Torah*, Moshe spent his entire day adjudicating \[^528] and Sefer Vayikra\[^529] mandates owning and using accurate weights and measures.

  Sefer Devarim includes Parshas Shoftim — a *parshah* named after the judges we are required to appoint and charge with the mission of “*tzedek tzedek tirdof* (pursue justice).”\[^530] The continuation of the *pasuk* conditions our survival and success in Eretz Yisrael upon this pursuit- “*l’ma’an tichyeh v’horashta es ha’aretz*.”\[^531] The connection is about more than heavenly reward and punishment: an unjust society inevitably disintegrates.

  Eretz Yisrael’s capital city — Yerushalayim — is meant to be Sedom’s foil. This is why Yerushalayim’s kings (even before the Jewish people’s arrival) included the word *tzedek* in their names: Malki-Tzedek was king of Yerushalayim in the days of Avraham,\[^532] and Adoni-Tzedek was king in the time of Yehoshua.\[^533] Yerushalayim was naturally associated with justice.\[^534]

  Understandably, Dovid and Shlomo founded their dynasty in Yerushalayim upon this principle. When Dovid Hamelech first ascended the throne in Yerushalayim, we are told that he was “*oseh mishpat u’tzedakah.”*\[^535] In Sefer Tehillim,\[^536] David linked Yerushalayim’s *achdus* (unity), peace, and security to the courts that ensured justice. Shlomo continued to emphasize this important tradition to the point that it was the most noticeable aspect of his kingdom.\[^537]

  Sadly, later generations of Jews in Yerushalayim and Eretz Yisrael veered from this path. In response, Yeshayahu HaNavi appropriately labeled them “officers of Sedom.”\[^538] A people and city meant to contrast Sedom’s ways ultimately came to emulate them. Yeshayahu quoted Hashem as lamenting that the city He invested with a natural proclivity for justice had become filled with murderers and cheaters.\[^539] This brought about the exile predicted by Parshas Shoftim.

  Yeshayahu prophesized that *ge’ulah* (redemption) will come when we return to the path of justice. At that point, Hashem will restore righteous judges, and Yerushalayim will again be associated with justice. Yirmiyahu\[^540] adds that, at that time, Hashem will restore the Davidic dynasty in its original form of “*mishpat u’tzedakah.”*

  May we use the values of *emes* and *din* to build communities, cities, and a country that embodies the truth and justice Hashem expects from our world!

  The next chapter will discuss the third of Rashbag’s values that sustain the world: *shalom*.  
**Sustaining Peace**

  רַבָּן שִׁמְעוֹן בֶּן גַּמְלִיאֵל אוֹמֵר, עַל שְׁלשָׁה דְבָרִים הָעוֹלָם עוֹמֵד: עַל הַדִּין,וְעַל הָאֱמֶת, וְעַל הַשָּׁלוֹם,  
שֶׁנֶּאֱמַר (זכריה ח) אֱמֶת וּמִשְׁפַּט שָׁלוֹם שִׁפְטוּ בְּשַׁעֲרֵיכֶם (אבות א:יח):

  **A Significant Peace**  
In the last chapter, we learned about *din* and *emes*, the first two values that sustain the world. The third value is *shalom*. Though we value and strive for truth and justice, it is crucial that we also maintain and foster *shalom*. A world that champions only truth and justice is not sustainable.\[^541]

  The Torah concludes *Birkas Kohanim* with *shalom.*\[^542] Chazal followed suit by concluding “all of our *tefilos*” (including *Shemoneh Esrei*, *Bentching,* and *Kaddish)* the same way.\[^543] Shas Mishnayos\[^544] also ends with *shalom*, which it celebrates as the “vessel” that receives and contains Hashem’s *berachah*.

  Chazal teach us that *shalom* is equal in significance to the *entire* creation and the combined importance of all that exists;\[^545] “Without it, we have nothing.”\[^546]

  *Shalom* is important to both man and G-d. Man needs peace to survive, thrive, and build society. Hashem also prioritizes *shalom*. He chose it as one of His names and gave us the Torah only once we were at peace with one another.\[^547] Chazal go even further and assert that when we live peacefully with one another, Hashem “*cannot*” (!) cause us to be defeated in war (even if we are worshiping idols).\[^548]

  **Pursuing Peace**  
Because of its importance, *shalom* should be *actively pursued*. Sefer Tehillim formulates it this way: “Seek and pursue peace.”\[^549] Most *mitzvos* are situational. If we happen upon eggs under a mother bird, we send away the mother before taking the eggs. If we choose to build a house, we put a mezuzah on it. *Shalom*, though, is something we need to seek out proactively.\[^550]

  \[clt] *Shalom*, though, is something we need to seek out proactively.

  Earlier in Avos’s first *perek*,\[^551] Hillel identifies Aharon HaKohen as a model of how to love and pursue peace. Aharon worked tirelessly to restore peace between spouses and between friends. He would help them appreciate one another and realize that the other also appreciated them.\[^552] Understandably, when Aharon died, the *entire* Jewish people deeply mourned his loss.\[^553]

  **Sacrificing For Peace**  
Pursuing peace is so important that we sacrifice other significant values to achieve it. Hashem Himself set this standard. To restore peace between husband and wife, the Torah commands a *sotah* (a woman suspected of infidelity) to drink water mixed with dirt, which miraculously clarifies her fidelity.\[^554] To show the importance of this goal, Hashem mandated erasing *His holy Name* as part of the process.\[^555] Hashem could have arranged for the test to work without this erasure; by requiring it, He taught us the great value of *shalom*.\[^556]

  Chazal learned from and expanded upon this precedent. To generate peace, they instituted using the word “*shalom”* as a friendly greeting, despite it being Hashem’s holy Name.\[^557] Though the Aseres Hadibros (Ten Commandments) prohibit uttering Hashem’s name in vain,\[^558] Chazal instituted using His name to greet each other because of *shalom*’s importance. In truth, we are not using His name *in vain*; we use it to *bring peace*.\[^559]

  Though also one of the world’s pillars, we (sometimes) sacrfice even *emes* for *shalom*‘s sake. Chazal derive this from the fact that Hashem lied to Avraham (about Sarah’s reaction to the news of her upcoming pregnancy), and Yosef’s brothers lied to him (about Yaakov’s request to forgive them) to sustain peace.\[^560]

  This is also why Beis Shamay and Beis Hillel married “across party lines” despite their disagreements concerning issues of marital status. Though both groups firmly believed in the truth of their respective opinions (about issues including marital status), they avoided separating from one another. The *gemara*\[^561] explains that their actions fulfilled Zecharyah HaNavi’s mandate to \*“\*love truth and peace.”\[^562] Beis Shamay and Beis Hillel loved *emes*, but they loved *shalom* as well.

  We should learn from them not to allow our commitment to truth to keep us from living in peace with one another as one people.

  **Higher Peace**  
The Akeidas Yitzchak\[^563] and Rav Kook\[^564] explain that *shalom* includes more than just the absence of hostility. The fullest sense of the word implies people appreciating and working together with others. As opposed to the word “peace,” which (derives from the Roman word “*pax*” and) is related to the word “appeasement,” the Hebrew word “*shalom*” (like the related word “*shalem*”) means “whole” or “complete.” “*Shalom*” describes a situation where the entire Jewish people (and the whole world) live and work together as part of a unified creation.

  As we saw in an earlier chapter, Rav Kook applies this to the goals described by Shimon Hatzaddik at the beginning of Avos’s first perek.\[^565] He explains that those involved in *gemilus chasadim* and those focused on *avodah* should appreciate one another’s endeavors. Additionally, both of them should respect and work with the theorists — those involved in Torah study. In this way, Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel’s statement (at the end of the *perek*) complements Shimon Hatzaddik’s earlier one.

  **Two Pieces**  
*Emes* and *shalom* are both critical. We see this from the *mishnah*‘s conclusion, which quotes a *pasuk* from Zecharyah\[^566] that mandates including both within our judicial system. This is why Jewish courts employ both *din* and *pesharah* (compromise).

  Despite the value of both, Rav Yehoshua ben Karcha learns from *the same pasuk* that *pesharah* should be the *preferred* option.\[^567] Though we value truth, we prefer reaching a decision that will foster peace.

  May this mishnah help us prioritize peace in our personal and national spheres!

  **Stepping Up**

  הוּא (הלל) הָיָה אוֹמֵר… בִמְקוֹם שֶׁאֵין אֲנָשִׁים, הִשְׁתַּדֵּל לִהְיוֹת אִישׁ (אבות ב:ה):

  We often find ourselves in situations where no one is ready to step up and take responsibility. In these situations, “where there is no other,” Hillel encourages us to be the *ish,* the person who takes responsibility and does what is needed.\[^568]

  The *meforshim* apply Hillel’s words to different types of situations.

  **Responsibility for Ourselves**  
According to the Rambam,\[^569] the *mishnah* refers to Torah learning and personal growth. Though we should seek *rebbeim* to teach and guide us, we must also be ready to teach and guide ourselves. We should view ourselves as capable of\[^570] and responsible for developing ourselves.\[^571] Though many others teach and inspire us, ultimately, “*Im ein ani li, mi li* — if I am not responsible for myself, who will be?”\[^572]

  The Meiri and Medrash Shmuel add another aspect to this sense of personal responsibility. They understand that Hillel teaches us to act properly even when no one else is around to see our actions. Our internal compass and commitment to Hashem should be strong enough to guide us without our needing positive reinforcement.\[^573]

  **Communal Role**  
Other *meforshim* understand Hillel’s words as a call to contribute to the broader community; when no one else volunteers for a communal role, we should fill the void. The Bartenura\[^574] gives the example of teaching Torah: When Torah teaching is needed, we should step up to teach.\[^575]

  Torah leaders have shown this sense of communal responsibility throughout the ages.

  Hillel himself personified his own words. When Hillel arrived in Israel and found no one else proficient in the laws of *kodshim,* he assumed the position of Nasi in order to teach them.\[^576]

  Centuries later, the *Amora* Rav followed this tradition. After passing through Sura and hearing a woman ask her friend how much milk to use when cooking meat, Rav decided to *move there* and open a *yeshiva* to educate the townspeople.\[^577] Rav already headed a *yeshiva* in Neharda’a but also felt responsible for Sura.

  One thousand seven hundred years later, Rav Aharon Kotler took a similar step. During the Second World War, Rav Aharon found himself in Shanghai with the Mir Yeshiva, unsure whether to proceed to Israel or America. He did a *goral ha’GRA* (a system that identifies a *pasuk* meant to guide us), which fell upon Hashem’s instruction to Aharon to meet and “help Moshe in the desert\*.\*”\[^578] Rav Aharon saw it as a directive to join Rav Moshe Feinstein in America, a country whose desperate need for Torah teachers made it like a desert wasteland. Though Israel was his preferred Torah destination, Rav Aharon accepted the mission to travel to America. When he arrived, he opened the Lakewood Yeshiva, which has played a central role in spreading Torah across America.

  **Leadership**  
Hillel’s words apply to other forms of leadership as well.\[^579] We should be the *ish* who volunteers for important missions not being addressed.\[^580]

  Moshe Rabbeinu was a model of such leadership. When he encountered an Egyptian assaulting a Jew, he “looked both ways and saw *ki ein ish*  — there was no man.*”*\[^581] Though (presumably) there were many other people at the building site (and we know that others found out about his action), no one intervened. As opposed to the others, who were afraid to act or did not see taking action as their responsibility, Moshe Rabbeinu stepped up and saved the Jew by killing the Egyptian, even though he realized that this action might force him to flee Egypt.

  Rabbi Jonathan Sacks develops the importance of this type of leadership:  
Leadership is where a person takes responsibility where others are not present… Leadership is born when we become active, not passive, when we do not wait for someone else to act because perhaps there is no one else — at least not here, not now.  
When bad things happen, some avert their eyes. Some wait for others to act. Some blame others for failing to act. Some simply complain. But there are some people who say, “If something is wrong, let me try to put it right.” They are the leaders. They are the ones who make a difference in their lifetimes. They are the ones who make ours a better world…  
A responsible life is a life that responds by taking responsibility.”\[^582]

  \[clt]“Leadership is where a person takes responsibility where others are not present.”

  Many of our ancestors have exemplified this trait. Nachshon ben Aminadav was an excellent example. Bnei Yisrael stood at the banks of the Yam Suf, hemmed in from behind by the Egyptians. Hashem told them to move forward into the sea, but the people were afraid. Nachshon saw that no one else was moving forward and led the way with his leap of faith.

  Many fine non-Jews have also taken such initiatives. An excellent example were the almost thirty thousand “righteous gentiles” who saved Jews during the Holocaust. These outstanding people, recognized by Israel as “*Chassidei Umos Ha’Olam*,” stepped up to assist their Jewish neighbors who had no one else to turn to. Like Moshe Rabbeinu, these righteous gentiles were willing to risk their own lives to help those no one else was willing to assist.

  **Being a Builder**  
Though our first responsibility is to ourselves, Hillel teaches us that we should also feel responsible for the broader community and world. Rebbe Elazar quoted Rebbe Chanina\[^583] who inferred this point from the *pasuk:* “*v’chol banayich limudei Hashem, v’rav shalom banayich.*”\[^584] Rebbe Chanina understood the word *banayich* (sons) as *bonayich* (builders) and explained that *talmidei chachamim* should aim to build the world. We grow from the status of *banayich* to that of *bonayich* or *ish* when we appreciate the role we are meant to play as builders.\[^585]

  May we be inspired by Hillel’s words to take responsibility for ourselves and for others in situations where we are needed!  
**Being On The Team**

  הִלֵּל אוֹמֵר, *אַל תִּפְרֹשׁ מִן הַצִּבּוּר*, וְאַל תַּאֲמִין בְּעַצְמְךָ עַד יוֹם מוֹתְךָ, וְאַל תָּדִין אֶת חֲבֵרְךָ עַד שֶׁתַּגִּיעַ לִמְקוֹמוֹ, וְאַל תֹּאמַר דָּבָר שֶׁאִי אֶפְשָׁר לִשְׁמֹעַ, שֶׁסּוֹפוֹ לְהִשָּׁמַע. וְאַל תֹּאמַר לִכְשֶׁאִפָּנֶה אֶשְׁנֶה, שֶׁמָּא לֹא תִפָּנֶה: (אבות ב:ה)

  **The Damage of Distance**  
The fifth *mishnah* of Avos’s second *perek* quotes five warnings issued by Hillel. The first cautions against separating from the *tzibur* (community).\[^586]

  The Rambam sees such separation as uniquely severe and presents the separatist as losing his portion in *Olam Haba*.*\[^587]* The Rambam adds that separating from the community is one of the five things that block the path to *teshuvah* because the separatist misses the opportunity (to be inspired) to do *teshuvah* together with the community.\[^588]

  Rabbeinu Yonah\[^589] adds that the separatist also causes others to disrespect Jewish values. Even if he himself actually respects these values, his separation from the community implies that he rejects their values.

  Conversely, the Maharal\[^590] explains that one connected to the *tzibur* benefits from the *“koach ha’tzibur”* — the community’s unique strength and eternal destiny.

  Many also see the *tzibur*’s unity as having *ontological* significance. The Ritva\[^591] and the Maharal\[^592] use this to explain the Torah’s juxtaposition of the prohibition against sectorial subdivision\*\[^593]\* to the words “*banim atem la’Hashem Elokeichem.”* As the children of G-d, we should represent His oneness with our own. When we separate from the *tzibur*, we imply godly divisiveness (*chas v’shalom*).

  Rav Kook took this further by comparing a separatist to the woman who was willing to accept Shlomo HaMelech’s proposal to cut a disputed baby in half.\[^594] Like a physical human being, the Jewish people are an organic whole and must remain unified.\[^595] Denial of our unity is tantamount to denial of the existence of our organic entity.

  **Through Thick…**  
The Rishonim identify the times when it is *most important* to emphasize our connection with the *tzibur*. The Rambam\[^596] and Rabbeinu Yonah\[^597] focus on community gatherings for *mitzvah* observance. Mass fulfillment of Hashem’s Will glorifies His presence; everyone should join.\[^598]

  **…And Thin**  
The Rambam also mentions times of *tzarah* (distress).\[^599] The Meiri\[^600] explains that even one able to save himself should endeavor to save the broader community. He references the words of Mordechai to Esther: “Don’t think that you are safe in the king’s palace. If you are quiet at this moment (and do not help the Jews), the Jews will be saved another way, and you and your family will be the ones lost.”\[^601]

  In addition to offering assistance, one should also empathize. The *gemara*\[^602] teaches that those who do not identify with the community’s suffering will be excluded from their eventual consolation.\[^603] This is why Moshe Rabbeinu chose a stone (as opposed to a pillow) to sit on while holding his arms up during the war with Amalek. Moshe did not want to feel comfortable while the community felt distress.*\[^604]*

  Moshe Rabbeinu already demonstrated this *midah* at the *beginning* of Sefer Shemos, where the Torah describes him as “seeing” the Jewish people’s suffering (in addition to their persecution).\[^605] Rashi[^606](Ibid.) explains that “seeing their suffering” means that “his eyes and heart sympathized with them.” The *medrash* adds that this sympathy motivated Moshe to actually help them carry their loads.\[^607] This merited Hashem’s show of *similar empathy* (in the very next *perek*) when he chose specifically a thorn bush within which to appear to Moshe.\[^608] Like Moshe, Hashem also identified with the Jewish People’s pain.

  **Don’t Daven Divided**  
Rabbeinu Bachaya adds a third area — *tefilah*. Communal prayer generates heavenly goodwill\[^609] and allows *even a rasha’s* prayers to be accepted.\[^610] For this reason, even when unable to get to shul, we should at least daven at the *same time* as the community.\[^611]

  The Zohar\[^612] explains that the *Ishah HaShunamis* took this even further. Her response to Elisha, who asked if he could request something on her behalf, was *“besoch ami ani yosheves.”\[^613]* The Zohar explains that it was Rosh Hashanah and Elisha was asking if he could *daven* for her (as she was barren and prayers for barren women are answered on Rosh Hashanah). She responded that she did not want anyone to daven for her specifically; she preferred to be *davened* for *as part of the Jewish People*.

  We, too, express this idea by formulating our prayers in plural.\[^614] We daven not specifically for ourselves but for all those who need what we need.

  \[clt]We daven not specifically for ourselves but for all those who need what we need.

  Even when we pray on behalf of individuals, we pray for them as part of the broader community. After we mention the names of those sick in the Mi Shebeirach we recite on their behalf, we add the words *“b’soch sha’ar cholei Yisrael* (amongst other sick Jews). Similarly, when we console mourners, we pray that Hashem consoles them “among the other mourners for Zion and Yerushalayim.” When we petition Hashem on behalf of individuals, we include them as part of the broader *tzibur*.

  **Individuality, Not Individualism**  
We live in a world that emphasizes individualism. Judaism, in contrast, values individuality but not individualism. Rabbi Jonathan Sacks *zt”l* explains that there is “all the difference in the world between individuality and individualism. Individuality means that I am a unique and valued member of a team. Individualism means that I am not a team player at all. I am interested in myself alone, not the group…  Judaism values individuality, not individualism. As Hillel said, ‘If I am only for myself, what am I?’\[^615]

  May Hillel’s warning inspire us to ensure that developing our unique individuality facilitates a stronger connection to and appreciation of the broader Jewish community!  
**Our Social Circles**

  יְהוֹשֻׁעַ בֶּן פְּרַחְיָה וְנִתַּאי הָאַרְבֵּלִי קִבְּלוּ מֵהֶם:  
יְהוֹשֻׁעַ בֶּן פְּרַחְיָה אוֹמֵר: עֲשֵׂה לְךָ רַב, וּקְנֵה לְךָ חָבֵר, וֶהֱוֵי דָן אֶת כָל הָאָדָם לְכַף זְכוּת: (אבות א:ו)  
נִתַּאי הָאַרְבֵּלִי אוֹמֵר: הַרְחֵק מִשָּׁכֵן רָע, וְאַל תִּתְחַבֵּר לָרָשָׁע, וְאַל תִּתְיָאֵשׁ מִן הַפֻּרְעָנוּת: (אבות א:ז)

  **Our Relationships**  
The second of the first *perek’s* *zugos* (the pairs who transmitted the *mesorah*) were Yehoshua ben Perachya and Nitai Ha’Arbeli. Both address the same topic — a person's social circle. Yehoshua ben Perachya identifies those we should seek to develop relationships with, while Nitai Ha’Arbeli tells us who to avoid.\[^616]

  **The Good and The Better**  
From the beginning, the Torah emphasizes the importance of our interpersonal relationships. After telling us (many times) that Hashem saw His creations as *good*, *Perek Beis* introduces, for the first time, something *not good*: *“Lo tov heyos Adam levado* — It is *not good* for man to be alone.”*\[^617]* Living alone is unnatural and morally wrong.

  The fate of the world’s first generations teaches us how destructive living only for oneself can be. The pre-*mabul* generations lived selfishly\*,\* with no care for one another. They raped, murdered, and trampled each other. Their destruction of society impacted the whole world and “forced” Hashem to restart the world. Our world can only survive when we transcend our natural selfishness and care for one another.

  \[clt]Our world can only survive when we transcend our natural selfishness and care for one another.

  Friendship is not just our responsibility towards others; it is also crucial for our own healthy development. Sefer Koheles gives two reasons why “two are better than one.”\[^618]

  The first reason is utilitarian: “When one falls, the other can help him up.”\[^619] Having a friend means having someone to turn to for help in our time of need. In addition, two people can help each other accomplish more than either could have achieved on their own. There is also a spiritual benefit. Friends help each other grow by offering constructive criticism and guidance about how to improve.\[^620]

  Koheles’s second reason relates to the importance of the relationship itself: “Even if both friends fall and are unable to help each other up, at least they can warm (console) one another.”\[^621] Hashem created human beings with the need to share our lives with other people,\[^622] to the extent that many people prefer death over loneliness.\[^623]

  Appreciating the great significance of friends and friendship, Yehoshua ben Perachya encourages us to create a relationship with a *rav* and to acquire a friend. Our *rav* helps us determine our path; our friends provide a context in which we can share and grow.

  \[clt]Our *rav* helps us determine our path; our friends provide a context in which we can share and grow.

  **Choosing Friends Well**  
Though Yehoshua ben Perachya mentions “acquiring a friend” without specifying the type, realizing the significant impact our friends have upon us should cause us to choose our friends carefully.

  The Rambam introduces his codification of our *mishnah* by emphasizing that\*“\*man is innately drawn towards and impacted by the ways of his friends.”\[^624] It is critical to surround ourselves with people who share our core values because our values will inevitably reflect those of our closest friends.

  This is why Nitai Ha’Arbeili (in the second part of our *mishnah*) adds that we should distance ourselves from bad friends and neighbors. Friends have a major impact on our life’s path. We need to choose them carefully.

  Our choice of friends is even more critical today, where social media connects us with thousands of virtual “friends” who determine the content and perspectives we are exposed to. Though we are not commanded to live in an echo chamber, and we can thoughtfully choose to expose ourselves to different perspectives, we should ensure that our inner circle consists of friends who reinforce our values.

  **Four Levels of Friends**  
The Rambam delineates four levels of friendship:\[^625]  
1) Those who share a common need or challenge which they address together — a utilitarian friendship.  
2) Those who share common tastes and get enjoyment from seeking and benefiting from pleasures together.  
3) Those who can trust each other with sensitive information. A partner in such a friendship is a “real” friend whom the Torah describes as “*rei’eihu*.”\[^626]  
4) The highest level of friendship is an *“ohev ma’alah”* — a friend with whom one shares the same sense of mission. This shared purpose allows people to work together to realize common goals.

  Ideally, *all Jews* should have this fourth type of relationship with one another. Our common understanding of Hashem having placed us in this world to serve Him should allow us to transcend our narrow needs and interests and work together to sanctify His name.

  This is why we begin the *Sheva Berachos* (we recite at a wedding) with the *berachah* of Shehakol Bara Lichvodo (All was created for His honor). At first glance, the *berachah* seems unrelated to a wedding. What does a marriage have to do with why Hashem created the world?

  The Rambam’s description of friendship’s highest level can help us understand why this *berachah* is foundational to Jewish marriage. Marriage is challenging because it expects two people who grew up separately and focused on themselves and their own self-realization to join together and care for one another as extensions of themselves. How is this possible? How should the bride and groom get there?

  The *berachah* of Shehakol Bara Lichvodo opens the Sheva Berachot with the answer. A healthy marriage begins with recognizing our life’s mission (like that of the rest of the world) as glorifying Hashem’s Name. This recognition makes it possible for the couple to transcend their (naturally) selfish selves and build a meaningful relationship — one in which they work together on their joint goal of *Avodas Hashem*.

  **How To Acquire**  
How does one develop meaningful friendships? The Rambam[^627](Ibid.) explains that one acquires friends by acting towards others in a way that *the other person* will appreciate. People naturally see the world through their own lens; forging relationships with others hinges on our ability to put ourselves in *their* shoes.

  This explains the connection between Yehoshua ben Perechaya’s third statement, “*Hevei dan es kol ha’Adam l’kaf zechut* (Judge others favorably),” and the first part of the *mishnah*. Judging others favorably is important in its own right, but also because it helps us forge friendships. Seeing others’ actions favorably hinges upon seeing things from their perspective; doing so brings us closer together and makes friendship possible.

  We live in a world where we interact with more people more quickly than in the past, but from a virtual distance. We should appreciate the value and importance of healthy, meaningful friendships and invest the extra effort required to develop them. Additionally, we should appreciate the importance of maintaining our relationship with our *rabbanim*, who can continue guiding us throughout our lives.  
**The Space Between Us**

  רַבִּי חֲנִינָא סְגַן הַכֹּהֲנִים אוֹמֵר, הֱוֵי מִתְפַּלֵּל בִּשְׁלוֹמָהּ שֶׁל מַלְכוּת, שֶׁאִלְמָלֵא מוֹרָאָהּ, *אִישׁ אֶת רֵעֵהוּ* חַיִּים בְּלָעוֹ.

  רַבִּי חֲנִינָא בֶן תְּרַדְיוֹן אוֹמֵר:  
שְׁנַיִם שֶׁיּוֹשְׁבִין וְאֵין בֵּינֵיהֶן דִּבְרֵי תוֹרָה, הֲרֵי זֶה מוֹשַׁב לֵצִים, שֶׁנֶּאֱמַר (תהלים א) וּבְמוֹשַׁב לֵצִים לֹא יָשָׁב.  
אֲבָל שְׁנַיִם שֶׁיּוֹשְׁבִין וְיֵשׁ בֵּינֵיהֶם דִּבְרֵי תוֹרָה, שְׁכִינָה שְׁרוּיָה בֵינֵיהֶם, שֶׁנֶּאֱמַר (מלאכי ג) אָז נִדְבְּרוּ יִרְאֵי ה' *אִישׁ אֶל רֵעֵהוּ* וַיַּקְשֵׁב ה' וַיִּשְׁמָע וַיִּכָּתֵב סֵפֶר זִכָּרוֹן לְפָנָיו לְיִרְאֵי ה' וּלְחֹשְׁבֵי שְׁמוֹ… (אבות ג:ב)

  The second *mishnah* of Avos’s third *perek* quotes two seemingly unrelated statements in the name of two Rebbe Chaninas. Why are the two statements placed together? Are they connected by anything more than the shared first name of those quoted?

  **The Interpersonal**  
Looking more carefully at the two statements, we notice that both include the same three words — “*ish es rei’eihu* (man with his fellow).*”* This is because both statements address interpersonal relationships.

  Though people are meant to assist and enrich each other’s lives, our natural selfishness and competitiveness often make relationships tense and even hurtful. These two *mishnayos* teach how to avoid negative relationships and forge positive ones.

  The *mishnah* opens with RCSH’s (Rebbe Chanina S’gan HaKohanim) encouragement to pray on behalf of the government. Without their authority, society descends into anarchy where people “swallow each other alive.” We should pray that we have a strong government that can effectively keep people from abusing one another.

  RCBT’s (Rebbe Chanina ben Teradyon) statement, quoted immediately after RCSH’s, teaches how to create the ideal interpersonal relationship that *even* Hashem’s *Shechina* resides within. He explains that when people include Torah as a basic part of their relationships and meetings, Hashem joins and even records their words in a special “book (!).”

  Though the idea of Hashem’s *Shechina* residing among people learning together appears later in this *perek,*\[^628] our *mishnah* also mentions it to highlight the impact Torah learning has on our relationships. RCBT’s usage of the term *“yesh beineihem divrei Torah”* to describe the context of the two people’s Torah learning emphasizes this point.\[^629] He aims to depict not just two people learning but specifically the Torah presence “between them.” Hashem is present and records their Torah because Torah is what the two share. It is their bond, which “fills the space between them.”\[^630]

  **Meeting Without Torah**  
RCBT also relates to the flipside — the problematic nature of meetings that lack Torah content. Sharing Torah at meetings is not just ideal; it is *critical*. Without it, the gathering is a “*moshav leitzim* (meeting of scoffers).*”* Why is a meeting without Torah content so problematic?\[^631]

  Some\[^632] explain that when meetings lack a Torah context, they inevitably gravitate to inappropriate or meaningless content.\[^633] The Rambam\[^634] describes the process this way: “Conversations may initially concern neutral (meaningless) matters, but the topic often moves to negativity about other people and eventually towards even Hashem Himself.” The Rambam concludes by contrasting this with *“sichas kashrei Yisrael* (the conversation of kosher Jews)” which focuses on Torah and wisdom.\[^635]

  This answer explains the juxtaposition of our *mishnah* to the *perek’s* first *mishnah*. The first *mishnah* encourages reflecting upon the nature of our existence in order to avoid sin\*.\* Our *mishnah* adds that avoiding sin is not enough. We need to involve ourselves in positive content. Spiritual “nature” (like its physical counterpart) abhors a vacuum. If we do not fill our time with *chochmah*, it will inevitably become filled with negative content.\[^636]

  \[clt]Avoiding sin is not enough. We need to involve ourselves in positive content.

  **What We Desire**  
We can take the *mishnah*’s message a step further: a meeting lacking Torah content is problematic, not only because of the eventual negative consequence but in and of itself. We see this in the proof text quoted by mishnah:\[^637] *“U’v’moshav leitzim lo yashav* (he did not sit with scoffers).” How does this *pasuk*, which merely encourages avoiding a *moshav leitzim,* prove that a meeting without Torah is considered one?

  Many *meforshim\[^638]* explain that the proof is from the continuation of the *pasuk*: “*ki im b’Torat Hashem cheftzo* (rather, he desires Hashem’s Torah).”[^639](Ibid.) Both parts of the *pasuk* together make an important point. We should avoid meaningless meetings because we desire Torah learning so much that we cannot bear to waste the time we could spend learning.

  Additionally, a person passionate about Torah will naturally share Torah with anyone he meets with. The Chassid Yavetz and the Sefas Emes compare such a person to one given the opportunity to keep whatever gold coins he manages to count out over a limited period of time. A person who squanders the opportunity by wasting his time clearly does not appreciate the value of gold. So, too, a person who truly values Torah will take full advantage of the opportunity to speak in Torah with anyone they meet.\[^640]

  May our mishnah inspire us to enhance and enrich our friendships and meetings with Torah learning!  
**Personal Development**

  **The Straight Path**

  **Mitzvah Observance**  
**The Straight Path**

  On The Straight and Narrow

  How Straight Is Beautiful

  How We Get There  
**On The Straight and Narrow**

  רַבִּי אוֹמֵר, *אֵיזוֹהִי דֶרֶךְ יְשָׁרָה שֶׁיָּבוֹר לוֹ הָאָדָם?*  
כֹּל שֶׁהִיא תִפְאֶרֶת לְעוֹשֶׂיהָ וְתִפְאֶרֶת לוֹ מִן הָאָדָם. וֶהֱוֵי זָהִיר בְּמִצְוָה קַלָּה כְּבַחֲמוּרָה, שֶׁאֵין אַתָּה יוֹדֵעַ מַתַּן שְׂכָרָן שֶׁל מִצְוֹת. וֶהֱוֵי מְחַשֵּׁב הֶפְסֵד מִצְוָה כְּנֶגֶד שְׂכָרָהּ, ושְׂכַר עֲבֵרָה כְּנֶגֶד הֶפְסֵדָהּ. (אבות ב:א)

  אָמַר לָהֶם (רַבָּן יוֹחָנָן בֶּן זַכַּאי), צְאוּ וּרְאוּ *אֵיזוֹהִי דֶרֶךְ יְשָׁרָה שֶׁיִּדְבַּק בָּהּ הָאָדָם?*  
רַבִּי אֱלִיעֶזֶר אוֹמֵר, עַיִן טוֹבָה. רַבִּי יְהוֹשֻׁעַ אוֹמֵר, חָבֵר טוֹב. רַבִּי יוֹסֵי אוֹמֵר, שָׁכֵן טוֹב. רַבִּי שִׁמְעוֹן אוֹמֵר, הָרוֹאֶה אֶת הַנּוֹלָד. רַבִּי אֶלְעָזָר אוֹמֵר, לֵב טוֹב. אָמַר לָהֶם, רוֹאֶה אֲנִי אֶת דִּבְרֵי אֶלְעָזָר בֶּן עֲרָךְ מִדִּבְרֵיכֶם, שֶׁבִּכְלָל דְּבָרָיו דִּבְרֵיכֶם…(אבות ב:ט)

  Pirkei Avos quotes two *Tannaim* who both asked the same question: *“Eizo he derech yeshara* — What is the straight path?” The question was first asked by Ribaz (Rabban Yochanan ben Zakkai),\[^641] who lived at the time of the *churban Beis Hamikdash*, and then again by Rebbe (Rebbe Yehudah HaNassi), who lived 150 years later. It seems like the question’s importance caused it to be discussed over the course of many generations.\[^642]

  **The Important and Consistent Choice**  
Before studying the *Tannaim*’s answers, we should note the difference in their formulations of the question. Rebbe speaks of “choosing” the straight path. Interestingly, instead of the word “*yivchar,*” which the Torah generally uses to connote choosing,\[^643] Rebbe uses the word “*yavor*.” This may be an allusion to the *pasuk* in *Koheles* that uses the same root when referring to *G-d’s choice of man*.\[^644] If this inference is correct, Rebbe’s implication is that because G-d has chosen us, we too must be mindful of our life choices. Hashem created man in His image, and we — the Jewish People — are his children. Our lives are significant, and we should live them properly.

  \[clt]Rebbe’s implication is that because G-d has chosen us, we too must be mindful of our life choices.

  Instead of the word “*yavor,*” Ribaz uses the term “*sheyidbak* (that he should stick to)” to describe man’s choice. Ribaz emphasizes the need for our choices and life paths to be consistent. Though different paths offer various benefits, it is crucial to live coherently. We do so by “sticking” to one path.

  **The Straight Path**  
Both *Tannaim* assume the importance of seeking the *“derech yesharah* (straight path).” Both these words — “*derech*” and *“yesharah”* — are significant.

  The phrase *derech* appears in Parshas Vayera where Hashems approved of Avraham’s intention to teach his children His “*derech”* of how to live in this world.*\[^645]* Hashem referred to more than just fulfilling a specific *mitzvah* or performing a particular good deed. He approved of Avraham’s *way of life*. While Vayera used the term “*derech*” to refer to acting  just kindly, the *Tannaim* ask a broader question: what attitude and goal enable us to make correct life decisions?

  The second word, “*yesharah* (straight),” sharpens the question’s formulation and directs us toward the answer. Our goal should be the straight path.

  The Torah commands us to emulate Hashem (whom Tanach describes as “straight”\[^646]) by living in a way He sees as straight: “*V’asisa ha’**yashar** v’hatov b’einei Hashem.”*\[^647] The *medrash* adds that Hashem created man in His image *solely* so that man could be *yashar* like Hashem!\[^648] Conversely, the Netziv adds that Hashem is the model of straightness and does not tolerate crooked people.\[^649] The Tannaim assume that we should aim to live on the straight path because it is a condition for a relationship with Hashem and thus an indication of how we are meant to live.

  **A Central Goal**  
*“Yashrut* — straightness” is a central goal of personal growth. This is why the Rambam asserts that most *mitzvos* are Hashem’s “advice” on fixing our perspectives and *straightening out* our lives.\[^650]

  Understandably, Rav Kook defines the ultimate objective of *chinuch* (education) as guiding individuals toward their correct “form,” which begins with being “good and straight.”\[^651] This is why the Ramchal called his *mussar sefer* Mesilas Yesharim — The Path of the Straight. The sefer aims to help people identify, reach, and persist upon the straight path.

  **Our Natural State**  
*“Yashrus*” is not an external value we need to transcend ourselves to achieve; it is our original, natural state. Sefer Koheles teaches that Hashem creates man naturally “*yashar.*” Man’s schemes and calculations cause him to veer from this innate straight path.\[^652]

  This explains why Rav Yochanan\[^653] understood the term “*Sefer Hayashar”*\[^654] as referring to Sefer Bereishis — “the book of Avraham, Yitzchak and Yaakov, who are considered *yesharim.”* Because *yashrus* is man’s natural state, even before the giving of the Torah, our Avos were able to live their lives this way.

  Thus, Rebbe and Ribaz merely asked how to sustain the natural *yashrus* with which we are all born.

  The next chapter presents their answers.  
**How Straight is Beautiful**

  רַבִּי אוֹמֵר, אֵיזוֹהִי דֶרֶךְ יְשָׁרָה שֶׁיָּבוֹר לוֹ הָאָדָם?  
כֹּל שֶׁהִיא תִפְאֶרֶת לְעוֹשֶׂיהָ וְתִפְאֶרֶת לוֹ מִן הָאָדָם… (אבות ב:א)

  As we saw in the last chapter, Rebbe Yehudah HaNasi asks: “What is the straight path that a person should choose?” His answer focuses on one word: “*tiferes*.” *Tiferes* means more than beauty; it connotes a harmonious balance between divergent ideas or components.\[^655] What different components should comprise the *tiferes* of one’s life path?

  Rebbe’s answer delineates two types of *tiferes*: “*l’oseha*” and “*min ha’Adam*.” Let us explore each of these.

  **Min Ha’Adam**  
*“Tiferes lo min ha’**Adam**”* means that others perceive our path as beautiful. We are all subjective and naturally view our life path positively.\[^656] Others seeing it this way is critical (more) objective confirmation.

  Many of *Avos’s mishnayos* emphasize the importance of how others perceive us. The fourth *perek*\[^657] quotes Rebbe Shimon, who rates the “crown” of a “good name” as greater than all other crowns.

  Rav Chanina Ben Dosa\[^658] goes even further by asserting that Hashem is pleased only with those other people are also pleased with. One who only performs *mitzvos bein Adam l’Makom* and focuses solely upon his relationship with Hashem not only lacks the approval of other people; he lacks Hashem’s approval as well.\[^659]

  \[clt]One who only performs *mitzvos bein Adam l’Makom* … not only lacks the approval of other people; he lacks Hashem’s approval as well.

  **Oseha**  
But this is only Rebbe’s second stated criteria; his first one is: *“tiferes l’oseha*.*”* Who is the “*oseha”* that Rebbe refers to?

  Most *meforshim\[^660]* understand the word *oseha* as referring to the person himself. According to this explanation, Rebbe emphasizes that, in addition to others’ appreciation, our path needs to resonate with our *own selves*. The way we live needs to be a true expression of our unique character and destiny.

  Rebbe’s complete statement thus teaches that our life needs to be beautiful to both us and to others.

  **Balance — The Middle Path**  
Many Rishonim\[^661] understand Rebbe as addressing an additional issue: the *balance* between ourselves and others. There is a natural tension between our focus on our own needs and aspirations versus our efforts to assist and care for others. Rebbe’s point is that we must strike the right balance between these often competing values.

  The Rambam\[^662] gives the example of the need to find the middle ground between being miserliness and altruism. Though we are obliged to be charitable, we should prioritize caring for ourselves and our families. The Rambam calls this the *“derech ha’emtzai* — the balanced path, "\[^663] one which appreciates and integrates both ideals harmoniously.\[^664] This, explains the Rambam, is what Rebbe means when he says that the straight path is beautiful both for oneself and others.\[^665]

  ***Osehu***  
The problem with this interpretation is that the word *oseha* is a strange way to describe  oneself. For this reason, many *meforshim*\[^666] prefer a different *girsa* (textual version) of the mishnah\*,\* which reads “*osehu*\*.\*\*”\*\[^667] This version emphasizes the importance of one’s path being beautiful in the eyes of “the one who made us” — Hashem.

  It is understandable that we should use our Creator’s perspective to validate our life’s path. We ought to aim to achieve the destiny we were created to realize. There is no greater authority on this matter than Hashem — our Creator.

  Rebbe Yochanan also used our Creator as his yardstick in his famous words: “*Ashrei mi … she’amalo baTorah v’oseh nachas ruach l’yotzro.*\[^668]*”* Happy is the one involved in Torah and gives *nachas* to his Creator. If Hashem has *nachas* from us, we know we are living up to our “factory settings.”

  Hashem’s opinion about our lives is particularly relevant to determining the “*derech yesharah*” Rebbe asked about. As Hashem is the model for the very concept of *yashrus*, it makes sense that He would be the one to determine whether we are achieving it.

  **The Eyes of G-d and Man**  
According to this interpretation, Rebbe’s full statement emphasizes the importance of man’s path being beautiful in the eyes of both G-d and man.\[^669] We find this idea in many places throughout *Tanach\[^670]* and in the words of *Chazal.*\[^671] The GRA connects our mishnah to the famous *pasuk* (we quote at the end of our *bentching*), which encourages man to “find favor and appear wise in the eyes of G-d and man.”\[^672]

  **Beautiful All Around**  
If we combine the two versions of the *mishnah*, we derive the importance of three different perceptions: how G-d perceives us, how society perceives us, and how we perceive ourselves. The straight path is one viewed by all three as beautiful and harmonious.

  **How We Get There**

  אָמַר לָהֶם (רַבָּן יוֹחָנָן בֶּן זַכַּאי), צְאוּ וּרְאוּ *אֵיזוֹהִי דֶרֶךְ יְשָׁרָה שֶׁיִּדְבַּק בָּהּ הָאָדָם?*  
רַבִּי אֱלִיעֶזֶר אוֹמֵר, עַיִן *טוֹבָה*. רַבִּי יְהוֹשֻׁעַ אוֹמֵר, חָבֵר *טוֹב*. רַבִּי יוֹסֵי אוֹמֵר, שָׁכֵן *טוֹב*.  
רַבִּי שִׁמְעוֹן אוֹמֵר, הָרוֹאֶה אֶת הַנּוֹלָד. רַבִּי אֶלְעָזָר אוֹמֵר, לֵב *טוֹב*.  
אָמַר לָהֶם, רוֹאֶה אֲנִי אֶת דִּבְרֵי אֶלְעָזָר בֶּן עֲרָךְ מִדִּבְרֵיכֶם, שֶׁבִּכְלָל דְּבָרָיו דִּבְרֵיכֶם…(אבות ב:ט)

  **Learning From the Outside**  
Both Rebbe\[^673] and Rabban Yochanan ben Zakkai (Ribaz)\[^674] inquired about the “straight path” we should aim to live by. As opposed to Rebbe, who answered his own question, Ribaz asked his students to suggest their own answers.

  The way Ribaz instructed his *talmidim* to find the answer is significant. His first words — *“Tze’u u’reu* — Go out and see”\[^675] — imply that his students should look for the answers outside the Beis Midrash.\[^676] Chazal\[^677] use these same words (“*tze’u u’reu*”) to encourage learning lessons from Moshe Rabbeinu and Yehoshua, as well as from non-Jews, donkey drivers, and even snails. Though we learn many lessons from the Torah, others (especially those reflective of fundamental wisdom and *mentschlichkeit*) can and should be derived from standard norms and behavior.

  Ribaz’s *talmidim* heeded his directive, and each returned with a different answer.

  **Foresight**  
Rebbe Shimon emphasized the importance of being *“ro’eh es ha’nolad* — anticipating future implications.” We often act without considering how our actions will impact the future. Wise people find the straight path when they apply such foresight.\[^678] Reb Simcha Bunim Mi’Peshischa said it this way: “Our decisions in life must be as strategically calculated as a person playing chess. See to it that your moves will not be regretted later .”\[^679]

  \[clt]We often act without considering how our actions will impact the future. Wise people find the straight path when they apply such foresight.

  Rashi\[^680] gives an example of the lack of such anticipation. The eviction of Bar Kamtza from the party to which he was mistakenly invited launched a chain of events that led to the destruction of the Beis HaMikdash. Had the party host and his guests thought a few steps ahead and realized their insensitivity towards Bar Kamtza could have such tragic results, the *Churban* might have been avoided.

  We should learn from them to consider the long-term implications of our decisions and actions before implementing them.

  **Seeking The Good**  
Ribaz’s other *talmidim* all answered his question by recommending something they described as *“tov*.” They saw the *straight* path as the *good* path.

  Each *talmid* focused on a different aspect of *tov*.

  **A Good Friend**  
Rebbe Yehoshua recommended choosing a “*chaver tov* — a good friend.” We saw in an earlier chapter\[^681] that Yehoshua ben Perachya encouraged acquiring a friend but did not specify the type of friend we should seek. Rebbe Yehoshua identifies the type of friend as a “good” one.

  What does he mean by a “good” friend? He could refer to the basis of the friendship or its nature. Good friends share values, goals, and sensitivities, care enough to help each other, and can be trusted with personal information.\[^682]

  Rav Ovadiah Mi’Bartenura adds that a good friendship includes offering constructive criticism. Friends should be more than flattering fans; they should help us improve and grow. Chazal\[^683] identify Ohn ben Peles’s wife\[^684] as an example of such a “friend.” Though Ohn’s name is included in the original list of Korach’s rebels, it is absent from the continuation of the story because his wife convinced him to drop out of the rebellion. She was a true friend, one who was able to help her husband appreciate his mistake and change course.

  **A Good Neighbor**  
The next *talmid* of Rabban Yochanan ben Zakkai, Rebbe Yossi, emphasized the importance of good neighbors. Good neighbors can be even more important than good friends because we interact with our neighbors constantly.\[^685]

  The *medrash* calls this phenomenon “*Tov l’tzaddik, tov l’shecheino*” and illustrates it by contrasting the fate of the *Shevatim* who camped next to Moshe and Aharon in the desert with those who lived next to Korach.\[^686] Yehudah, Zevulun, and Yissacher pitched their tents next to Moshe and Aharon’s and became Torah scholars like them. Reuven, Ga,d and Menashe lived beside Korach and became rabble-rousers like him. Though the Jewish People camped together, each *Shevet* was influenced differently by their respective neighbor.

  This idea guided Rebbe Yossi ben Kisma.\[^687] He rejected a large sum of money offered in exchange for moving to a town that lacked a strong Torah presence because he feared being impacted by neighbors who lacked a Torah orientation.\[^688]

  We are greatly influenced by our neighbors and need to choose them carefully.

  **The Tov Within**  
As opposed to the two afore-mentioned Tannaim, who apply the “*tov*” to those with whom we interact, the other two Tannaim, Rebbe Eliezer and Rebbe Elazar, focus on developing the “*tov*” *within ourselves*. Though surrounding ourselves with good influences is valuable, our ultimate goal should be improving our own selves.

  **The Good Eye**  
Rebbe Eliezer recommends developing a good eye — the ability to see and celebrate the good that we and others have.\[^689] This crucial characteristic is listed as the first of three factors that distinguish between the *talmidim* of Avraham Avinu and those of Bil’am HaRasha.*\[^690]*

  A good eye is critical because it determines our perception of reality.\[^691] It helps us focus on the good in other people and the world and facilitates our appreciation of the good done on our behalf.\[^692]

  Positive perception is also a condition for personal happiness. Sefer Tehillim\[^693] presents “seeing the good” as the way to love each day of our lives: “*Mi ha’ish he’chafetz chaim? Ohev yamim — lir’os tov.*”\[^694] One focused upon the negative will dislike the world and their life within it. Focusing on the good gives us reason to celebrate our existence within a world we see as wonderful. Though the perception of those focused upon the negative may be somewhat accurate, their perspective depresses them. People who focus on the positive in their family, friends, community, and life live happily throughout each day.

  Rav Kook\[^695] saw the good eye as even more significant. He asserted that it not only sets our perception but also *changes the reality*. Looking for the good in whatever we experience steers those events positively.\[^696]

  \[clt]Looking for the good in whatever we experience steers those events positively.

  **The Heart Of The Matter**  
The last of the *talmidim* — Rebbe Elazer ben Arach — focuses on something more internal than our eyes. He emphasizes developing a “*lev tov* —  good heart.” How we see reality hinges on our feelings and motivation — our “heart.” Because good-hearted people see the good, to see the good, we need to improve our hearts.

  After hearing all of the answers,  Rabban Yochanan ben Zakkai responded that he preferred this last answer as it includes all the others. Oour hearts determine our perspective on life, as well as who we seek as friends and neighbors. Our eyes see, but our heart decides what to look for and how to interpret it.

  Rabban Yochanan ben Zakkai’s *talmidim* provide a roadmap for how to find and sustain life’s straight path. The map includes who we associate with, but also, most importantly, what we choose to focus on and how we think and feel.

  May we use their advice to live our lives in the straight way Hashem created us!

  **Mitzvah Observance**

  The Cost and the Benefit

  What We Run To

  Protecting Ourselves

  The Pain, The Effort and The Process

  What We Gain From Pain

  For Poorer For Richer

  If Not Now  
**The Cost and the Benefit**

  רַבִּי אוֹמֵר... וֶהֱוֵי זָהִיר בְּמִצְוָה קַלָּה כְּבַחֲמוּרָה, שֶׁאֵין אַתָּה יוֹדֵעַ מַתַּן שְׂכָרָן שֶׁל מִצְוֹת.  
וֶהֱוֵי מְחַשֵּׁב הֶפְסֵד מִצְוָה כְּנֶגֶד שְׂכָרָהּ, ושְׂכַר עֲבֵרָה כְּנֶגֶד הֶפְסֵדָהּ (אבות ב:א):

  בֶּן עַזַּאי אוֹמֵר, הֱוֵי רָץ לְמִצְוָה קַלָּה כְּבַחֲמוּרָה, וּבוֹרֵחַ מִן הָעֲבֵרָה. שֶׁמִּצְוָה גּוֹרֶרֶת מִצְוָה, וַעֲבֵרָה גוֹרֶרֶת עֲבֵרָה. שֶׁשְּׂכַר מִצְוָה, מִצְוָה. וּשְׂכַר עֲבֵרָה, עֲבֵרָה (אבות ד:ב):
