  Two *mishnayos,* one authored by Rebbe Yehudah HaNasi and the other by Ben Azay\*,\* encourage us to value *mitzvah* fulfillment and avoid *aveirah* transgression\*.\* Both begin with the word *“havei,”* and both encourage\[^697] us to properly appreciate *mitzvah* observance.

  **Cost Versus Benefit**  
The second part of Rebbe’s teaching urges us to conduct a cost/benefit analysis of the performance of both *mitzvos* and *aveiros*.\[^698] Though *mitzvah* observance can sometimes be cumbersome, we should compare the great reward we receive to the (relatively) small sacrifice we make by observing them.\[^699] Conversely, when tempted to sin, we should remind ourselves that the long-term loss outweighs the short-term pleasure or gain.

  The *gemara*\[^700] elaborates on our *mishnah* and explains that the *yetzer hara* often lures us to sin by highlighting the pleasure we will receive from it. We need to “regain control” of ourselves by reflecting upon the long-term loss sin causes. People only sin if they are infiltrated by a *ruach shetus* (foolish spirit).\[^701] One who carefully considers the *true* consequences of their actions\[^702] will choose to fulfill *mitzvos* and avoid *aveiros.\[^703]*

  \[clt]One who carefully considers the *true* consequences of their actions will choose to fulfill *mitzvos* and avoid *aveiros*.

  In addition to helping us prevent future sins, our accurate appraisal of sin is also critical to our *teshuvah* for sins committed in the past. We express this idea in the words we add to our *viduy* (confession of sin): “*Sarnu mi’mitzvotecha… v’lo shavah lanu —* We have veered from the path of your *mitzvos,* and it was not worth it.”\[^704] This admission and honest evaluation of our past sins distances us from them and helps us avoid future ones.

  **The Long-Term**  
We have seen that Rebbe encourages us to consider the reward for *mitzvah* observance and the loss caused by sin. What kind of reward and loss is Rebbe referring to? Many *meforshim*\[^705] understand Rebbe as focusing upon the *sechar* (reward) and *onesh* (punishment) we receive in the *next* world. The loss involved in performing a *mitzvah* and the pleasure sin offers pale compared to the next world’s eternal and more meaningful reward and punishment.

  We can add that observing *mitzvos* and avoiding *aveiros* benefit us in *this* world as well. Sin often offers instant gratification but impacts us negatively later in life. This is why the Torah often uses the word *“yom”* to describe someone who chooses sin. For example, Yaakov asked Eisav to sell him the *bechor* (firstborn) rights “*ka’yom*.”\[^706] The Seforno\[^707] explains that Yaakov used this word to highlight why Eisav was unworthy of the *bechor* rights. One who prefers the short-term pleasures of today over meaningful long-term goals cannot be a link in the chain of Jewish generations rooted in eternal values.\[^708]

  **What We Don’t Know**  
Though the cost/benefit analysis is helpful, it can also harm the nature of our fulfillment. The focus on reward can cause people to pursue only the *mitzvos* they think offer the most compensation. Rebbe preempts this at the start of the *mishnah* by noting that we do not know the exact reward associated with each *mitzvah*. We should, therefore, value all *mitzvos* equally — those we assume to be less significant as much as those we presume to be more.\[^709]

  For this reason,\[^710] *halachah* mandates principles like *“Ha’osek b’mitzvah patur min ha’mitzvah”\[^711]* and *“Ein ma’avirin al ha’mitzvos,” \[^712]* which reject the prioritization of *mitzvos* presumed to be of greater significance. We seek to fulfill all *mitzvos* with equal passion and commitment, irrespective of the reward we associate with each.

  **Why We Don’t Know**  
Chazal\[^713] take this idea a step further by explaining that Hashem intentionally concealed the amount of *sechar* awarded for each *mitzvah* in order to preclude selective fulfillment. As we do not know the reward associated with each *mitzvah*, we have no choice but to commit ourselves to all of them equally. The *medrash*\[^714] conveys this idea by comparing Hashem to a king who asks his subjects to plant an orchard for him. He avoids revealing his favorite type of tree to ensure people plant all the necessary species.

  One question remains: why does Hashem want us to fulfill *all* the *mitzvos* instead of focusing on the most important ones? Many *meforshim*\[^715] compare *mitzvos* to medicines or vitamins. Even if certain ones are more important for our health, we must include them all in our diet. Others\[^716] link different *mitzvos* to various body parts. Our fingers may be less important than our heart, but they are still important; so are the *mitzvos* that are meant to purify and sustain our body parts.

  The Chasid Yavetz makes an additional point. Though we benefit from observing *mitzvos*, our primary goal should be fulfilling Hashem’s will. Antignus Ish Socho\[^717] made this point when he warned us not to serve Hashem for the reward. In this sense, all *mitzvos* are equal, as they all reflect Hashem’s Will.

  Similarly, the Ramban\[^718] quotes our *mishnah* as the basis of his assertion that all *mitzvos* are precious because they are all our way of thanking Hashem for having created us. As each *mitzvah* is an equal expression of Hashem’s Will, they are each an equally precious way of thanking Him.

  Let’s make sure to heed Rebbe’s advice and constantly remind ourselves of how much we gain from *mitzvos* and the price we pay for sin. May this inspire us to make wise decisions!  
**What We Run To**

  רַבִּי אוֹמֵר...   *וֶהֱוֵי* זָהִיר בְּ*מִצְוָה קַלָּה כְּבַחֲמוּרָה*, שֶׁאֵין אַתָּה יוֹדֵעַ מַתַּן שְׂכָרָן שֶׁל *מִצְוֹת*.  
*וֶהֱוֵי* מְחַשֵּׁב הֶפְסֵד *מִצְוָה* כְּנֶגֶד שְׂכָרָהּ, ושְׂכַר עֲבֵרָה כְּנֶגֶד הֶפְסֵדָהּ (אבות ב:א):

  בֶּן עַזַּאי אוֹמֵר, *הֱוֵי* רָץ לְ*מִצְוָה קַלָּה כְּבַחֲמוּרָה*, וּבוֹרֵחַ מִן הָעֲבֵרָה.  
*שֶׁמִּצְוָה* גּוֹרֶרֶת *מִצְוָה*, וַעֲבֵרָה גוֹרֶרֶת עֲבֵרָה. שֶׁשְּׂכַר *מִצְוָה*, *מִצְוָה*. וּשְׂכַר עֲבֵרָה, עֲבֵרָה (אבות ד:ב):

  In the second *perek*, Rebbe (Rav Yehudah HaNasi) emphasizes the importance of being cautious about the performance of *all mitzvos* — even those we assume to be less significant.\[^719] In the fourth *perek*, Ben Azay\[^720] continues this theme by encouraging us to not only be cautious but also to *run* to fulfill *mitzvos.*\[^721]

  **Passion**  
We learn the importance of running to fulfill *mitzvos* from Avraham Avinu. In telling the story of Avraham’s *hachnasas orchim* (hospitality), the Torah mentions (four times) that he *ran* and *rushed*.\[^722] First he *ran* to invite the guests into his home, then he *rushed* to ask Sarah to bake bread and *ran* to find meat, and, finally, he *rushed* to bring it to his guests.

  The Ramban\[^723] explains that the Torah emphasizes this point because Avraham’s haste reflects his passion for *chesed*. He saw *chesed* and *mitzvos* as more than mere obligations; they were his *passion*. We use the word *ratz*, which has a similar root to the word *ratzon* (will),\[^724] to describe running because we run towards things we are passionate about. The Torah’s description of Avraham’s actions teaches us to be passionate about *mitzvos* and to express this passion by running to fulfill them.\[^725]

  A powerful story about the Chofetz Chaim demonstrates his passion for fulfilling *mitzvos*. Late one night, a boy returning from the Radin Yeshiva amid a snowstorm encountered the Chofetz Chaim. The latter admonished him for being out late in the snow and told him to go home immediately. The following morning, the boy asked his host, the Chofetz Chaim’s sister, why the Chofetz Chaim himself was out that night. She explained that since the moon had not been visible for *kiddush levanah* the past *Motzei Shabbos*, the Chofetz Chaim had been walking around each night (even in the snow) hoping that he could find the moon and fulfill the mitzvah of *kiddush levanah*.

  **Eagerness**  
Another way to express such passion is *zerizus* — fulfilling *mitzvos* at the earliest possible moment.\[^726] Moshe Rabbeinu exemplified this trait. His eagerness to fulfill the *mitzvah* of establishing *Arei Miklat* (cities of refuge) inspired him to designate these cities as soon as they captured the western side of the Jordan River, even though the cities would not be operational until the Jews entered Eretz Yisrael.\[^727] Moshe valued *mitzvah* observance so highly that he seized the opportunity to fulfill *mitzvos* before they were mandated or necessary.

  **The Will Behind It All**  
Like Rebbe, Ben Azay stresses the importance of *all mitzvos* by encouraging us to run towards even *mitzvos* we assume to be less significant. The logic behind Ben Azay’s words is similar to that of Ben Teima, who recommends “running like a deer to fulfill the Will of your Father in heaven.”\[^728] We fulfill *mitzvos* because they are the quintessential expressions of Hashem’s Will; since they all equally express His Will, we should run towards all of them equally.

  The Pirkei Moshe\[^729] takes this idea further and explains that running to fulfill *mitzvos* whose fulfillment we assume to be less important is even *more* significant than running towards those we see as more important. Rushing to perform an “important” *mitzvah* reflects our appreciation of the *mitzvah*’s importance. Rushing to fulfill “less important” *mitzvos* expresses our eagerness to follow *Hashem’s Will*.



  **One Thing Leads to Another…**  
Ben Azay gives two additional reasons for why people should run to fulfill all *mitzvos*.

  The first reason is that *“mitzvah goreres mitzvah* (each *mitzvah* generates another one)*.”* Rabbeinu Yonah\[^730] explains that fulfilling any *mitzvah* accustoms us to such behavior and inevitably fosters our fulfillment of additional ones. Therefore, the specific *mitzvah* one starts with is less important than the fact that one performs a *mitzvah* because fulfilling any and all *mitzvos* inspires future observance.\[^731]

  Likewise, this same conditioning occurs with sin. We should run from any and all sin just as we run towards all *mitzvos* because violating any sin weakens our aversion to future ones. Based upon the first *pasuk* in Sefer Tehillim, the *gemara*\[^732] explains that even one who intends to just “pass by” sinners will inevitably stopto observe their activities and, eventually, “sit” and join them.

  Rav Huna presents this idea sharply by explaining that after committing a (specific) sin twice, we view it as permissible.\[^733] Though we initially see sin as taboo, transgression of even a minor *aveirah* erodes this aversion to the point where we see sin, including even very severe ones, as acceptable.\[^734] Like Rebbe Shimon, who emphasized the importance of considering the future implications of our actions,\[^735] Ben Azay urges us to run from even minor sins because of the additional ones our initial transgression will inevitably generate.

  The Chasid Yavetz adds an important point to our appreciation of Ben Azay’s teaching. He explains that *mitzvah* observance generates additional fulfillment only when we are *passionate* about the initial observance. Only *running* to fulfill, as opposed to the mere performance of, *mitzvos* expresses and reinforces our appreciation of *mitzvos*, which, in turn, inspires additional fulfillment.

  **Hashem’s Helping Hand**  
Ben Azay’s second reason is: *“Sechar mitzvah, mitzvah U’Sechar aveirah, aveirah.”* The *meforshim* address the relationship between this reason and the first one (*mitzvah goreres mitzvah*).\[^736]

  Rabbeinu Yonah explains\[^737] that, in addition to our own conditioning (*goreres*), when we perform a *mitzvah* Hashem rewards us (gives us *sechar*) by strengthening our efforts. This explanation recalls the teaching of Reish Lakish, who explains that Hashem “opens the door” for those who aim to sin and aids those who seek to purify themselves.\[^738] The first step is ours alone to take; once we make that choice, Hashem assists our efforts.

  \[clt]The first step is ours alone to take; once we make that choice, Hashem assists our efforts.

  Rav Chaim Volozhyn takes Rabbeinu Yonah’s idea further. Rav Chaim explains that Hashem assists us *even before* we begin the actual performance of the first *mitzvah*. He helps us as soon as we express interest in the *mitzvah*, as soon as we run toward it.\[^739] Ben Azay encourages us to run towards *mitzvos* because, when we do, Hashem helps us to fulfill them successfully. According to Rav Chaim Volozhyn, the initial “*mitzvah”* to which Ben Azay refers is the act of running to perform a *mitzvah*, while the “*sechar mitzvah*” reward refers to Hashem’s help in actually fulfilling the *mitzvah* we pursue.\[^740]

  **Inspiration and Assistance**  
Ben Azay encourages us to run towards *mitzvos* because expressing our passion for fulfilling them reinforces our appreciation. This appreciation earns Hashem’s assistance and inspires us to continue fulfilling in the future.

  May Ben Azay’s words help us appreciate the opportunity to observe *mitzvos* as well as the significance of running towards them, and may that, in turn, inspire us to continue fulfilling *mitzvos* in the future!  
**Protecting Ourselves**

  משֶׁה קִבֵּל תּוֹרָה מִסִּינַי, וּמְסָרָהּ לִיהוֹשֻׁעַ, וִיהוֹשֻׁעַ לִזְקֵנִים, וּזְקֵנִים לִנְבִיאִים,  
וּנְבִיאִים מְסָרוּהָ לְאַנְשֵׁי כְנֶסֶת הַגְּדוֹלָה.  
הֵם אָמְרוּ שְׁלשָׁה דְבָרִים, הֱווּ מְתוּנִים בַּדִּין, וְהַעֲמִידוּ תַלְמִידִים הַרְבֵּה, *וַעֲשׂוּ סְיָג לַתּוֹרָה*: (אבות א:א)

  Pirkei Avos’s first mishnah quotes three statements of the *Anshei* *Knesses HaGedolah*. The last of the three is: “*Asu siyag l’Torah —* Make a protective fence around the Torah.”\[^741] The Rambam and other *meforshim* explain that this refers to the *gezeiros* and *takanos* enacted throughout the generations to protect against transgressing *aveiros*.\[^742]

  **The Source**  
Avos D'Rebbe Nosson\[^743] points to two *mitzvos* that teach us this concept. The first is the laws of *nezirus.* Though the ultimate goal is avoiding wine, the Torah prohibits the consumption of *any grape product* to distance the *nazir* from wine.\[^744]

  The second model is the laws that govern relationships between men and women.\[^745] The Torah formulates the prohibition as “*lo sikrevu l’galos ervah* (do not *draw close* to prohibited relationships)” which prohibits not just the actual relations but *all* forms of intimacy.\[^746] Hugging, kissing, touching, and even *yichud* (seclusion together) are all forbidden in order to distance us from actual relations.\[^747]

  We find *siyagim* in general society as well. Governments protect the public by limiting access to dangerous items (such as poison, cliffs, and electrical generators) and by setting limits for medicines and weight and height standards (at a point lower than the actual danger level). Similarly, halachic *siyagim* help us avoid *aveiros*.\[^748]



  **The Inevitable Danger**  
*Siyagim* are critical to avoiding sin. Without them, transgression is inevitable. Rav Shimon Shkop, one of pre-war Europe’s pre-eminent Talmudic scholars, was once asked why so many Jews in Western countries violated the Torah prohibition against shaving with a razor. He responded that since cutting one’s beard was uncommon in ancient times, Chazal did not institute *siyagim* to protect against the prohibition of shaving with a razor. When people in Western countries began cutting their beards, they were left unprotected due to the lack of *siyagim* that distance us from the actual Torah prohibition.\[^749] Without the protection of *siyagim*, violation of Torah principles is inevitable.\[^750]

  **What We Care About**  
Rabbeinu Yonah\[^751] adds that *siyagim* are essential not just because they protect us from sin but also because their enactment expresses our concern about and commitment to avoiding sin. They show that we value our spiritual health as much as our physical health. Just as we avoid food and situations that threaten us physically, so we distance ourselves from things that threaten us spiritually.\[^752]

  \[clt]*Siyagim* are essential not just because they protect us from sin but also because their enactment expresses our concern about and commitment to avoiding sin. They show that we value our spiritual health as much as our physical health.

  We saw (in the last chater) that Ben Azay encourages running to fulfill *mitzvos* because it shows our eagerness to fulfill them.\[^753] *Siyagim* are to sin what running is for *mitzvos*. Just as running shows passion to fulfill, *siyagim* express concern about transgression.

  Chazal were very critical of those who do not distance themselves from sin. The *gemara*\[^754] brands one who chooses to pass by a place that might tempt him to sin a *rasha*. A God-fearing person does his best to avoid sin. One cavalier about putting himself in spiritual harm’s way is a *rasha*.

  **Personal Protection**  
The *Tiferes Yisrael*[*^755*](Ibid.) adds that the responsibility to enact *siyagim* lies on both communal leaders and individuals. Like our *mishnah’s* first two statements (about being cautious in judgment and training many students), the mandate to institute *siyagim* is one of the \*Beis Din’\*s responsibilities. *Batei Din* see the big picture of communal challenges and have the responsibility to address them.

  These broad, communal edicts are important but insufficient. In addition to the common challenges we all face, each individual has different desires and faces unique challenges and circumstances. To complement the generic *siyagim* Batei Din and communal leaders institute to address collective issues, we each need to add personal *siyagim* to protect ourselves from our personal challenges.

  **Guard Your Eyes**  
The Ra’avad\[^756] writes that our eyes need the most protection. The Torah warns us not to be “led astray by our hearts and eyes.”\[^757] Rashi explains that sin begins with what our eyes see: “Our eyes see, our hearts desire, and our bodies sin.”\[^758]

  To direct our hearts properly, the Torah commands us to look at our *tzitzis* and be reminded of Hashem and our commitment to Him.\[^759] The Ra’avad explains that, in addition to *tzizis*, we need *siyagim* to protect us from sights that draw us to sin. Sefer Mishlei\[^760] defines a tzaddik as someone who closes his eyes to avoid seeing “the bad.” The *gemara* applies this *pasuk* to one who avoids gazing at women (not fully dressed while) doing laundry in the river. The *gemara* explains that even if a person has no choice but to pass by the river, he should close his eyes because he should care about “protecting his soul.”

  This protection is even more necessary in today’s world. The internet offers unprecedented, easy access to sin and actively lures people to it. We need to enact *siyagim* (such as filters) to protect ourselves from being led astray.

  The Yerushalmi\[^761] teaches that Hashem knows that a person who “gives his eyes to Him,” one who is careful about what he sees, is entirely connected to Him. Looking at things that draw us towards sin pulls us away from Hashem. Focusing on Hashem and his *mitzvos* connects us to Him.

  May we value our spiritual health enough to heed Chazal’s *siyagim* and add whatever isnecessary to help us face our personal challenges.  
**The Pain, The Effort, and The Process**

  בֶּן הֵא הֵא אוֹמֵר, לְפוּם צַעֲרָא אגרא: (אבות ה:כג)

  **The Conclusion**  
Because Maseches Avos initially consisted of five *perakim*, the fifth *perek*’s last words are also the maseches’s(original) last words and are, therefore, especially important. Avos’s last words are a three-word statement from Ben Hei Hei: *“L’fum tza’ara, agra* — Reward is proportional to the pain (experienced in the process).”\[^762]

  This important point lies at the heart of *Avodas Hashem*. At the Pesach *seder*, the *rasha*\[^763] asks his father, *“Mah ha’avodah ha’zos lachem* — What is this *work* to you?” The Yerushalmi\[^764] has a slightly different version of the question, in which the *rasha* expresses his frustration with the *difficulty* of *Avodas Hashem*: *“Mah ha’torach ha’zeh* — Why is Judaism such a *tircha*, a burden?” Ben Hei Hei answers the *rasha*’s question by explaining that the gain is proportional to the pain. Hashem makes serving Him challenging so that the reward will be commensurate.

  **Fighting the Good Fight**  
When Ben Hei Hei taught that the reward for our actions is proportional to the *tza’ar*, what type of action was he referring to?

  The Yalkut Mei’am Loez saw the topic as the fulfillment of *mitzvos*\[^765] and ethical principles. He explains that Ben Hei Hei concluded the *maseches* by addressing those frustrated with their inability to properly fulfill *mitzvos* and Maseches Avos’s many dictates. Ben Hei Hei raises our spirits by reminding us that our reward hinges on the effort we exert, not on the degree of our success.

  \[clt]Our reward hinges on the amount of effort we exert, not on the degree of our success.

  For this reason, the Rambam\[^766] saw one who needs to overcome his *yetzer hara* in order to fulfill *mitzvos* (and avoid *aveiros*) as greater than the *chasid* who no longer desires sin. As opposed to the “philosophers” who prefer the *chasid*, the Rambam champions the one who needs to be *koveish* (conquer) his *yetzer*. His effort and, thus, his reward are greater.\[^767]

  This is how the Arizal explained the significance of the *Avodas Hashem* of later generations of Jews. With us so far removed from the Sinaitic revelation and thus on a much lower level than previous generations, Rav Chaim Vital (the Arizal’s main *talmid*) wondered what significance our lowly actions could possibly have. The Arizal answered that being on a lower level means that our mitzvah fulfillment is a greater challenge\[^768] and, therefore, much more significant.

  People are often disillusioned by their failures and the strength of their *yetzer hara*. Instead, we should appreciate how overcoming challenges adds significance to our successes.\[^769] The Ba’al HaTanya adds that we should realize that the struggles with challenges may be why we were created — not for our successes, but for the sake of our struggles.\[^770]

  **The Process of Torah Learning**  
Many *meforshim* apply Ben Hei Hei’s words to Torah learning.\[^771] Rabbeinu Yonah\[^772] connects our *mishnah* to Ben Bag Bag’s directive (in the previous *mishnah*): “*hafoch bah va’foch bah* (continue turning over the Torah).”\[^773] Even if one has learned all (they think) there is to learn from a particular Torah passage, it pays to continue studying it because we are rewarded for our effort, not just for our knowledge.

  We emphasize this point when making a *siyum*: *“Anu ameilim v’heim ameilim. Anu ameilim u’mekablim sachar; heim ameilim v’einam mekablim sachar.*”\[^774] We contrast the efforts we invest in Torah learning with those invested by others in alternative endeavors. We are rewarded; they are not.

  The Chofetz Chaim asked the obvious question. Are others not rewarded for their efforts? Aren’t all people paid for their work? The Chofetz Chaim explained that people are generally paid for the *results* of their efforts. If their efforts are fruitful, they get paid; if not, they are left empty-handed. The mitzvah of *talmud Torah* is unique in that Hashem rewards for the effort itself. We receive *sechar* for the time and energy we devote to Torah learning, even if we do not successfully understand or retain the knowledge.\[^775]

  These two understandings of Ben Hei Hei’s statement are, of course, not mutually exclusive. His words apply equally to rewards received for both Torah learning and *mitzvah* fulfillment. The reward for both is proportional to the *tza’ar*.

  **No Pain, No Gain?**  
What does Ben Hei Hei mean by the word *tza’ar*? What type of *tza’ar* is the reward proportional to?

  Many *meforshim*\[^776] interpret the word *tza’ar* in the literal sense — pain or suffering. Our reward is proportional to the pain we endure.

  According to this explanation, Ben Hei Hei’s message connects to the broader theme of the willingness to endure pain in order to accomplish our goals. This point is made by a *mishnah* in Avos’s final *perek*\[^777] which emphasizes the importance of the willingness to suffer in order to learn Torah: “*Kach hi darkah shel Torah: pas b’melach tochal, u’mayim b’mesurah sishteh, v’chayei **tza’ar** tichyeh* (The way of Torah is (even) eating bread with salt, rationing water, and living with pain).” A person should be willing to live a life of poverty and even endure a life of pain (a life lacking physical pleasures) if this is what his Torah learning requires.\[^778]

  The *medrash*\[^779] attributes this idea to Moshe Rabbeinu. Moshe described (in detail) the pain he had to endure in order to bring the Torah down from heaven for the Jewish People because they, too, needed to (be ready to) pay a similar price for Torah learning.

  Though he did not necessarily agree with the saying “No pain, no gain,” Moshe taught that gain hinges on the *willingness* to endure pain. Ben Hei Hei teaches that reward is proportional to the *actual* pain we experience.\[^780]

  **The Eved’s Effort**  
Many other Rishonim understand the word *tza’ar* as referring to the effort involved. Fulfilling *mitzvos* that require significant effort shows a higher level of care and commitment and thus deserves more reward.\[^781]

  The Ra’avyah\[^782] takes this approach and connects our mishnah to another teaching involving Ben Hei Hei. The *gemara*\[^783] relates that Ben Hei Hei asked Hillel to define the uniqueness of an *Eved Elokim.* Ben Hei Hei wondered why Malachi HaNavi used both that term and the term *tzaddik* in the same *pasuk*.\[^784] What is the difference between the two terms? What does the term *Eved Elokim* add to *tzaddik*?

  Hillel answered that a *tzaddik* reviews his Torah learning a hundred times. The *Eved Elokim* goes the “extra mile” and studies it a hundred *and one* times. The *tzaddik* ensures that he understands and remembers his Torah perfectly — a level of one hundred percent. The *eved Elokim*, *servant* of G-d, sees the goal of learning as more than just knowledge. Effort is also important to him, so he continues studying even after he has mastered the material.\[^785]

  After understanding what Ben Hei Hei means by *tza’ar*, we now need to appreciate how the *sechar* is proportional to it. In the next chapter, we will see how and why our reward hinges upon the pain we endure and the effort we invest.  
**What We Gain From Pain**

  בֶּן הֵא הֵא אוֹמֵר, לְפוּם צַעֲרָא אגרא: (אבות ה:כג)

  Previously, we saw how Ben Hei Hei links the *agra* (reward) received for Torah learning and *mitzvah* fulfillment to the *tza’ar,* the pain and effort\*,\* involved.\[^786] The obvious question is *why* this linkage exists. Why is the reward proportional to the *tza’ar*?

  The answer to this question hinges on how we understand the *agra* referred to by the mishnah. What type of reward does it refer to?

  **Reward**  
The standard explanation\[^787] is that the *mishnah* refers to the *sechar* given to us by Hashem. Hashem rewards us most for *mitzvah* fulfillment we find challenging and painful. The Sefer Chassidim\[^788] explains that Hashem appreciates our efforts, not just ouraccomplishments.

  **Benefit**  
Avos D'Rebbe Nosson\[^789] seems to have a different understanding of *agra,* because it links the *agra* to the fact that the *tza’ar* is inherently *“good for man.”* The *agra* is the *intrinsic* benefit we receive *naturally* from the *tza’ar*, not some external heavenly reward.

  What natural benefit do we derive from effort and pain?

  **Comprehension — The “Butter” of Torah**  
The Meiri\[^790] connects Ben Hei Hei’s statement to Torah learning and defines the reward as our *comprehension* of Torah. We grasp ideas best when we work hard to understand them.\[^791] The more effort we invest in learning, the better we understand the material. Thomas Edison famously said it this way: “Genius is one percent inspiration and ninety-nine percent perspiration.”

  The Meiri’s explanation echoes the *gemara*’s\[^792] assertion that *“yagati u’matzasi, ta’amin —* (only) one who claims that ‘I worked and found’ should be believed.*”* It is hard work that facilitates ultimate comprehension.

  For this reason, Rav Kook\[^793] rejected “pedagogical” attempts to make Torah learning more user-friendly. He argued that hard work is critical to achieving a high level of Torah study. This is how he explained the Gemara’s assertion that “the butter of Torah” can only be achieved by those who “spit out their mothers’ milk.”\[^794] Those who settle for “mothers milk,” learning that is spoon-fed to them, will never invest the effort needed to create “butter” *—* the deeper, more advanced form of Torah. Though we should help people succeed in their learning, we should avoid spoon-feeding because the extra effort yields a higher level of comprehension.\[^795]

  Calvin Coolidge remarked: “Nothing in this world can take the place of persistence. Talent will not; nothing is more common than unsuccessful men with talent. Genius will not; unrewarded genius is almost a proverb. Education will not; the world is full of educated derelicts. Persistence and determination alone are omnipotent.”

  **Retention — The Torah of “*Af*”**  
The Rambam\[^796] links Ben Hei Hei’s statement to our retention of Torah learning. We remember ideas we work hard to understand.\[^797] Reish Lakish\[^798] strongly made this point by asserting that Torah learning only “remains” with those who “kill themselves” to attain it.

  Easy come, easy go. Only the Torah we make sacrifices to learn remains with us years later. Shlomo HaMelech called this the Torah we study “*b’af*  — under duress.”\[^799] Even Shlomo HaMelech, the wisest of men, recognized that the wisdom he retained best was what he struggled to attain.\[^800]

  **Appreciation**  
We retain what we work hard to attain because we appreciate it more. We care about what we struggle and sacrifice for. This is why Hashem arranged for His three greatest gifts to us — Torah, Eretz Yisrael, and Olam Haba — to all be *nikneis b’yisurin*, acquired through suffering.\[^801] We appreciate the difficult missions; the *tza’ar* we experience helps us appreciate the goals we accomplish.

  The Beis Yosef and the Ramchal (based on the Zohar) saw this appreciation as why Hashem created our world. This world’s reality of Hashem’s hidden presence affords us the opportunity to choose to commit ourselves to Him. This makes our reward in the World to Come  — a close relationship with Hashem — more meaningful. We appreciate relationships we work hard to develop more than one easily achieved.\[^802]

  Rabbi Jonathan Sacks\[^803] used this idea to explain why the degree of observance of the Yamim Tovim is proportional to how difficult each one is to observe; the ones *hardest* to observe are also the ones *most* observed.\[^804] Pesach requires more advanced preparatory work than any other Yom Tov. Yom Kippur is the day hardest to complete. Surprisingly, the percentage of Jews who observe these two holidays is significantly higher than that of those easier to observe. We appreciate and are, therefore, most committed to what we find most difficult.

  \[clt]We appreciate and are, therefore, most committed to what we find most difficult.

  **Growth — Making Ourselves Stronger**  
The struggle and pain we endure also strengthen our character and bring us closer to Hashem. People turn to Hashem and feel His presence more when they face challenges. Many people who suffer through difficult times speak about how they felt closer to Hashem during that period than at any point before or after. Though no ones yearns to suffer, they miss the closeness they felt with Hashem when they did.

  Challenging circumstances also force us to develop additional skills and aspects of our personality. Rav Meilech Biderman\[^805] describes a street in Lakewood which was hit by Hurricane Sandy. The trees on one side of the street were all uprooted while the parallel ones on the other side remained standing. The only difference between the rows of trees was that the trees on the first side of the street benefited from a custom-made watering system, while the trees on the second side did not. The conveniently available water allowed the first set of trees to survive without needing to forge deep roots. They were thus easily uprooted. In contrast, the trees without easy access to water were forced to strike deep roots. These roots helped them survive the hurricane. Difficult circumstances force us to develop stronger survival skills.

  \[clt]Difficult circumstances force us to develop stronger survival skills.

  Rabbi Lamm\[^806] applied this idea to *chinuch ha’banim* (child rearing) and cautioned against over-protecting:  
Without labor and struggle, without exertion of the intellect and long hours of patient plodding, one can neither master the intricacies of any profession, nor can one achieve great and satisfying success in any business.  
It is true about children — if we over-protect them, if we train them to accept easy triumphs, the shortcuts to success, then they will grow up without backbones, nurtured on the infantile conception that a wishbone is enough.  
Such people can never fly, they can only flutter. Their vision never soars, their dreams remain myopic, their conceptions petty.



  **No Pain…**  
Our *mishnah* reminds us of the famous saying: “No pain, no gain.” Our “gain,” or, as the *mishnah* describes it, our *agra*, is proportional not only to our accomplishments, but also to the effort we invest and the pain we endure.

  This *agra* consists of both heavenly blessings as well as our own internal personal growth. The challenges we face help us reach and retain higher levels of learning and spirituality and also inspire us to develop ourselves as stronger people.

  May realizing this help us successfully face and even celebrate the opportunity to confront life’s challenges!  
**For Poorer, For Richer**

  רַבִּי יוֹנָתָן אוֹמֵר, כָּל הַמְקַיֵּם  אֶת הַתּוֹרָה מֵעֹנִי,   סוֹפוֹ לְקַיְּמָהּ מֵעשֶׁר.  
וְכָל הַמְבַטֵּל אֶת הַתּוֹרָה מֵעשֶׁר, סוֹפוֹ לְבַטְּלָהּ מֵעֹנִי: (אבות ד:ט)

  Though we aim to observe *mitzvos* properly and maximize our Torah learning, the realities of life often make this challenging. Rebbe Yonasan relates to one of these realities — poverty  —  and asserts that anyone who fulfills the Torah\[^807] in a state of poverty will eventually fulfill it in a state of wealth.\[^808]

  **Mei’oni: Poverty’s Potential**  
The *meforshim* debate how poverty (potentially) impacts Torah fulfillment. Most *meforshim\[^809]* understand that poverty makes observance *more difficult*. It generates discomfort, insecurity, and instability, which impair one’s ability to focus on Torah learning and spiritual growth. Rebbe Yonasan encourages the poor to overcome these challenges by promising them wealth as a future reward.\[^810]

  The problem with this interpretation is that Rebbe Yonasan’s promise is not always evident: many of the poor who live Torah lives remain impoverished. How can Rebbe Yonatan claim that “*all* those who fulfill in poverty” are rewarded with wealth if so many do not seem to be?\[^811]

  For this reason, other *meforshim* explain the *mishnah* differently. Their explanation begins with a careful reading of Rebbe Yonasan’s words. Rebbe Yonasan does not refer to those who fulfill “*b’oni* — *in* a state of poverty,” but, rather, *“mei’oni* — *through* poverty.*”* Poverty is not just the state within which one fulfills. It should also *facilitate* better fulfillment.\[^812]

  Avos’s second *perek* teaches that “the more we own, the more we worry.”\[^813] Poverty allows a person to live a simpler life. Having less money, property, and investments to manage affords us more time for *Avodas Hashem*.\[^814] This is why Avos’s sixth *perek* describes the Torah lifestyle as “eating bread with salt, rationing water, and sleeping on the ground.”\[^815] A Torah-inspired life focuses on developing the spiritual, not the physical, side of our existence.

  According to this explanation, Rebbe Yonasan made a profound statement about poverty. It is not (just) a challenge to overcome. It is also an *opportunity* to take advantage of; it should actually *assist* fulfillment.

  ***Sofo L’kayma Mei’osher***\*\*: Ultimate Riches\*\*  
This understanding of Rebbe Yonasan’s first words facilitates a deeper understanding of his next ones: “One who fulfills Torah *mei’oni* will eventually be *mekayem mei’osher* (fulfill with wealth).” How does fulfillment *mei’oni* lead to *kiyum* *mei’osher*?

  Many *meforshim* understand the *osher* as referring to heavenly reward in this world.\[^816] As mentioned previously, the difficulty with this interpretation is that the righteous are not always rewarded with wealth.

  The Alshich, therefore, explains the *mishnah* differently. The literal meaning of the word *“sofo* (ends up)” presents the *osher* as a *natural result* (not an external reward).\[^817] Though fulfillment *mei’oni* does not necessarily generate a heavenly reward of wealth, it does prepare us to succeed in such a state.

  Commitment to Torah in a state of poverty hinges on the ability to focus on what is truly important and helps one develop positive personality traits such as *pashtus* (simplicity), humility, and persistence. This personal development helps people maintain focus and continue serving Hashem properly even after becoming wealthy.\[^818] Rebbe Yonasan does not promise wealth. He explains what prepares us to live properly once we receive it. Living properly while poor enables one to do so when wealthy as well.

  **Taking Advantage of Our *Osher***  
In the second half of the *mishnah*, Rebbe Yonasan presents the reverse equation: “One who is *“mevatel Torah mei’osher* — negligent about Torah in a state of wealth\*”\* will be *“mevatel Torah mei’oni* — negligent about Torah in a state of poverty.” We are meant to use the *osher* that Hashem gives us to strengthen and enhance our *Avodas Hashem*. One who does not will have the *osher* taken away\[^819] from him.\[^820]

  What is the *osher* we are expected to use properly? The simple translation of the word “*osher*” is “wealth.” Hashem blesses us with wealth and resources that make our lives easier and more comfortable. We should show our appreciation for these gifts by using them to increase our commitment to *Avodas Hashem*.

  This message is particularly relevant to our generation. Hashem has blessed us with considerable wealth, comfort, and opportunities. Machines work for us and save us travel time, electricity extends our days, and the internet allows for quick communication and easy access to meaningful content anywhere at any moment. We should devote this “newfound” time and energy to Torah learning and fulfillment.

  The Ohr Hachayim explains that this is how the *mon* tested the readiness of the Jewish People for *kabbalas haTorah*.\[^821] Ready-made *mon* from heaven freed them from the need to devote time and energy to working and preparing food. Hashem wanted to see if they would use this time for meaningful pursuits. If they did, it would show they were ready to take advantage of the opportunity to learn Torah, thus deserving of it.

  Though we do not have *mon*, modern technology has freed us from backbreaking labor and afforded us much free time. We, too, need to ensure that we devote our time and energy to Torah learning and fulfillment. If we do, Hashem has good reason to continue blessing us with this “wealth.”

  In addition to wealth, Hashem blesses us with enabling abilities and enabling circumstances.\[^822] Our intellectual capacity, educational background, and social setting allow us to think, grow, and have a meaningful impact. These gifts are much more important than money and property. This is why the *gemara*\[^823] teaches that *“ein ani ela b’deah* — poverty is (only) the lack of intelligence.” True poverty is the inability to function meaningfully; true wealth is the capacity to do so.

  Each of us is meant to use the capabilities and opportunities Hashem blesses us with to serve Him. We should use our intellectual ability to study and appreciate Torah as deeply as possible and use our skills to serve Hashem to the best of our ability.

  If we use the resources and skills Hashem has granted us for the right purposes, He blesses us with more.\[^824] If we do not, He (*chas v’shalom*) takes them from us.

  \[clt]If we use the resources and skills Hashem has granted us for the right purposes, He blesses us with more. If we do not, He (*chas v’shalom*) takes them from us.

  **Challenges and Opportunities**  
Both poverty and wealth pose challenges.\[^825] Neither challenge is insurmountable. The *gemara*\[^826] teaches that after we die, we are asked why we failed to maximize our opportunities for Torah learning. Those who were poor or wealthy each use their challenging circumstances to justify their behavior. The heavenly court responds by pointing to poor and rich people (Hillel and Rebbe Elazar ben Charsum, respectively) who still found ways to devote themselves to Torah learning. When it comes to Avodas Hashem, if there is a will, there is a way.

  Poverty and wealth also offer opportunities. Poverty helps us develop healthy character traits; wealth makes life easier. We are meant to take advantage of whichever opportunities we are offered.

  May we realize that we have the ability to overcome whatever challenges Hashem places in our path and (therefore) take advantage of the opportunities Hashem offers us through our unique life circumstances. May doing so merit Hashem’s continued and increased blessings.  
**If Not Now**

  הוּא (הִלֵּל) הָיָה אוֹמֵר… וְאִם לֹא עַכְשָׁיו, אֵימָתָי (אבות א:יד):

  Hillel closes his series of statements in Avos’s first *perek* with a short, four-word question: “*Im lo achshav, eimasai? —* If not now, when?”\[^827] Now is the time to focus on important and meaningful tasks.\[^828]

  **Early Enough**  
The *meforshim* explain Hillel’s contrast between now and later in different ways.

  Rashi and the Bartenura\[^829] focus on this world as opposed to Olam Haba.\[^830] We should make sure to do *teshuvah* and *ma’asim tovim* while we can — in this world.\[^831] Once in the next world, it is too late for us to grow and earn reward.\[^832]

  The Rambam\[^833] understands the “now” as referring to youth (as opposed to old age).\[^834] It is important to choose what is right at an age when we still have the capacity and desire to potentially choose another path.

  Another reason to live properly in our youth is because of how hard it is to correct bad habits once they become part of our nature. Rabbeinu Yonah\[^835] compares a young man to a sapling and an older man to a tree. A young sapling can still be shaped, while a mature tree is already firmly formed.\[^836] We should pursue the correct path early in life when we can still develop ourselves properly.\[^837]

  **Right Away**  
The *mishnah* seems to go even further. It implies that we should take action not just in this world and while we are young but also *achshav —* now, *right away*, as soon as possible.

  Why is it important to do things right away?\[^838]

  **Before It Is Too Late**  
Rabbeinu Yonah explains that delaying may cause us to miss the opportunity entirely. We might forget to return to the good deed or lose our will to do it.\[^839]

  Additionally, the opportunity itself may pass us by. Shlomo Hamelech reminds us that “*lo seida mah yeileid yom* (you do not know what each day will bring).”\[^840] We often assume that tomorrow’s circumstances will be similar to today’s, but we are mistaken. The opportunity may be gone by tomorrow.

  In fact, we ourselves may no longer be here.\[^841] This is how Rebbe Elazar explained his encouragement to do *teshuvah* the day before we die.\[^842] When his *talmidim* responded that we do not know when we will die, Rebbe Elazar explained that this is why we should do *teshuvah* today and not wait until tomorrow.\[^843] We are mortals who do not know how long we have in this world. We should, therefore, do important things right away.\[^844]

  This idea fits nicely with the etymology of the word Hillel uses to refer to now — “*achshav*.” Many suggest that the word is an acronym for *“atah, k’mos shehu* — now, as things are (in the present).” The word itself emphasizes the fact that reality often changes quickly and dramatically.

  **Maximizing the Moment**  
Rabbeinu Yonah\[^845] adds that even if we eventually perform the task, delaying wastes the earlier moment. Life is all about taking advantage of time and opportunities. Every moment is important. Even if we accomplish tomorrow, today has been lost and cannot be retrieved.

  \[clt]Life is all about taking advantage of time and opportunities. Every moment is important. Even if we accomplish tomorrow, today has been lost and cannot be retrieved.

  The Mishnah Berurah derives an important *halachah* from this idea. The Shulchan Aruch writes that one can write down a Torah thought on Chol Hamoed if he is afraid that he might forget the idea.\[^846] The Mishnah Berurah\[^847] permits recording the idea even if one is not concerned about forgetting it. We should not delay transcribing today’s thoughts until tomorrow because tomorrow should be devoted to new ones. Every day has new potential we are meant to maximize.

  This idea applies even more significantly to *teshuvah*. The Rashbatz explains that delaying *teshuvah* not only wastes time and opportunities but also extends our life of sin longer than necessary. Doing *teshuvah* in the future improves our subsequent lives, but does not rectify the past.

  **No Time Like the Present**  
Hillel’s formulation of his teaching as a question — “If not now, when?” — makes an additional point. We sometimes justify delaying by hoping that things will become *easier* in the future, but this may not be the case. Hillel himself teaches in Avos’s next *perek*:\[^848] “Don’t say, ‘When I am free, I will learn,’ as perhaps you will not become free.” We have no reason to believe that things will become easier. In fact, they may even become more difficult. “If not now, when” — there is no reason to assume that the future will be a better time. There is, therefore, no time like (or better than) the present.

  When the *yetzer hara* sees that he cannot deter us from the proper course of action, he often reverts to a last “line of defense” — convincing us to delay.\[^849] This delay often causes us to miss critical life opportunities. Additionally, even if we eventually accomplish the task, by delaying we have missed critical opportunities and often wasted a significant portion of our lives.

  Let’s show our passion for what is right by focusing on it right away. There is no time like the present.

  **Character Development**

  Where Wisdom Begins

  A Wise Choice

  Action!

  More Action

  Doing More Than We Say

  True Character  
**Where Wisdom Begins**

  רַבִּי חֲנִינָא בֶן דּוֹסָא אוֹמֵר, כֹּל שֶׁיִּרְאַת חֶטְאוֹ קוֹדֶמֶת לְחָכְמָתוֹ, חָכְמָתוֹ מִתְקַיֶּמֶת.  
וְכֹל שֶׁחָכְמָתוֹ קוֹדֶמֶת לְיִרְאַת חֶטְאוֹ, אֵין חָכְמָתוֹ מִתְקַיֶּמֶת (אבות ג:ט)

  רַבִּי אֶלְעָזָר בֶּן עֲזַרְיָה אוֹמֵר,  
אִם אֵין תּוֹרָה, אֵין דֶּרֶךְ אֶרֶץ. אִם אֵין דֶּרֶךְ אֶרֶץ, אֵין תּוֹרָה.  
אִם אֵין חָכְמָה, אֵין יִרְאָה. *אִם אֵין יִרְאָה, אֵין חָכְמָה.*  
אִם אֵין בִּינָה, אֵין דַּעַת. אִם אֵין דַּעַת, אֵין בִּינָה.  
אִם אֵין קֶמַח, אֵין תּוֹרָה. אִם אֵין תּוֹרָה, אֵין קֶמַח (אבות ג:יז)

  **The Nexus of Chochmah and Yirah**  
At the end of Avos’s third *perek*, Rebbe Elazar ben Azaryah introduces us to four pairs of interdependent concepts. The first and the fourth pairings assert Torah’s interdependency with both *derech eretz* (living by the ways of the world) and *kemach* (“flour” and food in general)*.* Torah learning needs the support of *kemach* and breadth of *derech eretz* and also gives each  meaning.

  The middle two pairs relate to *chochmah*. The second of the two links the internal *chochmah* concepts of *binah* and *da’as* to one another, and the first establishes *chochmah*’s relationship with *yirah*.

  *Chochmah* and *yirah*, two central aspects of human experience, are generally seen as disconnected. The first resides in the mind, while the second is a matter of the heart, a matter of faith. Rebbe Elazar teaches that they are connected and, in fact, *absolutely interdependent* — neither truly exists without the other.

  ***Yirah*** **— What We Are All About**  
Moshe Rabbeinu emphasized the foundational nature of *yirah* when he identified it as “all that Hashem asks from us.”\[^850] In a world driven and controlled by Hashem, *yirah* (and the decisions and actions it inspires\[^851]) is all that is truly in our hands.\[^852] Understandably, Chazal\[^853] identify *yirah* as why Hashem created the entire world and, thus, most precious to Him.\[^854]

  We exist to achieve *yiras Shamayim*.\[^855] It is the goal and purpose of all the *mitzvos*.\[^856] Rav Elchonon\[^857] took this a step further. Based on a *pasuk* that presents *yirah* (and *mitzvah* observance) as *“kol ha’Adam,”*\[^858] he explained that *yirah* is a condition for maintaining a proper human standard\*.\* Without it, people inevitably descend to animalistic behavior.

  \[clt]*Yirah* is a condition for maintaining a proper human standard\*.\* Without it, people inevitably descend to animalistic behavior.

  **A Wise Foundation**  
In our *mishnah,* Rebbe Elazar ben Azaryah adds that (in addition to its general importance) *yirah* is *particularly* important for *chochmah*. Based on a *pasuk* in Sefer Yeshayahu (that enumerates other qualities that Chazal correlate with the six orders of Shas),\[^859] the *gemara* describes *yirah* as *chochmah*’s “storehouse.”\[^860] A person can learn all of Shas, but without *yirah,* he has nowhere to “store” his learning. *Chochmah* cannot stand alone. In Avos’s fourth *perek,\[^861]* Rebbe Nehorai formulated it this way: “Do not ‘lean’ upon your wisdom.” Wisdom alone is unreliable.

  Many *pesukim* in Tehillim\[^862] and Mishlei\[^863] (one of which we recite upon awakening in the morning) go a step further by describing *yiras Hashem* as the *beginning* (*techilas* and *reishis*) of *chochmah*. *Yiras* *Hashem* is the first step towards developing knowledge. Sefer Mishlei, which teaches the importance of *chochmah* and how to acquire it, opens by emphasizing that *yirah* is how we need to begin this acquisition. Recognition of Hashem and His place in the world is *chochmah*’s first principle.

  Rebbe Chanina ben Dosa went even further\[^864] (earlier in Avos’s third *perek*\[^865]) by presenting *yirah* as not only as *chochmah’s* “beginning” but also its *prerequisite*. *Yirah* ensures that we develop our *chochmah* properly. The *gemara*\[^866] explains that, though Torah can be a life-sustaining or even life-*saving* drug, if studied by one lacking *yiras Shamayim,* it can also be poisonous.\[^867] How we approach Torah determines our appreciation of it and its impact on our lives; we must approach Torah and wisdom with an attitude of *yiras Shamayim*.

  **How Chochmah Has Staying Power**  
Rebbe Chanina focused on an additional dimension: “*chachmaso miskayemes,”* \*chochmah’\*s staying power. He taught that “*chachmaso miskayemes”* hinges on *“yiras cheto kodemes l’chachmaso.”* Only *chochmah* preceded by *yirah* remains with us.\[^868]

  The words of Rashi and the GRA can help us understand why. Rashi\[^869] and the GRA\[^870] explain that *Yiras Shamayim* gives our wisdom not only direction but also significance. Recognizing Hashem as Creator helps us put what we learn from His world in perspective. We see it as the wisdom of G-d, which He reveals to us with the intention that we appreciate, retain, and live by it.

  **A Reciprocal Relationship**  
In addition to *chochmah’s* dependency on *yirah*, Rebbe Elazar ben Azarya also emphasized *yirah’s* dependency upon *chochmah*. Just as *chochmah* needs *yirah*, so *yirah* depends upon *chochmah* as a prerequisite. How can *yirah* and *chochmah* both be prerequisites for one another? Which one needs to come first?

  The next chapter addresses these questions.  
**A Wise Choice**

  רַבִּי אֶלְעָזָר בֶּן עֲזַרְיָה אוֹמֵר,  
אִם אֵין תּוֹרָה, אֵין דֶּרֶךְ אֶרֶץ. אִם אֵין דֶּרֶךְ אֶרֶץ, אֵין תּוֹרָה.  
*אִם אֵין חָכְמָה, אֵין יִרְאָה*. אִם אֵין יִרְאָה, אֵין חָכְמָה.  
אִם אֵין בִּינָה, אֵין דַּעַת. אִם אֵין דַּעַת, אֵין בִּינָה.  
אִם אֵין קֶמַח, אֵין תּוֹרָה. אִם אֵין תּוֹרָה, אֵין קֶמַח (אבות ג:יז)

  **A Reciprocal Relationship**  
The last chapter focused upon Rebbe Chanina ben Dosa’s linkage of *chochmah* to *yirah*. *Yirah* is wisdom’s first principle and needs to precede it for wisdom to be sustainable. Rebbe Elazar ben Azaryah reaffirms Rebbe Chanina’s (earlier) statement and also links the two in the opposite direction: just as *chochmah* depends on *yirah*, so *yirah* hinges upon *chochmah*. *Chochmah* is essential, not just as an independent value but also as a condition for *yirah*.

  Earlier in Avos,\[^871] Rabban Gamliel expressed this idea by asserting that “a brute cannot fear sin.” A person who lacks wisdom cannot properly appreciate Hashem’s place in the world and the severity of defying His Will.

  Sefer Mishlei goes even further by presenting *chochmah* as a prerequisite that needs to *precede yirah* in order to facilitate it. Only once a person has achieved wisdom can he “understand *Yiras Elokim* and attain knowledge of God.”\[^872]

  **Who’s On First?**  
*Chochmah* as a prerequisite to *yirah* seems to contradict Rebbe Chanina’s opposite portrayal of *yirah* as a pre-condition for *chochmah*. Rebbe Chanina emphasizes the importance of *yirah* preceding *chochmah,* while the pasuk in Mishlei underscores the need for *chochmah* to precede *yirah*. Which one precedes the other — *yirah* or *chochmah*?

  This question also emerges from the *gemara* in Shabbos,\[^873] which presents statements that support both directions. The *gemara* first quotes Rabbah bar Rav Huna, who compares someone who learns Torah but lacks *yiras Shamayim* to a treasurer who possesses keys to the treasury’s inner chamber but not to its outer doors. We cannot achieve *chochmah* without having *yirah* first.

  In the *very next line*, the *gemara* quotes Rav Yannai, who compares the same person to one who erects an entranceway to a courtyard that does not exist. While Rabbah saw *yirah* as the door through which one enters the world of *chochmah*, Rav Yannai saw *yirah* as the end goal to which *chochmah* leads. Which one is the entry, and which one is the goal?

  **Levels of Yirah**  
The Abarbanel,\[^874] and, later, the GRA\[^875] and the Ba’al Hatanya\[^876] explain that there are different types and levels of *Yiras Shamayim*.\[^877] The process of *yirah* and *chochmah* is meant to begin with one kind of *yirah* and ultimately (through the acquisition of *chochmah*) facilitate a second, higher form. What are these two types of *yirah*?

  On the most basic level, *yirah* means fear. We should fear sin because we fear Hashem and His potential punishment. Though Tanach sometimes uses the word *“pachad”* to describe this emotion,\[^878] we also find *yirah* used this way by Sefer Bereishis (see its description of Yitzchak’s fear of the Plishtim\[^879] and Yaakov’s fear of Eisav).\[^880] Accordingly, the Rambam\[^881] defines the mitzvah of *Yiras Shamayim*\[^882] as fearing Hashem and His punishment.

  *Yirah* also means awe. For example, Sefer Bereishis also describes Yaakov as having *yirah* when he awoke after his vision of the ladder and the angels.\[^883] He was in awe (not afraid) of the place and Hashem’s presence there.

  **Stages of Yirah**  
These two meanings of *yirah* are very different. The young or uneducated, who are unable to fully appreciate the awesomeness of Hashem’s presence, should be taught to fear Him.\[^884] Experience and education help people gain a deeper appreciation for, and depth of awareness of, Hashem — a sense of awe towards Him. This deeper appreciation is rooted in wisdom.

  The Rambam develops this idea in Hilchos Yesodei HaTorah,\[^885] where he teaches that, in addition to belief in Hashem, we must feel love and *yirah* towards Him. How do we develop emotional feelings towards someone we have never met? The Rambam explains that we get there by appreciating Him through studying His world.

  This higher form of *yirah* is the one that is ultimately significant. It was the purpose of creation\[^886] and is the goal of our personal development.\[^887] G-d does not seek our fear; He seeks our appreciation.

  *Yirah is* thus both a necessary backdrop to *chochmah* as well as its beneficiary. An initial fear of Hashem inspires taking learning, wisdom, and life seriously.\[^888] The learning and wisdom achieved, in turn, facilitates a higher level of *yirah*.

  \[clt]*Yirah is* thus both a necessary backdrop to *chochmah* as well as its beneficiary. An initial fear of Hashem inspires taking learning, wisdom, and life seriously.\[^889] The learning and wisdom achieved, in turn, facilitates a higher level of *yirah*.

  Rashi\[^890] has a powerful way of linking the two types of *yirah* and their stages of development. He explains that because wisdom’s goal is *yirah*, it needs to be motivated by it. The direction our *chochmah* leads us in hinges on its roots and motivation. Wisdom we associate with Hashem will inspire us to appreciate Him more.

  **An Integrated Cohesive Existence**  
At first glance, wisdom and *yirah* seem like unrelated aspects of our consciousness. The first is in the mind, while the second is in the heart. Rebbe Elazar ben Azaryah teaches us that healthy personal development hinges on integrating both. *Yirah* is where wisdom begins and what ensures its proper direction and significance. At the same time, true *yirah* flows from wisdom that makes a meaningful appreciation of Hashem possible.

  May Hashem help us succeed in pursuing *both properly* — in the merit of our intention to do so!  
**Action!**

  רַבִּי חֲנִינָא בֶן דּוֹסָא אוֹמֵר… כָּל שֶׁמַּעֲשָׂיו מְרֻבִּין מֵחָכְמָתוֹ, חָכְמָתוֹ מִתְקַיֶּמֶת. וְכָל שֶׁחָכְמָתוֹ מְרֻבָּה מִמַּעֲשָׂיו, אֵין חָכְמָתוֹ מִתְקַיֶּמֶת (אבות ג:ט):

  הַכֹּל צָפוּי, וְהָרְשׁוּת נְתוּנָה, ובְטוֹב הָעוֹלָם נִדּוֹן. וְהַכֹּל לְפִי רֹב הַמַּעֲשה (אבות ג:טו):

  רַבִּי אֶלְעָזָר בֶּן עֲזַרְיָה אוֹמֵר… כָּל שֶׁחָכְמָתוֹ מְרֻבָּה מִמַּעֲשָׂיו, לְמַה הוּא דוֹמֶה, לְאִילָן שֶׁעֲנָפָיו מְרֻבִּין וְשָׁרָשָׁיו מֻעָטִין, וְהָרוּחַ בָּאָה וְעוֹקַרְתּוֹ וְהוֹפַכְתּוֹ עַל פָּנָיו, שֶׁנֶּאֱמַר (ירמיה יז) וְהָיָה כְּעַרְעָר בָּעֲרָבָה וְלֹא יִרְאֶה כִּי יָבוֹא טוֹב וְשָׁכַן חֲרֵרִים בַּמִּדְבָּר אֶרֶץ מְלֵחָה וְלֹא תֵשֵׁב.  
אֲבָל כָּל שֶׁמַּעֲשָׂיו מְרֻבִּין מֵחָכְמָתוֹ, לְמַה הוּא דוֹמֶה, לְאִילָן שֶׁעֲנָפָיו מֻעָטִין וְשָׁרָשָׁיו מְרֻבִּין, שֶׁאֲפִלּוּ כָל הָרוּחוֹת שֶׁבָּעוֹלָם בָּאוֹת וְנוֹשְׁבוֹת בּוֹ אֵין מְזִיזִין אוֹתוֹ מִמְּקוֹמוֹ, שֶׁנֶּאֱמַר (שם) וְהָיָה כְּעֵץ שָׁתוּל עַל מַיִם וְעַל יוּבַל יְשַׁלַּח שָׁרָשָׁיו וְלֹא יִרְאֶה כִּי יָבֹא חֹם, וְהָיָה עָלֵהוּ רַעֲנָן, וּבִשְׁנַת בַּצֹּרֶת לֹא יִדְאָג, וְלֹא יָמִישׁ מֵעֲשׂוֹת פֶּרִי (אבות ג:יז):

  **The Need for Deed**  
In previous chapters, we saw how Rebbe Chanina ben Dosa and Rebbe Elazar ben Azarya condition *chochmah* on *yirah*. These same *Tannaim* also link *chochmah* to *ma’asim* (actions/deeds). We learn from the two *Tannaim* that wisdom should reflect faith and be rooted in action.\[^891]

  **Our Ultimate Central Goal**  
Though we attribute great value to Torah learning and accumulating knowledge (*talmud Torah k’neged kulam*\[^892]), we see action as more important. As Rebbe Shimon ben Rabban Gamliel taught in Avos’s first *perek*: “*Lo hamidrash hu ha’ikar, ella hama’aseh.”*\[^893]

  Action is of supreme importance. It shows that our beliefs are truly important to us\[^894] and transforms them into a sustainable reality. It is also how we internalize and remind ourselves\[^895] of the feelings we hope to ingrain. In the words of the Rambam: “Beliefs that lack accompanying deeds which give them real expression and help educate and create consistent consciousness (of the beliefs) among the masses will eventually be forgotten.”\[^896]

  This is why Judaism focuses on action and why action is a condition for entry into the next world. Rebbe Akiva went even further by identifying action as the *sole* factor that determines our heavenly reward and punishment.\[^897]

  This is why Rebbe Yossi ben Kisma told Rebbe Chanina ben Teradyon that (only) the latter’s good deeds merited him a place in Olam Haba.\[^898] Rebbe Chanina was known for his absolute commitment to Jewish principles; he would eventually be one of the ten martyrs murdered by the Romans. This commitment and sacrifice were not enough; his actions and deeds gained him entry into the next world.

  **Learning’s Critical Complement**  
*Ma’asim* should be learning’s ultimate goal. Later in Avos, Rebbe Yishmael ben Beroka defines ideal learning as one meant to facilitate action.\[^899] Rava often opined that “*teshuvah* and *ma’asim tovim* are the *tachlis* (goal) of *chochmah*.”\[^900] Ultimately, it is the *ma’asim* inspired by our learning that make learning of supreme importance. In the words of the *gemara*, “Learning is great(est) because it facilitates *ma’aseh*.”\[^901]

  *Ma’asim tovim* are not just Torah learning’s ideal goal but also its critical complement. Rebbe Yossi taught that someone who says he has *only* Torah lacks *even* Torah.\[^902] Rav Pappa added that anyone involved in study must also do good deeds; anyone who does not is considered not to be involved in learning either.

  \[clt]Anyone involved in study must also do good deeds; anyone who does not is considered not to be involved in learning either.

  Many contemporary *meforshim* use this idea to explain the *halachah* that we only place *tefilin* on our heads only after we have placed them upon our arms. We can only develop our minds after we have committed ourselves to the actions symbolized by the *tefilin* on our arms.

  **Whole Life**  
Understandably, the combination of Torah learning and good deeds is life’s ideal form. The *gemara*\[^903] explains that Abaye lived twenty years longer than Rabbah because he engaged in both good deeds and Torah learning, and not just the latter.\[^904] Not surprisingly, later in Avos,\[^905] (the afore-mentioned) Rebbe Yossi ben Kisma identifies *the two* as the only things we take with us when we leave this world.\[^906]

  For this reason, our prayers for children focus on *both* Torah and *ma’asim tovim*. At a *bris milah*, we pray that “just as the child entered the *bris*, so should he ‘enter into’ Torah and *ma’asim tovim*.” When lighting candles on Friday nights, women pray that Hashem help them raise children who use Torah learning and good deeds to enlighten the world.

  **More Ma’asim**  
In addition to stressing the importance of *ma’asim* in general and to *chochmah* in particular, Rebbe Chanina ben Dosa and Rebbe Elazar ben Azariyah encourage investing *more* in *ma’asim* than in *chochmah*. Why do we need more *ma’asim* than wisdom?

  There are two answers to this question, and they connect (respectively) to the two lines of Rebbe Elazar’s mishnah.\[^907] The last part of the *mishnah* warns against a situation where our *chochmah* exceeds our *ma’asim*. Rashi sees this as describing one who does not translate his wisdom into action. A situation where wisdom remains theoretical and does not determine the way we live our lives is problematic.

  Wisdom and emotions become real and significant only when they morph into action. The Ramban\[^908] explains that translating inspiration into deed is the *only way* to ensure that feelings remain part of our lives.\[^909] Rebbe Menachem Mendel MiVitebsk connects this to the *gemara’s* expectation that one who sees a *sotah’s* (unfaithful wife) sad fate should commit to separating from wine (by becoming a *nazir*).\[^910] Our learning and life experiences are only meaningful if we translate lessons into action.

  If non-actualization were the only concern, an amount of *ma’asim equal* to one’s *chochmah* would suffice. However, the *first* line of the *mishnah* teaches that wisdom’s sustainability hinges on our actions *surpassing* (the amount of) our wisdom. Why is this necessary? How is this *possible*?

  We will address these questions in our next chapter.  
**More Action**

  רַבִּי חֲנִינָא בֶן דּוֹסָא אוֹמֵר…  
כֹּל שֶׁיִּרְאַת חֶטְאוֹ קוֹדֶמֶת לְחָכְמָתוֹ, חָכְמָתוֹ מִתְקַיֶּמֶת. וְכֹל שֶׁחָכְמָתוֹ קוֹדֶמֶת לְיִרְאַת חֶטְאוֹ, אין חכמתו מתקיימת  
כָּל שֶׁמַּעֲשָׂיו מְרֻבִּין מֵחָכְמָתוֹ, חָכְמָתוֹ מִתְקַיֶּמֶת. וְכָל שֶׁחָכְמָתוֹ מְרֻבָּה מִמַּעֲשָׂיו, אֵין חָכְמָתוֹ מִתְקַיֶּמֶת (אבות ג:ט):

  רַבִּי אֶלְעָזָר בֶּן עֲזַרְיָה אוֹמֵר…  
כָּל שֶׁחָכְמָתוֹ מְרֻבָּה מִמַּעֲשָׂיו, לְמַה הוּא דוֹמֶה?  
לְאִילָן שֶׁעֲנָפָיו מְרֻבִּין וְשָׁרָשָׁיו מֻעָטִין, וְהָרוּחַ בָּאָה וְעוֹקַרְתּוֹ וְהוֹפַכְתּוֹ עַל פָּנָיו…  
אֲבָל כָּל שֶׁמַּעֲשָׂיו מְרֻבִּין מֵחָכְמָתוֹ, לְמַה הוּא דוֹמֶה?  
לְאִילָן שֶׁעֲנָפָיו מֻעָטִין וְשָׁרָשָׁיו מְרֻבִּין, שֶׁאֲפִלּוּ כָל הָרוּחוֹת שֶׁבָּעוֹלָם בָּאוֹת וְנוֹשְׁבוֹת בּוֹ אֵין מְזִיזִין אוֹתוֹ מִמְּקוֹמוֹ… שֶׁנֶּאֱמַר (שם), וְהָיָה כְּעֵץ שָׁתוּל עַל מַיִם וְעַל יוּבַל יְשַׁלַּח שָׁרָשָׁיו וְלֹא יִרְאֶה כִי יָבֹא חֹם, וְהָיָה עָלֵהוּ רַעֲנָן, *ובִשְׁנַת בַּצֹּרֶת לֹא יִדְאָג*, וְלֹא יָמִישׁ מֵעֲשׂוֹת פֶּרִי:  
(אבות ג:יז):

  **More Action**  
In the last chapter, we saw that Rebbe Chanina ben Dosa and Rebbe Elazar ben Azarya highlight the importance of action — both generally and, specifically, as a complement to wisdom. The two *Tannaim* emphasize not only the *problem* of action falling short of wisdom but also the *need* for action to *surpass* it.

  Performing less action than what wisdom dictates is obviously problematic; we need to translate our wisdom from the theoretical to the practical. But why should our deeds need to *exceed* our wisdom?

  The *meforshim* answer that the *mishnah* mandates action extending beyond (as opposed to depending upon) our *understanding* of the reasons for our actions.

  The Meiri is one example of this approach. He explains that the *mishnah* teaches us to do more than just what pure reason dictates. He presents *lifnim mi’shuras ha’din* (supererogatory conduct) as an example of this idea.\[^911] *Halachah* expects (relatively) minimal action; caring, sensitive, and passionate people exceed these expectations.

  ADRN (Avos D'Rebbe Nosson) seems to explain the need for action to extend beyond reason differently because it connects the *mishnah* to Chazal’s understanding of *“na’aseh v’nishma”* — that the Jews committed themselves to observe Torah and *mitzvos* before they heard the details.\[^912] Proper commitment to *Avodas Hashem* requires us to fulfill *mitzvos* *even before* we understand their rationale; in this way, actions exceed wisdom.\[^913]

  We find support for ADRN’s explanation in the \*bitachon-\*focused *pasuk* Rebbe Elazar quotes as a prooftext for his teaching (“*u’vishnas batzores lo yid’ag”*). Acting before understanding hinges upon trusting that the Torah and *mitzvos* Hashem gave us are truly valuable and meaningful.

  **Sustaining Wisdom**  
Action exceeding wisdom is more than just an important ideal; it is a *condition for sustaining wisdom*. Rebbe Elazar ben Azarya underscores this point by comparing the wisdom-action relationship to that of a tree’s branches and roots.\[^914] One might think that wisdom is the root from which action sprouts, but Rebbe Elazar teaches us that, ideally, wisdom stems from action.

  Active performance of *mitzvos* and good deeds assist us in developing a proper understanding of life and the world.\[^915] Shabbos is an excellent example. Mere study of the laws of Shabbos cannot provide a true appreciation of the day; only putting these laws into practice and thereby experiencing the day can provide this. Appreciating this point should inspire us to ground our lives and broader perspectives in action.

  Rabbeinu Yonah took this idea further to explain why wisdom’s sustainability hinges on *greater* action. Since wisdom stems from deeds, one who values wisdom will act beyond what their wisdom dictates, hoping this action will foster additional wisdom. Not working this way indicates a lack of appreciation for wisdom, and wisdom that is not appreciated is unsustainable.

  **A Healthy Balance**  
Rebbe Elazar ben Azarya’s illustrative comparison between man and tree helps us understand the significant impact balancing wisdom and action has on our spiritual fortitude. One whose actions exceed his wisdom has a strong and secure identity and belief system — he is well-grounded, stable, and protected from passing winds.

  One whose actions depend on his wisdom and understanding, however, can be swayed by the “winds” of passing ideas and ideologies; he may find himself, to use Rebbe Elazar ben Azarya’s term, “flipped on his head.”

  \[clt]One whose actions depend on his wisdom and understanding, however, can be swayed by the “winds” of passing ideas and ideologies; he may find himself, to use Rebbe Elazar ben Azarya’s term, “flipped on his head.”

  A person focused on action has two feet on the ground. He has a strong foundation and is impervious to the blustery winds of changing times.

  **Wisdom’s Context**  
The past few chapters have discussed Rebbe Chanina ben Dosa’s two statements about wisdom’s sustainability. Wisdom is of great value and worth pursuing, but its stability and sustainability hinge on it being preceded by *yirah* (fear of G-d) and exceeded by *ma’aseh*. A *yirah* foundation gives the wisdom acquired (afterward) true meaning, while excessive action provides wisdom with solid footing and fertile ground from which to grow.

  May we appreciate the importance of *yirah* and *ma’aseh* and use both to develop and sustain healthy, meaningful wisdom!  
**Doing More Than We Say**

  שַׁמַּאי אוֹמֵר… אֱמֹר מְעַט וַעֲשֵׂה הַרְבֵּה… (אבות א:טו)

  שִׁמְעוֹן בְּנוֹ אוֹמֵר, כָּל יָמַי גָּדַלְתִּי בֵין הַחֲכָמִים,  
וְלֹא מָצָאתִי לַגּוּף טוֹב אֶלָּא שְׁתִיקָה.  
וְלֹא הַמִּדְרָשׁ הוּא הָעִקָּר, אֶלָּא הַמַּעֲשֶׂה.  
וְכָל הַמַּרְבֶּה דְבָרִים, מֵבִיא חֵטְא (אבות א:יז)

  Toward the end of Avos’s first *perek*, we find a four-word statement of Shamay: “*Emor me’at va’aseh harbeh* (Say a little and do a lot).” Though only four words long, the statement teaches us two important lessons.

  ***Emor Me’at***\*\*: Say A Little\*\*  
The first half of Shamay’s statement was elaborated upon two generations later by Rashbag (Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel), who reported that his years of growing up amongst the wise taught him the virtue of silence.\[^916]

  Rashbag’s conclusion reminds us of the “ancient” saying quoted by the *gemara*: “If one word is worth a *selah* coin, (then) being quiet is worth two.”\[^917] Our words are often inaccurate,\[^918] extreme, hurtful, unnecessarily revealing, or a commitment to fulfill what we are not actually able or ready to.\[^919] The Sefer Chassidim adds: “We often regret what we say, but we rarely regret being quiet. Before we speak, we control our words. Afterwards, our words control us.”\[^920] How often do we look back and wish we had not spoken (or posted) rashly and irresponsibly?

  Rashbag adds that “all those who speak a lot facilitate sin.”\[^921] The Rambam\[^922] explains that our words invariably include sinful components. Increasing the amount we speak ultimately increases the amount we sin.

  This idea is even more pertinent in contemporary times. Verbal abuse and *lashon hara* are prevalent in today’s culture, and social media amplifies our words. Because both have become so prevalent, we risk becoming inured to them. Therefore, we must be exceedingly careful to reinforce the importance of the proper perspective: we must watch how much we speak, how we speak, and what we speak about.

  \[ctl]Verbal abuse and *lashon hara* are prevalent in today’s culture, and social media amplifies our words… We must watch how much we speak, how we speak, and what we speak about.

  The Rambam also connects Rashbag’s assertion to a broader idea found in Sefer Koheles. Shlomo HaMelech encourages man to limit his words because “we are down here, and God is above.”\[^923] We should realize that our view of reality is limited; therefore, we should think carefully before speaking. Our *yiras Shamayim*, our recognition that we live in Hashem’s world, should impact our conduct. Realizing that Hashem sees and knows much more than us should inspire us to speak cautiously and with humility.

  ***Aseh Harbeh***\*\*: Do A Lot\*\*  
Shamay’s message is not just about caution with speech. It also contrasts *amirah* (speaking) with *asiyah* (action). Many people spend their lives talking without acting upon their words. Shamay encourages us to invest in action; our words should be few because we focus on “doing” instead.

  \[clt]Our words should be few because we focus on “doing” instead.

  **Tzadikim and Reshaim**  
ADRN (Avos D'Rebbe Nosson)\[^924] and the *gemara*\[^925] see the balance between talk and action as the difference between *reshaim* and *tzadikim*; *reshaim* speak a lot and act a little, while *tzadikim* do the opposite.

  ADRN presents Ephron and Avraham Avinu as paradigms of each model, respectively.\[^926] Ephron initially offered Avraham *Ma’aras Hamachpelah* for free,\[^927] but later said, “What is land worth four hundred shekel between friends?,”\[^928] implying that he expected (that amount as) payment. The Torah tells us Avraham even had to pay Ephron in cash up front,\[^929] as he would accept no less.

  Avraham Avinu, on the other hand, promised his guests a mere *“pas lechem* (a small amount of bread)*,”*[*^930*](Ibid,18:5.) but actually gave them nine *sea’in* of bread\*\[^931]\* and choice cuts of meat.\[^932] Avraham and the *tzaddikim* who follow his lead do not “talk a big game.” They commit to very little and then provide much more.

  **Personal Growth**  
Rav Chaim Volozhin\[^933] applied Shamay’s words to our personal goals and growth. We should always see our spiritual state as “very little” and aim to accomplish much more. “Say little” about what you have done and seek further development.

  Rav Chaim’s application reminds us of Rabbeinu Yonah’s\[^934] explanation of Rabban Yochanan ben Zakkai's directive that one who has learned much of Torah “should not claim credit for himself.”\[^935] Rabbeinu Yonah explains that irrespective of how much we have learned, we should always see ourselves as less than halfway to where we need to be. Though Rabban Yochanan ben Zakay had mastered many areas of Torah and secular knowledge,\[^936] he saw himself as not even halfway “there”.

  We live in a world where people like to speak highly about themselves and their accomplishments. Instead, we should aim to reach higher levels and accomplish additional goals.

  May Shamay’s teachings remind us to be cautious about what we say and redirect our focus onto our actions!  
**True Character**

  בֶּן זוֹמָא אוֹמֵר, אֵיזֶהוּ חָכָם, הַלּוֹמֵד מִכָּל אָדָם, שֶׁנֶּאֱמַר (תהלים קיט) מִכָּל מְלַמְּדַי הִשְׂכַּלְתִּי כִּי עֵדְוֹתֶיךָ שִׂיחָה לִּי.  
אֵיזֶהוּ גִבּוֹר, הַכּוֹבֵשׁ אֶת יִצְרוֹ, שֶׁנֶּאֱמַר (משלי טז) טוֹב אֶרֶךְ אַפַּיִם מִגִּבּוֹר וּמשֵׁל בְּרוּחוֹ מִלֹּכֵד עִיר.  
אֵיזֶהוּ עָשִׁיר, הַשָּׂמֵחַ בְּחֶלְקוֹ, שֶׁנֶּאֱמַר (תהלים קכח) יְגִיעַ כַּפֶּיךָ כִּי תֹאכֵל אַשְׁרֶיךָ וְטוֹב לָךְ. אַשְׁרֶיךָ, בָּעוֹלָם הַזֶּה. וְטוֹב לָךְ, לָעוֹלָם הַבָּא.  
אֵיזֶהוּ מְכֻבָּד, הַמְכַבֵּד אֶת הַבְּרִיּוֹת, שֶׁנֶּאֱמַר (שמואל א ב) כִּי מְכַבְּדַי אֲכַבֵּד וּבֹזַי יֵקָלּוּ: (אבות ד:א)

  Each of *Maseches Avos’s perakim begins with a central teaching.\[^937] The fourth perek opens with Ben Zoma’s definition of four characters: the chacham (wise), the gibor (strong), the ashir (rich), and the mechubad (respected).*

  **Not The Ultimate Values**  
The first three characters — *chacham, gibor,* and *ashir* — were previously mentioned together by Yirmiyahu HaNavi,\[^938] who discouraged taking pride in possessing these attributes. Though society values these traits and people invest significant time and effort to attain them, they should not be our ultimate goals and sources of pride.

  Instead, Yirmiyahu recommends taking pride in knowing Hashem and living according to His values: *chesed*, *mishpat,* and *tzedakah* (kindness, justice, and fairness). They are what Hashem values and, thus, what our lives should be about. Living *this* way is something to be proud of.

  **Still Valuable**  
Though these traits are not the ultimate goals, they are still somewhat valuable. Even Hashem values them. The *gemara*\[^939] asserts that Hashem only “rests His Presence upon” (i.e., only offers prophecy through) someone with these characteristics.\[^940] Ben Zoma also attributes value to these traits and helps us define them properly.\[^941]

  In addition, Ben Zoma also mentions a fourth characteristic — “*mechubad.*” Most people’s ultimate goal is to attain respect. Some hope to be respected for their wisdom, others for their strength, and still others because of their wealth. Ben Zoma recognizes people’s yearning for respect and relates to this trait as well.\[^942]

  **Internal and External**  
Though Ben Zoma addresses each trait separately, his words about all four share a common goal: to correct our thinking about which characteristics depend upon our internal, personal perspective and which hinge upon things external to ourselves.

  People measure *ashirus* by money and possessions and *gevurah* by the ability to control other people or things. Ben Zoma disagrees and explains that both traits are actually internal.\[^943] The true *gibor* is the one who has the strength to control *himself*\[^944] and the real *ashir* is the one *satisfied* with what he has.\[^945]

  Why should we value the ability to control others? Even if that had value, what difference does that ability make if we cannot control ourselves? Furthermore, self-control is harder to achieve than the control of others.\[^946] Self-control is both a true accomplishment as well as the accomplishment that truly matters.

  \[ctl]Self-control is both a true accomplishment as well as the accomplishment that truly matters.

  True *ashirus* is also internal. What significance do material riches have if they do not make us happy? Many rich people spend their lives desperately trying to acquire more wealth. No matter how much they have, their jealousy of others keeps them from being satisfied with their lot.\[^947] Being happy with Hashem’s blessings\[^948] makes us truly rich. We live happily with the knowledge that we have what we are meant to have.

  Ben Zoma has a similar approach to *chochmah* and *kavod* but in the *reverse direction*. People generally see a *mechubad* as someone respected *by others*.
