Ben Zoma defines the *mechubad* as one who *respects others*. The one who respects others is inherently respectable and thus deserves respect.  
Sadly, many people are “full of themselves” — they feel they are “G-d’s gift to humanity.” In truth, what truly makes us special is that we are created in Hashem’s image. This attribute is, of course, shared by all of humanity. An accurate appreciation of ourselves should bring us to a similar appreciation of *all other* human beings.\[^949] This appreciation makes us worthy of respect.\[^950]

  \[clt]An accurate appreciation of ourselves should bring us to a similar appreciation of *all other* human beings. This appreciation makes us worthy of respect.

  Ben Zoma defines a *chacham* in a similar way. People generally assume that a *chacham* is intrinsically intelligent. Ben Zoma explains it otherwise: the *chacham* is the one who learns from all others. No one knows it all. Hashem scatters wisdom amongst all those He creates in His image. The more people we learn from, the wiser we become. The ultimate *chacham* is the one who learns from *everyone*.\[^951]

  **What We Accomplish**  
The Midrash Shmuel and Nesivos Shalom highlight another thread common to Ben Zoma’s teachings. The blessings of wisdom, strength, and wealth are all G-d-given, thus not reasons to be prideful or shown respect. It is what we achieve on our own that is praiseworthy.

  \[ctl]The blessings of wisdom, strength, and wealth are all G-d-given, thus not reasons to be prideful or shown respect. It is what we achieve on our own that is praiseworthy.

  We should not be proud of our natural intelligence but, rather, of the effort we invest in learning from others. We should not celebrate natural strength; we should celebrate the self-control we develop. We have no reason to show off the riches Hashem gifts us; instead, we should be proud of our appreciation for His giving them to us. We should not be proud of the respect others show us; we should be proud of our understanding of the basis and importance of showing respect to others.

  May Ben Zoma’s words help us appreciate the true meaning of wisdom, strength, wealth, and honor, and may they inspire us to focus on the personal growth that helps us achieve their proper form!

All Jews should see their existence as inclusive of the entire Jewish people. The “complete” person sees his existence as inclusive of all of humanity. This idea can explain another statement of Hillel. Talmud Bavli, Sukkah 53a tells us that Hillel would exclaim at the *Simchat Beis Hasho’eivah,* “If I am here, everything is here.” What did he mean by that surprising statement? Along these lines, he may have been saying that because he saw himself as inextricably linked to all of Hashem’s creations, his presence anywhere meant that all of creation was there as well.

  \[^168]:  Yeshayahu Hanavi writes: “For My thoughts are not like your thoughts (Yeshayahu 55:8).” The Rambam (Mishneh Torah, Hilchos Teshuva 5:5) explains that “it is not within the power of the knowledge of man to attain this matter of his having been created clearly, just as it is not within the power of man to attain and grasp the truth of the Creator.” In response to being asked why Hashem created the world, Rav Chaim Soloveitchik explained that we, of finite understanding and comprehension, are not able to know why; all we can know is what our purpose and goal ought to be. The Abarbanel explained that, at the most, we can look at the world and understand from it what our goal should be. Rav Yosef Dov Soloveitchik formulated it this way: “We can rarely ask *why* when it comes to G-d, but we can ask *what.”* This “what” is what the mishnah addresses.

For more on the approach of the Baal HaTanya and the contrast with that of the Ramchal, see *Mataras Habriyah V’Tachlis Ha’Adam*, Rav Yehudah Cohen, Maayanotecha- Riv’on L’Machsheves Chabad 48, pg. 16.

  \[^208]:  Medrash Rabbah, Bamidbar 5:6. This idea is expressed by Moshe Rabbeinu in his *tefilah* to Hashem after the *chet ha’egel* (Shemos 32:12). Moshe defends the Jews with the claim that their destruction would negatively impact G-d’s own name and reputation. In addition to our existence playing a positive role, our destruction (*chas v’shalom*) would have a negative impact.

  \[^212]:  Rav Kook, in a very powerful piece (Olas Hare’iyah, Likutim pg. 247), writes about how *shalom* and *achdus* can be rooted either in simple utilitarian goals of wanting to be able to function together as a society, or, ideally, in the sharing of common goals. When it is the former, the *achdus* lasts only as long as the utilitarian benefits apply. When the peace and unity are motivated by shared goals, they can truly be long lasting.

  \[^213]:  This joint definition and mission should be the basis of a healthy marriage. This is why the first *brachah* of *Sheva Berachos* is “*She’hakol bara lichvodo.*” Though this *brachah* does not mention marriage, we begin with it because awareness of and commitment to this joint goal can help a couple transcend their natural selfishness and commit to live and work together with one another. This idea is expressed in the Tehillim 128 (recited by German Jews at the chuppah), which describes how *yirei Shamayim* have their family unified around them. They provide their family with a higher mission they can all rally around.

  \[^260]:  It is noteworthy that Avraham is the only person called “*ohavi* (my lover)” by Hashem. See Yeshaya 41:8 and the Rambam (Mishneh Torah, Hilchos Teshuva 10:2). It is also interesting that Bilam, who Chazal saw as Avraham’s antithesis (*Avos* 5:19), differed from Avraham in this way. Though Hashem made it clear to Bilam that He did not want him to agree to curse the Jewish people, Bilam proceeded anyway once Hashem removed his formal objection. Bilam respected G-d’s formal commandments, but was not interested in His Will. (See Kovetz Divrei Sofrim (1:23–24)).

  \[^268]:  See Chovos Halevavos (Shaar Avodas Elokim, Perek 9), who compares life in this world to one given access to riches for only a year. If he is wise, the person will seek to move the riches to the place he will be after the year.

  \[^269]:  The Radvaz (She’eilos u’Teshuvot Radvaz, 3:555) talks about someone who didn’t cry when he lost a relative because he said “he was now in a better place.” He mentioned to him that this isn’t the Jewish approach, and that this is the approach of philosophers who see no value in this world. Judaism sees every moment in this world as valuable and as a way to prepare for the next world, so we definitely do and should cry over a person who can no longer work to prepare and improve himself.

  \[^270]:  See also the commentary of the Medrash Shmuel to the mishnah, which explains the phrase *‘mikol Olam Hazeh’* as implying that the *nachas ruach* of the next world comes from the effort in this one.

  \[^271]:  Kohelet Rabbah 4:5. Note that the *medrash* reverses the order of Rebbe Yaakov’s two statements and puts the one about *teshuvah* and *ma’asim tovim* first. This reversal puts the two ideas in chronological order. First he speaks about the work we need to perform in this world and then the *nachas ruach* we receive from it in the next. The order also strengthens the explanation of the Medrash Shmuel (quoted above).

  \[^272]:  See Talmud Bavli, Maseches Avodah Zarah 3a which tells us that Hashem explains at the time of  *y’mos haMoshiach* that it is too late to earn reward for *mitzvah* performance. The saying attributed to Hashem are that only those “who toiled on Erev Shabbos, have what to eat on Shabbos.”

Even a moment of mere intention can change a person’s nature and identity.

  \[^297]:  We learn the importance of this idea from the contrast Parshas Va’era (9:20–21) makes between those who feared Hashem and those who did not “pay attention” to HIs words. *Yiras Shamayim* requires more than just belief. It requires consciousness and reflection. See Mesilas Yesharim (Perek 2), who compares someone who lives life without reflection to the danger faced by a blind man walking on the riverbank.

  \[^298]:  The first *perek* of *Avos* tracks the *ba’alei ha’mesorah* all the way down to the generation of Hillel and Shamay. From Hillel and Shamay, the *mishnah* digresses to Rabban Gamliel, who was not technically one of the “official” *ba’al mesorah*, but, rather, the grandson of Hillel in order to get to Rebbe (Rebbe Yehudah Hanasi) who lived in the last generation of the *Tanna’im*. He is chosen to open the second *perek* of *Avos* in order to highlight his central role in editing the mishnah. (Masesches Avot continues with the official *ba’alei mesorah* later in the *perek* \[2:8] when it introduces Rabban Yochanan ben Zakay who received the mesorah from Hillel and Shamay).

  \[^299]:  The fact that *Tannaim* from both ends of the *tannaitic* period encourage reflecting upon three things shows the relevance of this model to different historical periods.

  \[^300]:  *Maseches Avos* 2:1.

  \[^302]:  See Rambam (Mishneh Torah, Hilchos Mezuzah 6:13), who seems to use *tzitzis* as a model for the *mitzvos* of *tefilin* and *mezuzah*. All three mitzvot are meant to help us avoid sin by reminding us of Hashem’s presence in our lives.

  \[^303]:  Sefer Tehillim 16:8. See Rema who opens his comments on the Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chayim 1:1, by describing this *pasuk* as “the great principle of the Torah and personal growth.”

  \[^304]:  Rav Mayer Twersky explains that this is why the Rambam (Sefer Hamitzvos, Aseh 73 and Mishneh Torah, Hilchos Teshuva 1:1) emphasizes that *viduy* (confession) for sins needs to be done “*lifnei Hashem*.” Sin is rooted in and reinforces a lack of awareness of Hakadosh Baruch Hu. Ezra HaNavi described (*Sefer Ezra* 9:6) the shame his sin caused him to feel. The source of the shame is the knowledge that Hashem is omnipresent and sees what we are doing.

  \[^305]:  See the commentary of the Maharal, where he explains that Rebbe uses the language of *“li’dei aveirah*” to connote the need to keep far away from sin. Once one is caught in the throes of sin, it is hard to separate.

  \[^306]:  Rabbeinu Yonah emphasizes that the term “seeing eye” implies more than just (abstract) knowledge; it means that Hashem is actually watching. See Sefer HaChinuch (364) who explains that the *mitzvah* to confess sins expresses our recognition that Hashem has already seen what we did.

  \[^307]:  Based on Ara Norenzayan, *Big Gods: How Religion Transformed Cooperation and Conflict*, Princeton University Press, 2013, pp. 13–54. Other studies have shown that fewer people cheat on tests after being exposed to words related to God or being asked to recall the Ten Commandments. This piece is taken from Rabbi Jonathan Sacks’s Covenant \& Conversation, Parshas Shelach 5775, 5782. He discusses this topic at length.

  \[^311]:  The Pirkei Moshe points out that instead of the verb “*kotvin*,” the mishnah uses the passive “*nichtavin.*” Hashem and His angels do not do the writing. The record is created automatically. Alternatively, the writing is done by our hand. The gemara tells us that our own signatures appear in the book about our lives. As it says in *U’nesanah Tokef*: “*chosem yad kol Adam bo —* the signatures of each person are in it.”

  \[^312]:  Akavya Ben Mahalalel also emphasized the importance of maintaining G-d-consciousness. The *mishnah* in Maseches Ediyos (5:6) describes how he responded to an offer of an appointment on the condition that he retract certain personal beliefs and opinions by saying that he would “rather be called a fool his whole life than to be wicked for even one moment before G-d.” Our cognizance of G-d’s presence should inspire us to remain true to our principles.

One should not allow overconfidence to cause him to separate from the community with the assumption that he does not need their support.

  \[^338]:  Brachos 29b. The Medrash Tanchuma (Toldos 7) uses this idea to explain why Hashem avoids associating himself with a tzaddik until the tzaddik passes away. As long as he is living, it’s still possible that the tzaddik will sin. The Medrash Rabbah (Shemos 6:1) uses this idea to explain why some tzaddikim die at an early age. Hashem takes the tzaddik early because He is afraid that the tzaddik might falter and sin.

Tehillim 139 describes Hashem’s ability to see us and follow us wherever we go. The Ibn Ezra highlights this *mizmor* as very important, and unique within Sefer Tehillim.

  \[^358]:  Devarim 30:15, 19. See Medrash HaGadol Devarim 30:15. The Ramban proves this concept from Hashem’s words in Devarim, when He says after the Jews were inspired by the revelation at Har Sinai, “Who can assure that this heart should remain theirs, to fear Me and observe all My commandments all the days?” (Devarim 5:26). Hashem saying that He “hopes” that the *yirah* should continue shows that it is not in His hands.

  \[^364]:  The Gemara (Niddah 16b) describes how people are born with different natures — some are weak, some are strong, some are smart, some are not. Shabbos 156 talks about the astrological influence of when a person is born: for example, someone born under Mars will be drawn towards bloodthirsty pursuits. Chullin 7 says that a person will not even cut his finger without that first being declared in *Shamayim.* Two different gemaros declare that *hakol bi’dei Shamayim* (with two exceptions — *yiras Shamayim*, and *tzinim u’pachim*).

  \[^377]:  Sefer Bereishis 15:5.

  \[^378]:  The following statement by Rav Moshe Feinstein beautifully exemplifies this idea. A man who lived his life far from Torah and *mitzvos* asked his son who learned in *Kollel* after becoming a ba’al teshuva,  to learn gemara with him. Though the son explained that the father should begin with more basic areas of Torah, the father insisted that they learn gemara. After they worked hard and finished one *daf* in the course of a full year the father asked the son to arrange for a *siyum*. The son argued that a *siyum* could only be made after they finished a whole *mesechta*. The father insisted and the son asked Rav Moshe what to do. Rav Moshe answered that they should make a glamorous *siyum* to celebrate the accomplishment and that he would join the party. The morning after the *siyum*, the father passed away. Rav Moshe came to the funeral and eulogized the father. Paraphrasing the famous words of Rebbe (Avodah Zarah 18a), Rav Moshe exclaimed that “‘some acquire their place in the world-to-come with one daf.” It is just not a matter of how much we accomplish.

  \[^379]:  Sefer Tehillim 57:3.

Moshe had to first create the *luchos* which Hashem then filled in.

  \[^386]:  Though the word “Torah” often refers to Torah observance, since the continuation of this *mishnah* mentions examples of observance, the *meforshim* understood that “Torah” in this mishnah refers to Torah learning.

  \[^387]:  Rav Kook saw Torah as our entry into **Hashem’s** world to study His wisdom and *tefillah* as our bringing the issues of **our** world to Hashem.

  \[^388]:  Similarly, Rabbeinu Bachaye sees Torah learning as employing the mouth, *avodah* (prayer) as the work of the heart, and *gemilus chasadim* as the realm of action. The Maharal (Nesivos Olam, Nesiv Ha’avodah) explains that Torah is how we use our *sechel* (intellect), *avodah* is how we use our *regesh* (emotions), and *gemilus chasadim* is our *ma’asim* (actions)*.*

  \[^411]:  See Talmud Bavli, Bava Metzia 32a, which teaches that we combat feelings of dislike towards others through helping them. Michtav Mei'Eliyahu points out that the relationship of strongest love for another — that of a parent towards a child — is fostered through complete (one-sided) giving. This is why the word *“ahava”* centers upon the two-letter-word “*hav* — to give.” Lord Jacobowitz explained that this is why specifically the *Mizbei’ach* cries when there is a divorce (Gittin 90a). Marriage hinges upon sacrifice. When marriage fails because of the lack of sacrifice, the *Mizbei’ach,* which symbolizes sacrifice, cries.

This idea, of course, applies to all mitzvos. Rav Yochanan’s famous words (Talmud Bavli, Berachos 17a) about one who grows in Torah generating *“nachas ruach l’Yotzro”* may be the source for Rashi’s idea.

  \[^451]:  Of course, we are still meant to play our part. *Avos* 2:2 declares that “Torah together with *derech eretz* is beautiful”; Rambam (Hilchos Talmud Torah 3:10) warns that learning without a means of *parnassa* is *sofo beteila v’goreres avon*. The point is that it wouldn't be an overbearing, weighty yoke, but a manageable responsibility. Similarly, Adam and Chavah were meant to work in the Garden of Eden, but only after the curse did *parnassah* become a difficult, sweat-inducing labor.

  \[^568]:  See, for example, King David’s words to his son Shlomo: “*V’chazakta v’hayesa l’ish —* Strengthen yourself and be an *ish*” (Sefer Melachim I 1:2). See also Sefer Yeshayahu 59:16, where Hashem bemoans the lack of an *ish* who can bring judgment to the world. Noach (Sefer Bereishis 6:9), Moshe (Sefer Bamidbar 12:3), Iyov (Sefer Iyov 1:1), and Mordechai (Megillas Esther 2:5) are each called “*ish*.” The *meraglim* in Sefer Bamidbar 13:3 are referred to as “*anashim*.”

Hillel himself taught: *“B’sha’as ha’machnisin, pazer* *—* When others are collecting their Torah and not sharing it, you should be the one to step up and spread it” (Talmud Bavli, Maseches Berachos 63a). In fact, the gemara there quotes the statement of Bar Kapara, who seems to translate our mishnah into Aramaic, showing the connection between the two statements.

  \[^580]:  Hillel mentions specifically a situation where others are not stepping up. The Gra”m Horovitz understands the gemara in Berachos (63a) as teaching that one should not assume an additional position of leadership  when others are available, even if it comes naturally. (See Tosafos to Talmud Bavli, Maseches Sotah 22b s.v *b’shavin* and the Maharsha to Talmud Bavli, Maseches Berachos who give other explanations about what the gemara adds.) This is an important lesson in today’s world, where many speak of their qualifications for leadership and wish to lead. Leadership is necessary when others are not already leading.

  \[^581]:  Sefer Shemos 2:12. See HaKesav V’hakabbalah who explains the pasuk this way (and quotes Bar Kapara’s words from Talmud Bavli, Maseches Berachos 63a). Interestingly, at the end of the second *perek*, Yitro uses the term “*ish*” as a way of describing Moshe as a respectable person.

  \[^585]:  Functioning as an *ish* is not just an ideal. There are times when failure to do so can lead to destruction. The gemara in the Bavli, Maseches Gittin 55b, tells us that Rebbe Zecharia ben Avkolas, the *Kohen Gadol* at the time of the *churban*, failed to make a decision regarding the sacrifice of a *korban* with a *mum* brought to the *Mikdash*. His lack of action led to the destruction of the *Beis Hamikdash*.

  \[^586]:  We find in the discussions of Chazal and the *Rishonim* different ways of talking about our relationship with the *tzibur*. The first is simply that a person should act the way a Jew should act, the opposite of which the gemara calls being *poresh mi’darkei ha’tzibur* (Talmud Bavli, Maseches Rosh Hashanah 16b) — separating oneself from the proper behavior exhibited by the rest of the community. In this vein, we’re told that “*kol ha’poresh mi’darkei tzibur ein mitaskin imo b’chol davar —* A person who “leaves *Klal Yisrael,”* in the sense that he doesn’t act as a Jew, is cut off; when he dies, people are not supposed to sit *b’aveilus* over him (Maseches Evel Rabbasi, Chapter 2, Mishneh Torah, Hilchos Avel 1:10).

The inclusion of a teacher, *rav*, here, is different than in mishnah 16. There, the point of the *rav* is to facilitate the following phrase, *histalek min ha’safek* — avoid doubt by asking for *psak*. Here, the focus is on the relationship itself.

Berachos 5a teaches that if a person is suffering and can’t identify a particular sin as the source, he should assume that he is being punished for not taking full advantage of his time to learn Torah.

  \[^734]:  The Sifrei to Sefer Devarim 186 says that a person begins by transgressing an *aveirah kalah*, but eventually reaches an *aveirah chamura*. For example, a person could start with hatred and progress to murder, exactly like Kayin did with Hevel. The gemara (Talmud Bavli, Maseches Shabbos 105b) explains that this is the strategy of the *yetzer hara*: It starts with something small and eventually works its way up to *avodah zarah*. The medrash (Devarim Rabbah 6/Tanchuma 1) at the beginning of Parshas Ki Seitzei infers from the juxtaposition of various *aveiros* (and then *mitzvos*) to one another that (in both cases) one leads to the next.

  \[^736]:  Based on the fact that there is no *vav* between the two reasons, many *meforshim* (see, for example, Chasid Yavetz and Maharal) believe that this second reason is merely a continuation of the first.

  \[^738]:  Talmud Bavli, Meseches Yoma 38b. Rabbeinu Yonah points out that regarding *tumah*, Hashem merely “opens the door” to more, while, by *taharah*, Hashem actively helps. See further in that gemara where it describes how Hashem strengthens the efforts of one who chooses to avoid sin. See also Talmud Bavli, Maseches Bava Basra 9b, which explains that Hashem rewards those who pursue tzedakah with the resources to continue to be able to fulfill this mitzvah.

  \[^739]:  See also Medrash Shmuel to *Maseches Avos* 4:2, who also explains that we receive *sechar* for running.

  \[^740]:  Other *meforshim* also understand this line as Hashem rewarding additional parts of mitzvah fulfillment, in addition to the actual fulfillment. Rav Ovadia Bartenura, for example, learns from Ben Azay’s words that Hashem rewards us for enjoying mitzvah observance.

  \[^741]:  See Shir HaShirim 7:3 (and Rashi there), where the word *“siyag”* is used to refer to a protective fence.  Most *meforshim* (see the commentaries of Rashi and Rambam to this *mishnah*) understand the mishnah as referring to a fence that protects the Torah.

  \[^742]:  The Rambam also references Vayikra 18:30, which mandates being “*shomer* Hashem’s *mishmeres.”* The gemara (Maseches Yevamos 21a) uses this *pasuk* as a source for the importance of enacting *gezeiros* and *takanos.* See also *Ohr Hachayim* Devarim 11:22.

  \[^746]:  Sefer Vayikra 18:6. See also Rashi (Vayikra 19:2 and Yevamos 20a D”H *Af*), who sees *kedusha* as separating from prohibited relations and other *aveiros*.

  \[^747]:  Along the same lines, *Avos* 1:5 warns men to avoid excessive conversation with women, as the gemara (Nedarim 20a) warns that doing so will inevitably lead to sin. See also Talmud Bavli, Maseches Sanhedrin 75a, which highlights the severity of these other forms of intimacy.

  \[^748]:  The Chazon Ish (*Emunah U’bitachon* 4:9) compares the need to protect our spiritual health to the way we protect our physical health.

  \[^749]:  A similar example of this phenomenon is the Shabbos observance of Jews in Shanghai during World War 2. Because of the question of how halacha views the international dateline, Jews who fled to China were not sure which day to observe as Shabbos. Many were instructed to refrain from Torah-level prohibitions on the questionable day, in addition to the day observed as Shabbos. Without the Rabbinic *siyagim*, people found that they ended up violating Torah prohibitions as well.

  \[^750]:  The Maharit (in his sefer *Tzafnas Pa’anei’ach*) explains that Sefer Koheles’s assertion that there is no tzaddik who never sins (7:20) refers to one who does not add *siyagim*.

We do not have someone like Rav Chaim Ozer. We do not have a Rav Meir Simcha of Dvinsk or a Rav Chaim Soleveitchik of Brisk. By working so hard to learn the material, he “carved out the space” needed to retain it once it was later given to him as a gift. If he hadn’t invested the time in trying to learn it, it would have simply slipped out when it was gifted to him.

  \[^943]:  Judaism deemphasizes physical strength. Lionel Trilling observed that “not all cultures develop the idea of the heroic. I once had occasion to observe in connection with Wordsworth that **in the rabbinical literature there is no touch of the heroic idea**. The rabbis, in speaking of virtue, never mention the virtue of courage, which Aristotle regarded as basic to the heroic character” (Lionel Trilling, Sincerity and Authenticity).

  \[^944]:  See Bereishis 4:7, where Hashem highlights self-control as the way to avoid sin. *Avos D'Rebbe Nosson* (Perek 33) and the midrash (Vayikrah Rabbah 1:1, Midrash Tanchuma Vayikra 1) explain Sefer Tehillim’s (Tehillim 103:20) usage of the term “*giborei koach*” as referring to those who fulfill mitzvos. Esther Rabbah sees the difference between *tzaddikim* and *reshaim* in that *tzaddikim* control their emotions while *reshaim* are controlled by them.

  \[^945]:  See Talmud Bavli, Maseches Shabbos 25b, which expresses a similar sentiment.

  \[^946]:  See Chovas Halevavos (Sha’ar Chamishi, Yichud Hama’aseh 5), who describes controlling oneself as the great war (as opposed to physical wars as smaller ones). See also Ohr Hachayim Devarim 21:11.

  \[^947]:  The word “*chelek*” is meant to imply both of the words' meanings — “portion” and “part.” A person should view what they have as the portion they are meant to have and also realize that, even though we want it all, we will never have more than just part of what we desire.

  \[^948]:  See Rashi (*Avos* 4:1), who explains the word *“chelko”* this way.

  \[^949]:  Understandably, Ben Zoma refers to the other people we are meant to respect as “*beriyos* — creations.” It is the fact that all people are Hashem’s creations that makes them worthy of respect. It is also noteworthy that Ben Zoma derives the importance of showing respect to other people from a *pasuk* that describes showing respect to Hashem. When we show respect to Hashem’s creations, we are showing respect to Him.

  \[^950]:  We are, of course, familiar with the importance of showing respect to one another from the gemara (Talmud Bavli, Maseches Yevamos 62b) which explains that Rebbe Akiva’s 24,000 *talmidim* perished because they violated this principle.

  \[^951]:  This idea applies both to Torah scholarship (Talmud Bavli, Maseches Eiruvin 19a and Maseches Berachos 63b) and general knowledge (Oros Hakodesh 1:13).
